rpHE imports of case Whisky during the year 1897 amounted to 115,357 cases,
1 those of case Brandy to 43,723 cases. These figures exhibit a marked increase
in the consumption of Brandy, and show a strong tendency of the public to revert
to that spirit as their favorite drink. It was thought by many that the consumption
of Whisky was fully eight cases to every one of Brandy, whereas the proportion is
only about 2| to 1. . . . The wholesomeness of

THE RED PAGE.
Recent Verse.
In Issue of Jan. 15 was reprinted that freakish,
Blakish thing appearing anonymously in the
famous “ Wit and Drollery ” miscellany—“ Tom
of Bedlam.” To people with a poetio sense it is
one of the most fascinating fancies in English.
Now comes Francis Thompson in The Dome and
of the fine fragment makes a very fine poem. He
omits the insanest verse—
The moon embraces her shepherd,
And the Queen of Love her warrior }
While the first does horn
The stars of the morn,
And the next the heavenly farrier.
And the other five form the first five lines of the
first stanza, and the whole of the third and fourth
stanzas, in this piece of brilliant workmanship.
TOM O’ BEDLAM'S SONG.
From the hag and hungry goblin
That into rags would rend ye,
All the spirits that stand
By the naked man,
In the book of moons, defend ye.
Beware of the black rider
Through blasted dreams borne nightly j
From Venus Queen
Saved may you bin,
And the dead that die unrightly.
With a wench of wanton beauties
I came unto this ailing :
Her breast was strewn
Like the half o’ the moon
With a cloud of gliding veiling.
In her snow-beds to couch me
I had so white a yearning,
Like a moon-struck man
Her pale breast ’gan
To set my wits a-turning.
I know more than Apollo ;
For oft, when he lies sleeping,
I behold the stars
At mortal wars,
And the rounded welkin weeping.
The moon’s my constant mistress
Aud the lovely owl my marrow ;
The flaming drake
And the night-crow make
Me music, to my sorrow.
With a heart of furious fancies,
Whereof I am commander ;
With a burning spear
And a horse of air,
To the wilderness I wander :
With a knight of ghosts and shadow*
I summoned am to tourney,
Ten leagues beyond
The wide world’s end ;
Methinks it is no journey!
The shadows plot against me,
Aud lie in ambush for me $
The stars conspire,
And a net of tire
Have set for my farings o’er mat
I ride by ways that are not
With a trumpet sounding to me
From goblin lists,
And the maws of mists
Are opened to undo me.
Hate, Terror, Lust, and Frenzy
Look in on mtj with faces ;
And monstrous haunch
And toad-blown paunch
Do show me loathed disgraces.
1 hear on imminent cities
The league-long watches arm&d,
Dead cities lost
Ere the moon grew a ghost,
Phantasmal, viewless, charmed.
With sights I, seeing, see not,
The air is all a-bustle ;
Draughty with wings
And seething things
That without sound do rustle.
It is not light nor darkness
In that place which is placeless:
With honor of doom
Drift by like fume
Faces that arc most faceless.
As a burst and blood-blown insect
Gleaves to the wall it dies on,
The smeared sun
Doth clot upon
A heaven without horizon.
I dare nob bub be dreadless,
Because all things to dread are;
With a trumpet blown
Through the mists alone
From a land where the lists of the dead are.
Francis Thompson.
• • * *
To the writer’s mind, that performance fixes
Francis Thompson’s place as poet more than any-
thing else he has written. (Hitherto he has been
all a-ferment with fancies and phrases : like the
alchemist’s crucible just before the gold-birth,
with red fumes rising, and strange odours, and a
dazzling gleam caught by moments through the
bubble and seethe.) One would not have thought
it possible to breathe so easily in the Bedlam
atmosphere : word and sense, the reinforcement
is perfect : it is indistinguishable from the ori-
ginal. And the original is a crazy splendid ghost
that haunts fit minds unexorcisably.
* * • *
Thompson’s feat needed a poet: Kipling, with
all his craftsmanship, could not accomplish it.
That second stanza required a forcible projection
of the imagination into the unknown-; and Kip-
ling’s forte is fact, and the highly-skilled com-
mentary on fact: it is his lack of imagination
which (to anyone trained in good verses) so ob-
viously limits his sphere. Within his bounds
Kipling’s cleverness is as remarkable as Thomp-
son’s ; but Thompson overpasses the bounds.
Kipling’s last bib of magazine verse, by the way—
“The Destroyers”—is hard and mechanical,
despite neab construction and an occasional
dexterous epithet. This—applied to torpedo-
boats—is photography : a picture well composed
and focussed, bub photography merely, lacking
essential creative art.
The strength of twice three thousand horse
That seek the single goal—
The line that holds the signalled course,
The hate that swings the whole :
The stripped hulls, slinking through the gloom.
Half-guessed and gone again—
The brides of Death that wait the groom—
The Choosers of the Slain !
* » * •
Follows the single good item in Henry Newbolt’B
pamphlet of ballads, “ Admirals All,” whose
appeal to English pride and prejudice has been
rewarded by the sale of a dozen editions. Bub
there are three or four pretty fair items—and
three or four entirely worthless. At his besb,
Newbolt has a swing and an emotion ; and ha
takes credit for being independent of Kipling.
DRAKE’S DRUM.
Drake, he’s in his hammock an’ a thousand mile
away,
(Capten, art tha sleepin’ there below ?)
Slung atween the round shot in Nombre Dios
Bay,
An’ dreamin’ arl the time o’ Plymouth Hoe.
Yarnder lumes the island, yarnder lie the ships,
Wi’ sailor lads a-dancin’ heel-an-toe,
An’ the shore-lights flashin’, an’ the night-tide
dashin’,
He sees et arl so plainly as he saw et long ago.
Drake he was a Devon man, an’ ruled the Devon
seas,
(Oapben, at t tha sleepin’ there below ?),
Bovin’ tho’ his death fell, he went wi’ heart at
ease,
An’ dreamin’ arl the time o’ Plymouth Hoe.
“ Take my drum to England, hang et by the
shore,
Strike et when your powder’8 runnin’ low }
If the Dons sight Devon, I’ll quit the port o’
Heaven,
An’ drum them up the Channel as we drummed
them long ago.”
Drake he’s in his hammock till the great Armadas
come,
(Capten, art tha sleepin’ there below 1)
Slung atween the round shot, listenin’ for the
drum,
An’ dreamin’ arl the time o’ Plymouth Hoe.
Call him on the deep sea, call him up the Sound,
Call him when ye sail to meet the foe ;
Where the old trade’s flyin’ an’ the old flag flyin’,
They shall find him ware an’ wakin’, as they
found him long ago !
Henry Newbolt.
• * * *
Since Newbolt’s success he has contributed
freely to the magazines, and he is soon to publish
an enlarged book. Both he and Le Gallienne
wrote war verses for the American magazines :
Newbolt’s were not good, but they were easily
better. Le Gallienne is said to have been ap-
pointed Professor of English Literature in the
advertising University being established by the
proprietor of Cosmopolitan magazine. The death
of Eric Mack ay adds to the trouble of the
Corellian household. The redoubtable Marie
(Eric’s wife, according to a Bulletin contributor)
has been libelled as an author who cannot be
taken seriously, and has been taken seriously ill.
Eric Mackay was 47 ; and poetically his father
was worth two of him. His talent lay in cover-
ing the commonplace with flowery verbiage, in
the manner of those useful ladies who turn
old boots into ornaments with gold paint and
ribbons. But one feels safe in saying that not an
Original idea or vivid emotion can be found in all
his tangle of spun words. Erie Mackay’s^com-
mon-place admirers praise especially his “ Bee-
thoven at the Piano,” which especially justifies
this opinion, and which contains the interesting
lines—
Ah angel by direct descent, a German by alliance,
Thou didst intone the wonder-chords which made
despair a science.
Yea, thou didst strike so grand a note that, in its
large vibration,
It seemed the roaring of the sea in Nature’s
jubilation.
That is typical Eric Mackay. Charles Mackay
was commonplace, too, but at best there was a
heart-beat to him, he was vital. In his son the
Nile is lost in wastes of sandy phrases.
* * * *
Local writers are rather torpid, waiting the
surge of Spring. Daley, whose book has been
cordially welcomed, is meditating a promising
poem or two. has been ill with a recurrence
of lung trouble, and has been medically ordered
to the dry interior. Brady, whose fine sea-ballads
The Bulletin is preparing for book-publication,
has also been ill with overwork, but is now, his
friends gladly hear, on the road to recovery.
Arthur Adams is busy on a comic opera to eclipse
“Hinemoa”; and Ogilvie, whose book, “Fair
Girls and Gray Horses,” is now well advanced, has
contributed several pleasant items lately. Pater-
son is still reported as engaged on his novel; ana
Lawson, who contemplates a visit to England
next year, wishes first to publish in Australia a
second book of verses. Kenna has written little
verse lately ; but is developing good capacity as a
psychological-story-writer. Those are all one
thinks of except Brunton Stephens, our
“father and master” as Premier Byrnes
calls him, who has the energy of three
of the younger men put together, and is busy on
important work which will see light in good time.
Just at present the time is postponed by official
promotion (B.S. is Q. Chief Secretary’s. chief
clerk), which brings much official labor in its
train, Anu Chris. Brennan, who has an air of
holding up in Australia the banner of the French
symbolists, is making new verse-steps “ Towards
the Source ” —and sends this small piece, un-
titled —
The N.S. W. School Libraries, instituted by Mr.
J. Kevin, now number between 90 and 100 in the Lithgow
district, and as many in Duntrog district. Mr. Kevin
raised a voluntary £6OO at Lithgovv, and gave the children
nearly 7000 volumes—not goody-goodies, but literature of
a strong masculine stamp—biography, voyage and travel,
discovery, moral stories, novels, poetry, and elementary
science. The Dungog libraries, opened little more than
two years ago, have a total of between 8000 and 9000 hooks.
Scores of similar libraries have been, and are being, estab-
lished in other districts. And Government has not assisted
the movement in any way: it is due entirely to the en-
thusiasm of individuals.
M. Dubout’s play was unfavourably criticised
in the Revue des Dem Mondes, and M. Dubout was
refused space to reply. So he brought legal action, and
the French Appeal Court decides that any person
attacked in print has the right of reply—or cash com-
pensation. At latest the Rivue had appealed against the
appeal.
The proper place of Robert Hichens’ “The
Londoners” is on the stage as a farcical comedy: the
broad humor grows tiresome in a novel. Quote: “In his
hands the commonest actions assumed the dignity of
feats. In his mouth the most ordinary remarks took on
an aspeot of Mount Sinai.”
In “The Disaster’’ Paul and Victor Mar-
gueritte imitate Zola’s “La ; their subject is
the downfall of Bazaine’s army of the Rhine. But even
Zola’s synthetic power is often beaten by his facts ; and
their facts 'always beat the Marguerittes. The quasi-
romantic relation of the causes of Metz is so dull that one
has not strength to reach the capitulation. The book, in
fact, is another example of a publisher’s pleasant little
way of guessing a public want, procuring a book to suit,
and booming it per ardua advts. ad alta sales.
Hermes : The frequency of Bulletin references
is establishing a popular impression that Sydney Public
Library consists wholly of Burton’s “Arabian Nights.”
I trust that the following authentic notes will intensify
that excellent impression. To wit: When a lady calls
for Burton’s “Nights” the gentle attendant promptly
brings her Lane’s highly expurgated translation —three
nicely printed volumes with hundreds of beautiful wood-
cuts of gorgeous architecture and wondrous landscape,
The difference is that between lemonade and vitriol. If
the lady, rejecting the nice print and delicate wood-cuts,
sternly insists upon Burton, the attendant mentally cal-
culates if she is 21. If he thinks not, he point-blank re-
fuses to help her to sin. If she is, he goes sadly away,
washes his hands, takes in the wicked volume softly, and
darts away to hide his blushes. It was long ago, before
Burton’s edition became popular, thin a sweet young
thing wandered in one hot afternoon and languidly asked
how many volumes there were. “ Sixteen! ” replied the
dismayed attendant. “Oh, well! fetch me the first
three,” said the languid lady, drawing off her gloves and
exposing her wedding ring. Tie attendant’s knees
smote together, and his side muscles shook. “But —er
—it’s —ah —immoral 1” he gasped. “Oh !Is it very im-
moral?’’ “Very!” he said. “Well, then, fetcli me the
whole sixteen,” replied the lady eagerly.
She listens by the sources
where olden quiet lies
unto the silent forces
where the bound depths are wise
They tell her of their knowing
—and knows she what they tell?
The lithe ways of her going
dumbly declare it well
We deem her child whose bliss 13
to weave a winsome wile
we give her smiles and kisses
—O strange, that she should smile!

BEAD THE SYMPTOMS,
and, if you have any of them, you require the remarkable treatment
which lias made both fame and fortune for Drs. FREEMAN &

In the Laboratory.
Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back ?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms:—
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy ?
Do you have ringing in the ears ?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility-
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken ?
Do you have hot flushes ?
Is your memory impaired?
Have you no manly vigor?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches ?
Is there nausea after eating?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has'the brightness left your eyes?
Do you feel unfit to marry?

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Moths.
Msths around the candle,
Grey and dappled grey ;
You silly things with fragile wings,
Away, away, away!
Into the gloom they fluttered ;
Out of the gloom they came ;
And one fell dead on a page that said,
“And he gave his life for Fame.”
Moths from the old red roses
They wheeled about above ;
And a poet sighed for a maid that died :
“All for the sake of Love ! ”
Moths from the walnut wardrobe
Where silks lie fold on fold ;
The way of to-day is the old, old way,
And “ We die and are damned for gold.”
Moths from the crystal clusters
That swoon on the green grape-vine,
And, “ Sirs, fill up the flowing cup,”
And “ There is no god like Wine.”
Moths around the candle
Ye tire me with your rout;
Enough, enough, one little puff,
And at least your candle’s out !
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Modern Story.
Once on a time, as Love was sweeping
Across a bustling city street,
He heard below a sound of weeping
Among the tramp of thousand feet.
He saw a heart so sore and mangled
That he forgot his prudent care ;
And, ere he knew, he was entangled
In countless strands of golden hair.
And merrily the maiden carried
Her captive to a hidden spot;
But mankind went its way, and married
And smiled and kissed, and troubled not.
Long years went by, until the maiden
Had grown into a woman sweet,
With drooping lips, and sorrow-laden,
Though half the world was at her feet!
For all that Love can claim was offered
In lavish bounty at her door.
But all that Love endures she suffered,
And she could bear her lot no more.
Sick with the aftertaste of pleasure,
Of broken vow and spoken lie,
With burning eyes she took her treasure
And sold him to a passer-by.
They kept him in a prison tainted
By flaunting shame and sullied trust,
Where, prison-fed and coarsely painted,
They leased him out to vice and last.
Amid the offal and the shoddy,
Beneath the glare of revels grim,
Upon their foulest victim’s body
They crucified, they murdered him !
But still—and this is just the story —
The world caressed and laughed and wed ;
Men never missed their crown of glory ;
Men never knew that Love was dead.
Q.
VON KOTZE.
Tempted.
Cheek of damask, lip of crimson,
Breath of flowers, throat of snow,
Eyes, deep lakes the starlight swims in
To the softer dusk below —
Temptress fair ! ’tis more than laughter
Such sweet lips should tremble after ;
’Tis no thought demure and wise
Lights those torches in your eyes.
Temptress ! did you learn that blush in
Rigid Virtue’s solemn rules?
Face on fire against the cushion—
Is it taught in saintly schools ?
Temptress, temptress tantalising,
Flushed and fluttered and enticing,
On your own heart’s wish have ruth ;
I and Cupid know the truth !
Hands were made for hands to hold them.
Cheeks were softened cheeks to press,
Lips were left for lips to mould them
To a pouting tenderness ;
You were fashioned for fruition,
Not a prudent moral mission;
Sigh denial as you will,
Love flaunts rosy signals still.
Temptress, dear ! I mock your chiding:
In your veins and mine at once
Cytherea’s cars are riding
With their winged postillions.
Temptress ! —by your pulse’s quiver;
Temptress ! —by your lashes’ quiver;
Temptress, temptress ! Don’t protest:
I and Eros know you best !
Vic.
THE PAGAN.

PREMIER REID’S UNDELIVERED SPEECH .-ILLUSTRATED BY
“ HOP.”
The Bulletin
Registered at the General Post Office, Sydney , for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper.
Vol. 19.—N0. 962.
SATURDAY, JULY 23, 1898.
Prick, 6d.
To the Electors of King Division.
The following is the unpublished and un-
delivered address of Premier George H.
Reid to the electors of King division,
Sydney. The Bulletin has been waiting
to hear Reid deliver it, but at last its
patience has given out, so it puts on an
eye-glass, and takes a large chew of toffee
these accessories are required to “ make-up”
the character, in the language of the stage—
and delivers the address itself:
To The Electors op King Division. —
Gentlemen : I appear before you for themanyth
time. In 1887, when this electorate was called
"I appear before you.”
East Sydney and had four members, you kindly
put me second on the poll (below Burdekin)
with 4108 votes—2262 votes ahead of the de-
feated candidate. By 1891 my popularity had
so far increased that I just got in as the last of
the four members, being badly licked by
M'Millan, Barton and Varney Parkes. I
got 2946 votes, and was just 686 ahead of the
defeated candidate. In 1894, when the King
was a single electorate, I got 819 votes, and
had a majority of 469. In 1895, I had 610
votes, and scraped in by a majority of only
132. I represent 610 out of 2174 electors.
This isn’t why I believe in majority-rule as
applied to Federation.
I have been a great success at dropping
things. In fact, I claim to be the champion
dropper of Australasia. Among other things
which I have first promised and then dropped
are the State Bank, State note issue, the
The champion dropper.
resumption of big estates
for closer settlement,
State Land Bank, Local
Government, Reform of
the Legislative Council,
Freetrade (I am going
now to secure a few votes
by protecting the New
South Wales sugar in-
dustry, after swearing to
exterminate it), absen-
tee tax, graduated land
tax, the fiscal referen-
dum, and the raising of
loans locally. Which is
a good deal of drop-
ping to do in four years.
My only two conspicuously democratic
measures were the Navigation Bill and the
Shops and Factories Bill. The nominee Council
destroyed about all the good that was in both of
them. It also threw out my Stamp and Pro-
bate duties. Although you elected me in 1895
on my assurance that I was going to wipe the
Wiping the floor.
floor with the Council, that floor remains un-
wiped to this day. I have now announced (1)
that I am going to fight this election also on
the question of Council Reform, and (2) that I
have a great esteem for the unreformed Coun-
cil, and am going to carefully preserve the
nominee principle.
I promised at the late lamented Jubilee that
I wouldn’t go to England
or take a title. Naturally
I did both.
* • »
I am the author of the
famous £333,000 surplus of
1896. The published Govern-
ment Statistics show a defi-
cit of £448,000 that year,
but my surplus was quite
legal, for I passed a law
by which nearly £BOO,OOO
of the year’s expenditure
was not the year’s expendi-
ture at all. If I had passed
My Jubilee title.
a bigger law I could have made the surplus
.£3,000,000 instead of .£333,000.
The accumulated deficit is just about where
I found it. Also, I have just finished the
biggest year’s borrowing
since ’9l, and the big-
gest year’s loan expendi-
ture since ’93.
I was a bitter provin-
cialist till 1894, when it
became necessary to dish
Parkes. At that date
I turned Federationist,
and have remained so
ever since. Any one who
wants to see a real
Federationist is request-
ed to walk round me
twice and consider the
spectacle.
I invited the other
My financial state-
ment.
provinces to Federate
with New South Wales. Every invitation to
Federate has, so far, come from New South
Wales. That is why Jack Want and I always
throw mud at the other provinces and insinuate
Mud.
that it is they who are intensely anxious to
federate with us from the lowest and most
mercenary motives. This sort of thing keeps
the Federal spirit alive. Jack Want didn’t
call the other provinces “wolves.” I heard
him do it.
I agreed to the insertion of the 50,000 mini-
mum in the Enabling Bill, just to give Pro-
vincialism a good start. When it was proposed
to increase the minimum I suggested 80,000,
and said I would accept it, and voted partly
for it and partly against it. If I had wanted
to keep faith with the other provinces, and had
made it a Government question, and had told
my party to vote against the 80,000 minimum,
of course it would have been defeated ; but I
didn’t. lam an unusual kind of Premier, and
you never know what I am going to do.
When the Federal Bill was before the Council
my esteemed colleague, Jack Want, declared
that he was a provincialist, and the “arch-
destroying angel of Federation.” Also he de-
scribed Federation as “ a hydra-headed monster
basking in its constitu-
tionalbeastliness in this
bright and sunny land.”
I have taken Mr. Want
back into the Ministry
as a guarantee of our
joint sincerity in seek-
ing Federation. We
make a really beautiful
pair —we do indeed.
Hooray !
* * *
At the Federal Con-
vention I voted for the
three-fifths majority on
a joint sitting of the
two Houses (it was my
valued colleague, Car-
ruthers, who proposed
it), for equal represen-
tation of the States in
the Senate, and for leav-
ing the division of the
He had kept Jack Want's
wig in a soft place.
surplus to the Federal .
Parliament. I didn’t even call for a division
on the bounties question, or on the right of the
Senate to suggest amendments in money bills.
I voted against federating the debts and rail-
ways, and I didn’t once ask for a guarantee of
the territorial integrity of the States.
Now, I am against equal State representation
in the Senate, against the three-fifths majority,
the bounties arrangement, the power of the
Senate to suggest amendments in money bills,
and the leaving of the division of the surplus
to the Federal Parliament. Also, I demand a
guarantee of the territorial integrity of the
States, and I leant to federate the debts and
railways. In fact, I am against every im-
portant point that I supported, and I am howl-
ing for everything that I previously denounced.
Since writing the above I have begun to wobble
again, and I don’t really know whether I want
to federate the debts and the railways or not.
As I have beautifully expressed it, “ the last
word has not yet been spoken” with regard to
the financial arrangements. Therefore my
Federal amendment proposals are as clear as
mud. That is why I jeer at Barton for not
making his Federal programme more definite.
* • * *
When I came back from the Convention I
at once started to denounce the Bill because of
the things I had done my level best to put in
it, and because of its lack of the things I had
helped to keep out. I told the people to vote
against it. I voted for it my-
self. lam built that way.
I am a quaint and remark-
able person all round.
There were 72.000 votes for
the Bill and 66,000 against
it, but by reason of the 80,000
minimum, which I cordially
accepted and voted sometimes
for and sometimes against,
the minority wins. That is
why I am now hollering for
majority rule. When this
discrepancy is pointed out I
simply say that 7*,000 isn’t
a majority of the 295,000
electors. The 610 voters who
Quaint and re-
markable.
return me for King Division aren’t a majority
of the 2174 electors either, but that is quite
a different thing.
My friend and colleague, Jack Want, an-
nounced, before the Federal vote was taken,
that, if the Bill was carried, he would still do
his uttermost to block it in Parliament here
and also'in England. In fact, he was going to
damn the majority. He also
hollers for majority-rule now.
• * •
I am particularly strong
now in demanding that the
Referendum shall be inserted
in the Bill. When Wise pro-
posed the Referendum in the
Convention, I voted against
it. Wise is a beastly Aus-
tralian native.
When the Bill was lost,
The Attorney-
General’s gy-
rations.
on account of my 80,000
minimum, I, on behalf of the minority, and
in the name of majority-rule, asked Turner,
Braddon and Kingston to hold a Conference at
once, before the New South Wales general
election came off, to consider amendments.
Braddon refused, which conduct I have since
denounced as discourteous. I called Braddon
a lunatic, or something like that, at the Gon-
vention. That wasn’t discourteous. Besides,
he is a refined, frail, grey-headed old gentle-
man in bad health. Kingston also refused. I
didn’t call him any bad names in the Conven-
tion. He is a large, healthy, middle-aged per-
son who loves fighting, and he has a very short
temper. T i j
Turner agreed to the Conference I asked
for, but I took all manner of care that he
didn’t get it. He also asked what amendments
I wanted, but I refused to supply them.
Turner is a beastly lawyer. lam also a
lawyer.
By this arrangement I showed my sweet
reasonableness in asking for a Conference, and
the man who agreed to confer and the people
who didn’t agree to confer were equally left. I
have since denounced Turner for his lack of
sincerity. The fact that I promised to furnish
my list of proposed amendments was sufficient
reason for not doing it. And the fact that I
demanded the Conference before the general
election fully explains why the election is here
now and the Conference isn’t.
* * • *
When Barton was Attorney-General and
tried to put a Federation Bill through Parlia-
ment Want and I both opposed him. That is
why I now sling mud at Barton and accuse
him of making no attempt whatever to help on
Federation.
When Barton decided to contest Bourke he
showed himself a coward for running away
from Sydney. I challenged any of the Federal
leaders to meet me in King Division, and
Barton, much to my surprise and disgust,
accepted the challenge. That also shows that
I never expected YOU!
he is a coward—for running
away from Bourke. I don’t
want to fight big Barton.
I only wanted to fight little
Tom Slattery.
Barton wants to reform
the New South Wales
Council by making it an
elective paid body with no
property qualification and
based on manhood suffrage
—just like the proposed
Federal Senate. Thatproves
him to be a wretched Con-
servative. I propose to
keep it an unpaid House
of Nominees. That shows
that I am the only genuine
Liberal now on the market.
The other reasons why I am a Liberal are
that I voted against the Referendum at the
Convention, and against the rule of the
majority in the joint Federal House, and in
favor of giving Tasmania, with 170,000 people,
the same voice in the Senate as New South
Wales with 1,300,000. Also, I dropped the
State Bank and State note-issue and other
things for the same reason. Another proof of
my Liberalism is that I hold a 66,000 anti-
Federal minority should boss a 72,000 Federal
majority.
* * * *
With regard to purely New South Wales
politics, I promise Local Government and a few
other things, just as I did before, and mv pro-
mise is just as valuable as it was then. lam a
strong believer in the Referendum, which is the
reason why I broke my promise to have one on
the fiscal question. And lam the same reliable
Freetrader as of old, in consequence of which
I am going to protect the sugar duty and I
levy £1,300,000 per head in Customs duties —a
larger sum in proportion to population than is
levied in such Protectionist countries as France
or Germany, and nearly twice as much per
head as is levied in the United States.
I am the head of a just and GoD-fearing
Government. My Government is a whale for
justice. That is why we refuse Suffield a
hearing, despite the strongest doubts as to the
fairness of his conviction.
Lastly, I have assured you that if you re-elect
me you will find me the Same Old George. In
other words, the same old Shuffler ; the same
old No-Yes ; same old Janus and Facing-Both-
Ways; same old two-headed curiosity on a
Same old George!
fence. And, under these conditions, I present
you with my amended Federal programme
(which I haven’t submitted to the other pro-
vinces, lest they should reply that they aren’t
having any, and then I couldn’t very well go
to the country on it) ; also my choice assort-
ment of broken promises. My other six or
eight amended Federal programmes will be
out shortly. If you re-elect me I don’t
know exactly what I will do, for I never
have known ; I have merely wobbled on the
spur of the moment. But at least you will find
me a fearless administrator, just as I was when
I let the Council reject my Probate Duties Bill,
and destroy all the good points in my Navigation
Bill and Shops and Factories Bill, after which
I didn’t demolish the Council, but grovelled
to it, and promised to preserve its nominee
character intact. Therefore I look back on my
steadily-diminishing majority of past years, and
wobble before you with confidence. And my
confidence is just the same kind of thing as my
old promise to abolish the unemployed in 24
hours, and my fiscal Referendum, and the other
things,
Here endeth (for want of space—not want
of matter) the unissued manifesto which the
same old George Reid— always different—
didn’t present to the electors of King
Division.

SATURDAY, JULY 23, 1898.
To the Electors of New South Wales.
The coining election in New South Wales is
not a mere local affair ; it is a matter which
concerns Australia in general. The whole
question of the making or unmaking of an
Australian nation may hang on the issue.
Last month, South Australia, Tasmania, and
Victoria decided in favor of Federation.
New South Wales also gave it a majority,
and 22,000 votes over and above the mini-
mum which was agreed upon when the Con-
vention was elected, and when the other pro-
vinces went into the movement on New
South Wales’s invitation. But, owing to a
curious law passed by certain M’s.L.A., who
themselves represent only a small minority
of the electors, the said majority is of no
account. There were 72,000 voters for the
on d I<3SS t ' lan 67,000 against it, and
too,ooo who didn’t vote, and, consequently,
d.dn t count. At least, they never count
at i aihamentary elections, for even Premier
Beid himself only represents 610 voters out
ot over 2100, and they never count at Par-
liamentary divisions, so it is fair enough to
assume that they dori’t count this time
ei ler. (If they do count, the only fair way
is to assume that the people who didn’t
vo e weie divided in the same pro-
portions as those who did.) Therefore, the
province is tied in a hard knot, and the
object of the present election is to untie it
somehow. -J.'he 72,000 voters who were for
the Bill can't have their way Because they.'
were not the required 80,000. The 06,000
odd aninßillious have a great deal less right
to have their way; if a majority isn’t
allowed to win it is utterly ridiculous that a
minority should do so. The unutterable
foolishness of having any minimum at all
protrudes conspicuously here ; if it was con-
sidered necessary to make unusually dead
sure of the feeling of the province on the
matter the one sensible way of doing it was
to make voting compulsory. And the un-
utterable foolishness of the anti-Billious
crowd who created that minimum glares
forth like the bleached skeleton of some-
body's reputation spread out in the dry
wilderness where the dead reputations lie.
* * * *
There is no fiscal issue in the question
this time; that weary object is away re-
cuperating. If Reid is Premier in next
Parliament he promises to stick to Free-
trade and protect the sugar industry with
heavy duties. If Barton is Premier he
pledges himself to let the present tariff re-
main untouched, but to do his best to create
a Federal Legislature which will take over
the whole fiscal controversy. Both men go to
the country on the Federal ticket. It is solely
a question between Reid, who represented
the minority on June 3, and who came a bad
second at the Convention election, and
who is responsible for the Act which
nullified majority rule; and Barton,
who came first at the Convention elec-
tion and who represented the majority at
the Referendum. Both men acknowledge
that the Bill which was thrown out by a
minority on that date wasn’t perfect—noth-
ing is perfect in this world. Both admit that
it would be the better of repairs and improve-
ments—everything in this world would be
the better of repairs and improvements. The
difference is that Reid represents the
minority’s idea of amendment, while Barton
represents the ideas of the majority. The
other difference between them is that the
Reid party says it will get certain altera-
tions—which is a crude and stupid lie de-
signed for no purpose except to catch votes,
for five or four or three, or even two, inde-
pendent provinces can’t federate except on
terms agreeable to all of them, and theREiD-
Want crowd won’t exact a fraction more in
the way of concessions than the other pro-
vinces choose to give. The Barton party, on
the other hand, says it will get any improve-
ments it can (and that is all that any party
can do), and then submit the question
afresh to the New South Wales electors and
let them decide whether the bargain is good
enough. There is not such a wild shriek of
red and green gorgeousness about that state-
ment, but it is true. All the yell about the
concessions which the Reid-Want party is
going to “demand” in the name of the
Federal minority is gas and bunkum ; for
all the power New South Wales has of en-
forcing its “ demands ” against even the fly-
speck of Tasmania it nrght as well heave
them at the Russian empire, and draw up
Majah Neild in a hollow square of one man
by way of enforcing them.
• * * *
The theory that New South Wales is to
call on the other provinces with a club and
exact concessions is based on a wild idea
that the other provinces are, in some way,
to make a large profit out of New South
Wales. And the theory won’t bear inspec-
tion. Victoria wants free access to New
South Wales markets, and while Freetrade
lasts in New South Wales it has that already.
It wants new railways built without regard
for provincial boundaries, so that the trade
of all the regions for which Melbourne is the
natural outlet shall go to Melbourne ; but
neither the present Bill nor Reid’s proposed
amendments grant that. It doesn't parti-
cularly want to combine its debt with that
of New South Wales, for it has much the
smaller debt of the two, and Reid proposes
that they should combine. It wants to have
an equal share of the Murray, but that
isn’t in the Bill. South Australia also
has the free run of the New ■ South
Wales markets already. It also wants
a F ederal policy of railway construc-
tion so that the trade of those districts for
which Adelaide is the most convenient port
may go there, but no such thing is offered to
it. It wants some guarantee that New South
Wales won't destroy its one river by using
all the upper waters for irrigation purposes,
and that Premier Reid proposes to expli-
citly refuse. South Australia, Victoria, and
Tasmania are all to pay the same taxes as
New South Wales, and to give New South
Wales free access to their markets, which at
present it hasn't got, and it is also proposed
that, in the gamble for the big prize of the
I ederal, capital they should be left entirely
out. There is plenty of reason why South
Australia, Victoria, and Tasmania should
want to federate with each other, because
each wants the run of the other’s markets,
now guarded by heavy tariffs. But New
South Wales has no such concession to
offer, because it has given it away already.
The extra age and size and wealth of New
South Wales don’t affect the question, be-
cause it proposes to keep its size and its
wealth, w hile its age is of no use to anyone
save the owner. Therefore, the Reid-
party, when it promises
°n its own aggressive' hook to secure
certain alterations in the Bill,* and
to secure Federation along With them,
is only a loud-mouthed fraud cadging
after votes, and whoever votes'for it because
.it promises on its own responsibility to get
an amended Federation is a fool, and might
as well vote for it because ib-ptomised to-get
the United States. Even the loudest-mouthed
roarer in politics can only enter into a part-
nership on such terms as the other parties to
the transaction think worth while.
* * « *
J he people have to choose in this matter
bet ween Reid and Barton. They are almost
equal on fiscal grounds ; the only difference
is that Barton’s promise to sink the fiscal
issue is worth any promise by Wriggler Reid
many times over. Behind Barton are Bruce
Smith, Wise, and R. E. O’Connor —three
names that have always been kept clean and
in good repute. Behind Reid, the Tory of
many broken promises who now whoops for
the retention of the nominee Chamber, are
foolish old Brunker, who told the public
the dismal cram that a 27 per cent, tariff
meant £27 taken out of every £lOO of every-
body’s earnings ; Ghould, the bloodless
lawyer who refuses Sdefield a hearing, but
practically offers to let him sneak out of
prison quietly if he will beg for mercy in-
stead of for justice ; Carruthers, the
author of the three-fifths majority clause
which he now denounces ; Sydney Smith,
who helped to wreck Federation by his wild
fiction that the Victorian bounties were to
be charged to the Commonwealth ; and
Want, who calls himself a Federalist and
says also that he is the “arch-destroying
angel of Federation.” Barton represents
the 72,000 majority; Reid demands his
alterations in the name of the 67,000
minority. But, above all things, the Barton
party calls for respect because it promises
what it can perform—that it will make the
best bargain it can with the other provinces
in the interests of New South Wales, and
then leave it to the people of New South
Wales to decide whether the bargain is good
enough to finally accept. And the Reid
party promises what it can’t perform, for no
one province can federate by itself, or make
or vary the federal compact by itself.
* * * *
For these reasons, and for many more.
New South Wales should support Barton,
and every candidate of the Barton party, at
the approaching election. As regards Reid’s
promise to find work for all the unemployed
when he gets back to power, there may be
no Reid left when that gorgeous P.N. comes
due, and if there is it will be the same Reid
who promised the same thing in ’95, and
with the same result. As regards the in-
numerable roads and bridges promised by
the supporters of the present Government,
the next Government will have as many
roads and bridges to give away as this one,
and it will have the same revenue to pay the
municipal subsidy out of, and the local mem-
ber will howl to it for the culvert just as he
howls to the one now in office. The people
who support any one Government for the
sake of iocal works are mostly a very great
ass, for the local works are always with us,
and, amid all political changes and variations
and comings and goings, the parish pump
assuredly asserts itself. But while that un-
obtrusive implement is invariably present,
the chance of making a new Australian
nation, with a continent and a history and
a future of its own, may come only once,
ihis is the only place in all the earth where
there is a chance to unite a whole continent
as one people—and the present obstacle is
the Reid Government, and the 67,000
minority which rules by virtue of the Reid
Government’s Act, and the provincial ani-
mosities which the Reid Government has
sedulously fanned, and the lies which certain
members of that Government and certain of
its supporters carefully circulated about
things which were never in the Federal Bill
at all, and the Government’s wobblings and
treachery and truculence and miserable in-
capacity. So it is time for New South
Wales to reject it and all its works.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Great Scott!!
Extract from N. S. W. Premier No-Yes Reid’s
speech at Paddington
Only about halt-a-dozen things required vitally alter-
ing. The first of these was the provision for deadlocks,
under whicli 38 representatives could defeat 56. (A
Voice : “ You voted for it.”) He did not, and those who
said so were trying to mislead the audience.—Daily
Telegraph, July 13.
There was, first, the question of the three-fifths
majority at the joint sitting. (“ You voted for it.”)
These gentlemen who s aid that knew perfectly well they
were imposing on that audience.— S.M. Herald, July 13.
Unless George Reid can prove that both these
papers made exactly the same mistake in re-
porting him, this narrows things down to the
alternative that either the official Hansard of
the Convention is a liar, or that Reid is lying
himself. On page 933 of the Convention Han-
sard (21st Sept., 1897) it is officially stated that
the three-fifths majority required to carry any-
thing at a joint sitting of the two Federal
Houses was first demanded by Reid’s own col-
league, Carruthers. According to that cold-
blooded record (page 932), Lyne moved to in-
sert in the Federal Bill
If, after a dissolution of both Houses of the Federal Par-
liament, as above provided, the subject matter of the
contention that led to such dual dissolution is again
passed by the Hou.se of Representatives, and again re-
jected by the Senate, such measure shall be referred to
the electors of the Commonwealth by means of a'National
Referendum, and, if resolved in the affirmative, shall be-
.come law.
Premier Turner (Vic.) and Wise (N.S.'W.)
moved, as an addition to this, that the two
Federal Houses should he dissolved simul-
taneously, instead of-one after the other, and
If, after such dissolution, the proposed law fails to pass,
-with or without amendment, the proposed law may be re-
ferred to the direct determination of the people as here-
inafter provided.
Therefore Turner, Wise, and Lyne stood up
as the advocates of the National Referendum.
And Reid’s valued colleague, Carruthers, got
on to his two valuable feet to demand.that the
Referendum be ignominiously “ chucked,’’ and
the three-fifths majority inserted in its stead.
His amendment (Hansard, page 933, 21st
Sept., ’97) reads :
To leave out “the direct determination of the people
as hereinafter provided ” with a view to add “ the mem-
bers of the.two Houses deliberating and voting together
thereon, and shall be adopted or rejected according: to
the decision of THREE-FIFTHS of the members present
and voting: on the question.
Premier Kingston, of S.A., promptly moved
to fire out the three-fifths majority and return
to the Referendum ; but the three-fifths majority
was supported by 30 votes against 11. Among
its supporters (page 967) were Barton,
Brunner, O’Connor, Walker, Carruthers,
and Premier George Reid, while Wise alone of
the N.S. W. contingent voted against it. Then
Turner, Deakin, and Higgins, all Victorians,
tried to fire out the three-fifths majority with
a view to enacting the democratic principle
that a bare majority should decide, and Reid’s
valued colleague, Carruthers, described them
as “wreckers.” On page 974 of the Conven-
tion Hansard (21st Sept., 1897) appears the
motion “ That the word ‘ three fifths ’ stand
part of the amendment.” It was carried by a
majority of 15, the said majority including
Barton, Brunker, M'Millan, O’Connor,
Walker, Carruthers, and G. H. Reid. Not
a single N.S. W. delegate voted against the
three-fifths majority this time. Then it was
finally moved that Carruthers’ three-fifths
majority idea be inserted in the Bill, and it
was inserted by 29 votes against 12, among the
29 being Barton, Brunker, O’Connor,
Walker, Carruthers, and G. H. Reid.
M'Millan’s was the only adverse vote from
N.S. W. And, unless S.M. Herald and Syd-
ney Telegraph have conspired to shamefully
misrepresent him, Reid solemnly alleges, in
the face of all these official records, that he
didn’t support the three-fifths majority. Bar-
ton supported it also, but then Barton didn’t
shuffle and prevaricate about it afterwards.
The Referendum was thrown out at Sydney on
the motion of Carruthers and with the sup-
port of Reid ; of the eleven supporters of the
Referendum (Convention Hansard, page 967,
21st September, 1897) Victoria supplied six,
S.A. three, W'estralia and N.S.W. one each.
Yet Jack Want, shrieking in the provincial
interest, told the N.S.W. people that the
Referendum was kicked out in Melbourne by
the “ wolves ” of the other provinces, in their
anxiety to take a mean advantage of the Corn-
stalk community ! and that slander has never
been withdrawn or apologised for to this day

Barton’s Record.
The election campaign speeches of Premier
Reid have mostly degenerated into furious
attacks on Barton for not securing Federation
while he was Attorney-General in the DibßS
Government. Yet when the circumstances are
fairly considered the record of the Dißfia
Government, bad as it was, doesn’t compare so
very badly with that of the one which followed
it. Each Government went into power to
establish it new fiscal system, and it did—so far,
they are about level. Each had one notable
Democratic reform on hand—the Dibbs Govern-
ment undertook to establish One-man-one-vote,
and did it j the Reid Government findettook
to reform the Upper House, and didn’t do it, el-
even attempt it. The Dibbs Government,
having its tinm largely taken up by the great
financial crisis" and by 13 votes of censure
moved by a certain office-hungry obstructionist
named Reid, didn’t manage to accomplish
Federation in its 34 months of office ; the Reid
Government, with no financial crisis on hand, and
with no hungry patriot always blocking busi-
ness with votes of, censure, hasn’t accomplished
Federation in 46 months. Barton, the Feder-
alist, coalesced with the Provincialist Dibbs, and
severed the connection when he found that he
could make no progress. Reid, the alleged Fede-
ralist, coalesced with the Provincialist Want,
and hugs him to his bingey to this day. Reid
is the boss man in the Ministry, so his chance
of accomplishing Federation has been vastly
better than that of Barton, who was only,
second man in the Dibbs Government. Despite
these disadvantages, Barton did get a Federa
tion Bill partly through Parliament, but it was
killed by the continued obstruction of the Op-
position-led by Reid. Reid got his Bill
through, partly, at least, because he was helped
instead of obstructed by the Opposition. Under
all these circumstances, Reid’s accusation of
treachery comes with very bad grace—especi-
ally when it is levelled by the boss Iscariot of
the movement at Australia’s most consistent
Federationist.
The “Blot.”
Edmund Barton, leader of the N:S.W.
Federal party, has declared, like most other
N.S.W. politicians, for the abolition of the
three-fifths majority in the Federal Consti-
tution Bill, and the elimination of the"'
“ Braddon Blot.” The Poor Old Blot seems
to have not a friend in the world ; and yet -
there is nothing the matter with it except the
utterly stupid lies that have been told concern-
ing it. It provides that not more than one-
fourth of the customs and excise revenue, if
there is any such revenue, shall be used for the
expenses of the Federal Government. There-
fore there may be a large Federal customs and
excise revenue, and the whole of the Federal
Government expenses be paid out of the fourth
or eighth or sixteenth part of it. Or there
may be a medium-sized Customs tariff, or a
small one, and the Federal Government may
pay part of its expenses out of the fourth of
the customs revenue which it is allowed to retain
if it feels so inclined, and the rest out of direct
taxation. Or there may be no customs tariff at
all, and the Federal Government may" pay all
its expenses out of- direct taxation. -. Or -there
may be a small,-or medium, or large customs
revenue, but. the Federal Government may not
take any of it for its own expenses. . The other
three-fourths, or as much more as the Govern-
ment pleases, of the customs and excise
revenue, if there is any,' are either to
be returned to the States to pay in-
terest on their debts with, or to be used by -
the Federal Government to pay interest on the
State debts ..when the Federal Government
takes them over. If there is no customs and
' excise revenue, or very little, then the States
get no refund, or a very sinall one, and the in-
terest on the public debt has to be paid wholly
or partly out of other sources. Premier Reid
truly stated at the beginning of his anti-Billious
campaign that the Braddon clause didn’t com-


mit the Federal Government to any fiscal
policy, and then he perpetrated the usual
wobble afterwards and said that it did. If a
similar clause, providing that not more than a
fourth of the N.S.W. customs and excise
revenue should be appropriated to cover the
cost of the State departments, and that the
other three-fourths should either be returned to
the taxpayers or should go towards paying
interest on the public debt, had existed
for the last dozen years in the N.S.W. Con-
stitution, it would have made no difference.
N.S.W., despite all its fiscal changes, has
been working on exactly these same lines all
the time without knowing it. Its famous G.
Reid is working on them now. It has about
£1,600,000 customs and excise revenue. It sets
aside three-fourths (£1,200,000) of this uncon-
sciously to pay interest, and makes up the
balance (about .£1,000,000) out of other sources
of revenue. It sets aside the other fourth
(£400,000) without knowing it to cover the
cost of Government, and makes up the balance
out of other sources of revenue without being
aware of the fact. If its tariff yielded
£3,000,000, or only £400,000, or only £1 per
annum, New South Wales would be still work-
ing on the Braddon Blot system, without
noticing the fact. Every other province, except
Westralia, is also working on the same system,
without knowing it, and has been for many
years. When the Federal Government takes
over the State debts, it will go on working on
the Braddon system without knowing it. The
Braddon Blot was a mistake simply because it
left things exactly where they would have been
if there had never been any Blot at all ; it was
doubtless meant to make a difference, but it
didn’t. It wasn’t worth putting in. It wasn’t
worth the slightest notice when it was in. And
it isn’t worth the trouble of taking out. It
makes just as little difference as a postage-stamp
pasted on Mount Kosciusko. It has certainly
proved a handy thing for the provincial crowd
to lie about, but, if it hadn’t been there, the
same crowd would have lied about something
else instead.
+
Government by Bounce.
When Premier Reid, the celebrated N.S.W.
Wobbler, re-opened the Federal question, after
the original Bill was defeated by a minority on
June 3, he asked the other Premiers for a Con-
ference and made three statements. They
were (1) that he wanted to confer with regard
to certain amendments ; (2) that a list of the
amendments would be submitted; (3) that he
wanted to have the Conference before the
N.S.W. general election so that he could take
the amended scheme to the country as part of
the Ministerial policy. Turner, of Victoria,
agreed to confer, but Reid’s promises were
promptly broken, as usual ; there was no Con-
ference, and the list of amendments was never
even submitted. In this matter Attorney-
General Want has risen to explain on behalf of
his chief :
They arranged, when he rejoined the Ministry, that
they would point out to the other provinces what were
the clauses which they considered were injurious to the
interests of N.S. Wales. This resulted in the adoption of
what has heen called a placard. Mr. Reid had been
blamed because he did not tell Sir George Turner
exactly what he wanted altered in the Bill. He (Mr.
Want) was not in the Ministry then, hut he could well
imagine that Mr. Reid was not such a fool as to state
what he wanted, because Sir George Turner would have
said: “There is one thing we cannot agree to.” . . .
The Government promised to have those things which
were injurious to N.S W., and N.S.W. only, altered ; they
would have it in black and white, and the other colonies,
seeing that we would insist on these amendments, would
give in. But Mr. Barton would negotiate—(laughter)—
negotiate—(renewed laughter) - negotiate (roars of
laughter). He was telling the other colonies, “I know
we can’t get them, hut you and I will have a chat, and
then I will come back to N.S.W. and tell them I tried to
get them but failed.”
This is a fine exposition of the bounceable atti-
tude of the great india-rubber Ministry. It
offered to negotiate with the other provinces.
It refused to negotiate with the principal of
them when its invitation was accepted. It
damns Barton because be is going to negotiate ;
yet when N.S. W. asks the other provinces to
come in and federate with it, it is obviously
necessary to negotiate the terms of the Federa-
tion. N.S.W. can’t federate all in a corner by
•itself. Though the Government promised to
state the amendments it wanted, it would,
according to Mr. Want, have been a fool to do
so, because Turner might have objected to one
of them. Possibly he would. In fact, he
probably will object to one or more, if he
doesn’t like them—just as Reid and Want
are objecting now. Apparently King Jack
thinks that Turner, or any other Pre-
mier, is not to be allowed to object to,any
of them ; but if the Reid Ministry is not going
to negotiate, and is not going to allow Turner
or anybody else to object to any of its terms,
and yet is going to federate, the only thing it
can do is to conquer the other provinces by force
of arms. It must either negotiate a union and
give the other provinces a say in fixing the terms,
or it must unite by conquest, or not unite at
all; and even the most insane variety of bully
Government is fully aware of this. Further,
King Cabbage-Tree alleges that the Reid Minis-
try would be a fool to let Turner know what
alterations it wants; and yet how it is going to
get alterations without letting the other parties
know what they are, the Lord only knows. And,
finally, the N.S.W. Government, according to
Mr. Want, promises to get certain alterations
made. It promises to get them without asking
the other partners whether it can get them or
not, and without knowing whether it can get
them or not, but with a view to obtaining votes
in the meantime it simply promises. Which
all makes up a fine blood-and-thunder attitude,
but this frantic stage-cursing means nothing
after all. For the Reid Government can’t
promise anything without the assent of the
other provinces—if it does promise, that fact
merely shows what an utterly unscrupulous
combination it is. And, if it comes back to
power, it will have to diminish its swelled head
and negotiate like anybody else. And it can’t
guarantee that the other provinces will give in ;
all it can say is that if N.S.W. doesn’t like the
terms which Reid or Barton gets, it needn’t
federate at all. If there is anything in its terms
that theother provinces cannot agree to, it will be
in exactly the same situation as any other Minis-
try which didn’t swell up and roar in this awful
fashion. Also, it will have to be exactly the
kind of fool that King Jack says it isn’t, and
explain what alterations it would like, just the
same as any other fool.

“The Bulletin’s” Mixed Correspondent.
A letter, from “ Mixed ” :
Dear Bulletin. —I ain a bit mixed as to how I shall
vote at the forthcoming election, and appeal to you to
put me on the straight and narrow track. I voted
against the Convention Bill because, in imagination, I
saw 38 Fatmen firing out a measure (say an Old Age
Pensions Bill) approved of by 56 Thinmen. And because
I prefer the Referendum to the 38 Fatmen, I am branded
a Provincialist, a gutter-snipe, and several other horrible
things. But calling me these hard names has not con-
vinced me that my vote was wrong. But, to make doubly'
sure next time, I submit my dillicnlty to you, and ask for
your guidance. The Bulletin has hall-marked B. R.
Wise with the stamp of its approval by recommending
him to the electors as a fit, and proper person to vote for.
Well, Wise at the Convention stigmatised the Referendum
as an appeal from the enlightened few to the ignorant
many, and what adds force to his remark is the fact that
the ignorant many sent him there, therefore he was
peculiarly qualified to correctly gauge and express an
opinion on the impenetrable density and utter awfulness
of popular ignorance. I am an ignorant-many person
myself, which possibly accounts for my belief in the
Referendum, but my belief is mostly founded on the
teachings of The Bulletin, and strengthened and con-
firmed by the published utterances of two Britishers—
who probably knew half as much as Wise named
Gladstone and Balfour, both of whom publicly ex-
pressed their approval of the principle. Well, this is my
difficulty. Suppose the intellectual and pure merino
Wise contests this constituency, and is opposed by John
Smith who advocates the Referendum, and won’t be happy
till he gets it, will The Bulletin kindly advise me which
candidate to vote for. Shall it be the enlightened B. R.
Wise and the 38 Fatmen, or plain John Smith and the
Referendum ?
The Bulletin is pleased to offer “Mixed”
some advice. (1) He began by assuming that
because the existing nominee or property-vote
Upper Houses are Tory, therefore everything
that is called an Upper House (even the pro-
posed Federal Senate with no property or
plural vote, no property qualification for mem-
bers, and with paid Senators elected on the
Democratic franchise of the existing provincial
Assemblies) must be Tory also. Therefore, he
was an Ass and had an asinine dream about 38
Fatmen and 56 Thinmen, when there was
hardly the ghost of a chance for any person of
Fat principles to get in at all. (2) “ Mixed ”
voted against the Federal Constitution, because
it hadn’t the Referendum in it, and the only
remedy for deadlocks was the joint sitting.
Well, the result is, that he remains under a
Constitution which contains neither. And
the Federal Legislature, with both Houses
elected by manhood suffrage, would have
been much more likely to grant the Referen-
dum than the provincial Tory Councils
are. (3) Wise didn’t advocate the principle
under which the 38 could outvote the 56. He
voted against it. Reid and Carruthers voted
for it. Also it was Carruthers’ inven-
tion. (4) Would The Bulletin vote for
John Smith rather than Wise, if John
Smith supported the Referendum ? Depends
on the kind of Smith. If a very weak John
Smith supported all The Bulletin’s ideas, but
was such an essentially dull and commonplace
individual that his support was not likely to
get them a bit further forward, and a very
strong and vigorous and eloquent Jones or
Wise supported half of them, and would prob-
ably be able to carry them into effect, then it
would support the latter. It is quite possible
for 100 well-meaning John Smiths to do no
good all put together. It is necessary to inspect
the imaginary John Smith before passing an
opinion. Also, to know if he is the kind of
Smith whose word is to be relied upon. For
instance, Parkes supported Local Government
for 25 years, and left it no further advanced
after all.
Financial Confusion.
A week ago, before he began to totter on his
basis again, Premier Reid suggested, in a tenta-
tive sort of way, that his idea of a proper settle-
ment of the Federal financial problem was that
the Central Government should take over the
State debts and railways in a mass. Sydney
Telegraph, which cordially supported Reid
and tried to make the best case it could for
him, decided that the result would be as
follows :
Debts Assets Debt not
transferred transferred represented
by States. by States. by assets.
N.S.W. ... £58,588,000 £43,321,000 £15,267,000
Victoria ... 47,029,000 41,245,000 5,784,000
Queensland 33,498,000 23,128,000 10,370,000
S.A. ... 24,414,000 15,221,000 9,193,000
Tasmania ... 7,882,000 4,180,000 3,702,000
£171,411,000 £127,095,000 £44,316,000
This means that at present N.S.W. has debt to
the amount of nearly £l2 per head with no
transferable assets to show for it; that Queens-
land, S.A. and Tasmania have from £2O to
.£25 per head; while Victoria has only £5 per
head of debt which isn’t represented by assets.
If the debts and railways were all lumped
together, however, the combined provinces
would have about £l2 per head of debt for
which there were no assets; in other words,
N.S.W. would remain just about where it is
now ; Queensland, Tasmania, and S.A. would
have their debt burden greatly reduced ; while
Victoria, instead of only owing £5 per head
over and above its assets, would find its debt
increased to over £l2 per head. The Telegraph
so far acknowledged the difficulty of the situa-
tion as to publish a suggestion that the Federal
Government should guarantee Victoria a sub-
sidy of £250,000 a year on the ground that it
handed over to the Commonwealth the smallest
proportion of liabilities and the largest propor-
tion of assets. But Premier Reid, so far as
he has gone, doesn’t propose any subsidy or any
compensation. Also he proposes to continue the
present system of provincial railway construc-
tion, so as to still force the Riverina trade to Syd-
ney, and prevent Victoria getting any indirect
compensation that way. And on top of all he
demands that N.S.W. shall have the Federal
capital, and, apparently, the control of the
Murray, and that Victoria shall help to guaran-
tee, under the proposed new territorial clause,
that the Riverina shall never be allowed to
secede from N.S.W. however much it wants to.
The proposed amendments are very good for
Queensland, S.A. and Tasmania, which would
get a huge reduction of their debt at Victoria’s
expense, in consideration of agreeing that
N.S.W. should have the capital and would be
guaranteed against any risk of secession as well;
bat Victoria will almost certainly jump on
them with all its feet. There are two possible
ways out of the difficulty. By far the better
of the two is for the Federal Government
to take over the debts and railways with
sole power to construct new lines, and all
differential rates to be abolished. The other is
to let the railway problem rest awhile and for
the Federal Government to secure the solvency
of the States meanwhile by taking over £4O per
head of the debt of each State. It couldn’t
equitably take over more than that, for the least
indebted State only owes £4O per head. But
the proposal of the N.S.W. Premier—if it is
definite enough to be called one—only makes
the financial confusion several times worse than
it w’as before.

The Pall in Wages.
From time to time we hear—and we almost grow sick unto
death of hearing it—that the policy of Freetrade as intro-
duced by Mr. Reid spells ruin and disaster to the manu-
facturing industries of the colony, but what does an in-
vestigation, a throwing aside of the veil, reveal ? The
factories of Anthony Hordern and Sons give the lie direct
to this assertion, and there is no firm in Australia, per-
haps, that goes in for manufacturing to such an extent,
and in so many different ways, as does this firm, which is
ever growing as it lives. . . . And the rub lies
in the fact that since the liberal policy of Mr. Reid
took shape wages have had a general tendency to in-
crease rather than diminish. (Probably, however, the
prodigiously prolific brain of the Protectionist will invent
a ready explanation in this regard.)—Sydney Telegraph,
July 6.
The Bulletin, speaking on behalf of the pro-
lific brain of the Protectionist, has the greatest
pleasure in explaining the general tendency of
wages to increase in N.S.W. under the Reid
policy. Its explanation is that they don’t in-
crease, but that instead they diminish regu-
larly and shamefully and ruinously. Its evi-
dence on this point is not the loose statements
said to have been made by any gentleman in
business (no gentleman in business could very
well give himself a bad advt. by saying that he
had reduced wages), but the annual official
statistics published by the N.S.W. Govern-
ment Statistician. The following, according to
that official authority, are the minimum wages
paid in ’95 and in ’97 to the furniture-making
class—that being the big feature of the great
Hordern business:—
Minimum Minimum
wage, 1895 wage, 1897
Foremen £3 5 0 ... £2 10 0
Machinists 212 0 ... 17 6
Cabinet-makers 115 0 ... 112 0
Polishers 2 8 0 ... 2 0 0
Chair-makers 212 0 ... 19 0
Wood-turners 210 0 ... 116 0
Upholsterers 212 0 ... 2 0 0
Upholstresses 10 0 ... 012 6
Seamstresses 1 0 0 ... 0 9 0
Mount-cutters 116 0 ... 115 0
Girls 010 0 ... 0 5 0
Venetian-blind makers ... 210 0 ... 110 0
Do. painters ... 116 0 ... 15 0
Billiard-table makers ... 214 0 ... 2 0 0
Ivory-turners 3 0 0 ... 2 0 0
Packers 2 5 0 ... 012 0
Boys 0 8 0 ... 0 5 0
Hair and Flock Hands ... 115 0 ... 017 0
Against these there are two lines in the furni-
ture trade where, according to the official
State statistics, the minimum wage was higher in
1897, when the Reid policy had got a firm hold
on N.S.W., than in 1895 ; and four where the
minimum wage remained unchanged. And
this is just one trade, chosen nob because it
supports the Protectionist case better than
any other, but because it is specially instanced
by the insolent shoddy organ as a proof of
how the Reid policy has made wages go up.
In the clothing-trade cutters’ minimum wages
are quoted as having fallen from £2 10s. a week
in ’95 to £\ ss. in ’97 ; foremen’s from £3 to
£2 ; pressers, £2 to £1 ss. ; and so on.
There are 22 columns of such wage-statis-
tics in the N.S.W. Statistical Register,
and anyone who chooses to compare the figures
for 1895—the last year of the Protectionist tariff
—with those for 1897, can wade right through
them and find such results everywhere. That
is just where the prodigiously prolific brain of
the Protectionist comes in. These results may
or may not be produced by Freetrade, but that
isn’t the question. The matter at issue is that
the Freetrader denies that there are any such
results, and says that the absence of them is a
proof of the advantages of his policy. And the
Freetrader who says that is a broken idol, and
a Gehazi, and a weather-prophet, and a Tozer,
and an exploded paper-bag generally.
Gone Smash..
A first attempt at establishing the Referendum
in Victoria as a means of deciding differences
between the People’s House and the House of
48 Rich Landlords, came to grief as soon as it
gob started. The Government had a Bill on
hand for the purpose, but private member
Outtrim got his measure to the same effect in
first, so the Turner Ministry gave it the Minis-
terial vote and support. The second reading
was carried with a rush by 47 votes against 21.
But according to an ancient, decomposed pre-
cedent, which was dug up some time ago by the
48 Rich Landlords for the express purpose of
squashing Democratic legislation, any Bill
which amends the Constitution must he carried
by an absolute majority of both Houses, and in
the lamentable absence of six pledged sup-
porters of the measure—Trenwith, Vale,
Maloney, Salmon, Duggan and Baronet
O’Loghlen—there was one affirmative vote
short. So the Referendum Bill went down,
despite its big majority, through the neglect of
six men from whom their country expected
better things. The Government measure is
still to come on, but the House of 48 Rich
Landlords has dug up another old precedent,
even more decayed than the first one, to the
effect that a proposal for the reform of the Con-
stitution, once lost, can’t he revived again in
the same session. Old Nicholas Fitzgerald,
M.L.C., therefore informs the Government, on
behalf of the4B Rich Landlords, that if Outtrim’s
Referendum Bill had reached their Chamber it
would have been cast out, and if the Govern-
ment’s Referendum Bill gets there this session
it won’t be even considered, but simply ruled
out of order. The fine, old bloated Landlords
were in good form on this occasion. They
announced, per medium of Nicholas Fitz-
gerald, that they were quite willing to bow to
public opinion, if it is placed before them in a
form which “impresses” them, but, as the
Landlords have thrown out almost every Demo-
cratic measure which came before them, no
matter how large the popular majority which
backed it, they are evidently waiting for the
only kind of impression which will impress
them—that delivered by the people at the end
of a gun, or when they poke a long, red pole,
through the window with the head of Cor-
nelius Job Ham on the endof.it. Also, the
Council desired to intimate that it also repre-
sented the people—which it doesn’t. The people
elect the Vic. Councillors certainly, but when
they are only allowed to elect wealthy-landlords
(no other class being eligible) they have just as
free a choice as Nicholas Fitzgerald would
have if he had the option of voting for the
Referendum or the abolition of the Council,
and for nothing else. One thing seems tolerably
certain ; the bulk of the 48 Rich Landlords,
though they boast of having popular opinion
behind them, are too cowardly, and have too
little faith in the truth of their own statements,
to come out from behind their entrenchments
and submit their pretensions to a popular vote.",
Possibly the Federal Parliament may find
means to shift them some day. Possibly the
people may some day shift them with a red
banner, and an insurrection, and the offer of a ,
rope with a noose at the end of it. Or possibly ;
the Government, when it has more money to')
spare, will pass a Bill through the Assembly i
providing that future Councillors shall be paid,":
that the property qualification shall be wholly
abolished, and that any of the existing Coun-
cillors who are fired out by the people when ;
they get a free choice of M’sL.C. for the first'*
time, shall receive a pension of £lOOO a year A
each from the country in grateful acknowledg- j
ment of their past services. The 48 Landlords ;
would doubtless reject the Bill wdth scorn on ;
its first or second appearance, but about the ■
third time it came up they would grab at the -
country’s grateful reward for their services with •
both hands. The deliverance from its 48 Rich -
Landlords would only cost Victoria £48,000 a :
vear, and it would be dirt cheap at the price.

“ Barrierite ” to The Bulletin : *
It is doubtful whether any previous N.S.W. administra-
tor has made such audacious bids for the support of coun- 1
try constituencies as G. H. Reid. Here is a san" pie. A "
day or two before Parliament was dissolved he announced, "
after having conveniently allowed the matter to drift for ..
some time, that he couldn’t undertake to purchase the ‘
Tanawingee railway-line on his own responsibility ; but
he promised if returned with a majority to recommend
Parliament to provide for the purchase within;a month. 7
The excuse for this obvious bid was that the line would",
cost more than ±20,000 ; but, as a matter of fact, the r
prescribed maximum of expenditure without Parliamen-
tary authority (and Reid’s colleague, Carrutiiers, could 1
tell him how strictly the provision has hitherto been ob-;
served) was only reached by tacking to the £15,000!
(which was the purchase-money asked by the eo.)
the sums which it is estimated the repairs and addi-
tions to the line will cost during the next five
years. It was a pretty bold manoeuvre ; and at first sight
it is calculated to make at least four constituencies think
themselves interested in getting Reid back. But there
are, unfortunately for Mr. Reid,* one or two other con-
siderations. Thus the same four constituencies will
probably ask themselves who it is that might have, bought-
the line from this private company, and didn’t, do it ; and
they will probably also want to know who it was that, has
jeopardised the purchase of the line altogether, because
the Government’s right to purchase will have expired'
long before the new r House meets. And when they have
answered that the man who might have served them and
the country-but didn’t—is Reid, they may also come to
the conclusion that if through Reid's inaction the line
isn’t lost altogether, Barton is as likely to close with a
good bargain when he sees it as Reid is. Anyhow, it. isn’t
much of a recommendation to take a man to do certain '
wmrk that, when he might have done it before, he delibe-
rately declined to do it.
N.S.W. Wriggler Reid’s promise to tlie un-
employed when lie was hunting for votes just
before the 1895 election :
The Government have determined upon a vigorous
public works policy. The old system of relief works has
been discarded for legitimate undertakings that will ab- -
sorb all available labor, and further conduce to the pro- ?"
gress of the colony. These works are not to be confined
to Sydney, or in a special extent to any part of the "
colony. The whole country will participate in them.
The system of allowing the unemployed of the other
colonies to participate will not be allowed ; our own
workmen only will be employed on them. The works :
are to be of so varied a nature that skilled as well -
as unskilled men will find little difficulty; in se- ,
curing employment, and at properly remunerative :
wages. These works will include railways, harbor works,
bridges, roads, sewerage works, water supply, artesian
boring, wharves, public buildings, thinning out and other-
wise improving forest reserves, clearing land to promote
settlement, &e., so that employment will.be plentiful
both in town and country for some time to come. In this
w>ay work will be afforded in the interior, so that eventu-
ally the men will be able to draft themselves off into ;
private undertakings, and will in many cases also, no
doubt, be able, under the provisions of the land law just t
passed, to take up holdings and settle, thus further con- ,
ducing towards their own prosperity as well as to the
well-being of the colony. ,
N.S.W. Wriggler Reid’s promise to the un-
employed now that he is hunting for votes just ■
before the 1898 election : ;
I am happy to tell you that I don’t come before public ■
meetings and express my sympathy with the unemployed
members of the community, and then when I get into ■
office make no attempt to do anything for them. ._. . I
And I am happy to be able to tell you now that I am in a
position to put Work into the hands of the men of Sydney ,
which will more than exhaust all the able-bodied men
ready to take such work. Of course, I admit you can
never really do that, because the more men you take out
of the city the more you find coming in to take their
places. But that is the report which I have received >
from the officials in connection with this establishment.
The politician who promises just before the
election that if he is returned to power he will
find work for everybody just after the election,
js always with us.
Chamberlain’s attempt to buttress the consti-
tutional character of the Papuan land-grab.
agreement by pointing out that it was approved
by an ordinance of the Legislature of New
Guinea, directs attention to the constitution of
that Legislature. Joseph’s talk is so bold and
free, and the word “ Legislature ” carries, with
it such an air of popular right and Parliaitoen-" 4
tary judgment, that the unwary would be likely
to take such an ordinance as the equivalent of a
House of Commons Act. The Legislature of
New Guinea is, in the Parliamentary sense, a
Don Quixote or Sancho Panza ’sham. In con-
sists of five persons, four of whom are civil-ser-
vants find the remaining one manager for the
trading firm of Burns, Philp and Co.' One is
Secretary, one Treasurer, one a Judge, and one
a magistrate ; all practically in the employ of
Gov. MacGregor, subject to his directions and
bound to follow his ideas on Papuan develop-
ment. To apply the term Legislature ”* to
such a body, without attaching an'explanation,
is merely to throw dust in the public eyes.
The ordinance of such a body Should carry no
more constitutional weight than an agreement
signed by the Governor alone. .-o a
