The “Little Minister" who toils in the
bleak vineyard of Ihrums is prospering reasonably
well at Her Majesty’s, but he isn’t by any means
the overwhelming success that he deserves to be
The “ Royal Divorce," which was hardly anything
more than a great outbreak of clothes and big pic-
ture, and had practically no literary merit, boomed
with a decidedly bigger boom. Yet for art and
literary finish and human interest and flesh-and-
blood, the present abbreviated preacher is miles
ahead of that big, explosive glare. One is soul ;
the other was only paint-though it was certainly
very beautiful paint. But, perhaps, the trouble is
that, to the bulk of the inhabitants of this new
dry, hurried, sun-blasted land, Thrums is not
understandable. The four ancient Scotch elders
mourning on the fence because they think then-
hired expounder is running after a girl belong to a
country that still takes its religion seriously. The
£rey stone town with its houses 300 years old, and
its grey moss, and its red roofs, and its cobble-
stones, and its sleep, is a mystery to the country
where the unpainted, sun-cracked, wooden cot-
tage gets up like Jonah’s gourd on its four
posts, and it is only necessary to hide an
old kerosene-tin and a lank dog underneath to
make it home. Then the mortgage strikes it, and
the overdraft breathes upon it, and it crumples up
again like Jonah’s gourd, and is gone. The
picture of the slow old men who live in the slow
old town and have their chief recreation in think-
ing of the hereafter, and whose worldly joys
centre mostly round the kirk, is a very clever
picture. Also, the ridiculous back-view of the
elder is very true to life ; there are a few places in
the cold little earth-speck fhat Premier Reid
comes from where the hinder part of the elder
looks almost as ridiculous as that to this day.
But it is difficult for the Australian to realise the
fact.
* * * *
The co. which Harry Rickards has got
together at Her Majesty’s handles the Scotch
drama carefully. All its effect depends on that.
It is a delicately painted Old Master ; if an in-
ferior co. tackled it in an off-hand fashion it would
be only a loud raw oleograph. The deep, thick
Presbyterian piety of Thrums is laid on carefully
and the sleepy moral atmosphere, and the faint
haze of whusky on top of all. The Scotch
accent, however, has been mercifully toned
sufficiently to make things intelligible,
the Minister, and the two doddering small elders
and the big, hairy elder, and Miss Eloise Juno’s
old woman, and sweet Pattie Brown as the vision
of loveliness that leads the Minister away from
the straight paths of the Auld Lichts, are hardly
to be improved upon. But it begins to be ques-
tionable if Sweeney is quite certain whether he is
Insli or Scotch, and he compromises by being
very drunk in a language that is mostly plain
English. Miss Athena Claudius, as the small
boy Micah, does her little part well, and reveals a
pair of shapely bare brown feet, also a good deal
of the long human supports that are above the
feet, I hey are also brown and shapely, and Miss
Claudius does that end of the small'Scotoh boy
adnuralily—even better than she does the other
end. Ihe only despairful item is the red, unreal
soldier of Walner Gregory, who is stiff, and
wooden, and otherwise harrowing.
** * *
There is not one new drama in Sydney this
week. “White Heather,” at the Royal, seems
bent on an indefinite stay, and so many good
reasons can be found for its continuance that it
will probably last into August. Miss Gill, as
Codiva, is one of these reasons. There is no ab-
solute necessity for the intrusion of that ancient
and shocking person into a modern drama, but
she is introduced as part of certain private theatri-
cals, and she takes off her long cloak, and reveals
neiself dressed in nothing but fleshings and an
immense mass of fair hair, so suddenly that the
scandalised Lady Janet Maclintock hasn’t a chance
to burst through the door and escape in time.
Having once seen the shapely vision, the Scotch
Jadv Becomes too weak from shock to get
away, so she remains and sees some more.
e, i ifc ’ s ully explained to her that
tne other lady has undressed in the sacred
cause of charity, she becomes reconciled.
One point about “White Heather” which
calls for deafening applause is the absence of the
regulation fallen girl. Miss Watson is certainly
supposed to have fallen, but it proves that the
record of her marriage has only gone down at sea,
and towards the end it is honorably fished up.
Also Miss Frances Ross calls on bad man Baker
and offers to fall if necessary ; if he can get rid of
bis ■ first wife and marry her she admits that she
will prefer it, but if he can’t marry her she is
willing to fall. The providential drowning of
saker however, prevents this sacrifice of a very
beautiful and nicely-dressed woman ; so “ White
Heather gets through without a single blemish
on its character.
** * *
John Lemmone’s new concert co. opened
at Sydney Town Hall on Saturday to a fair house.
Evidently the eo. s Melbourne popularity had ar-
rived before it, for, with the exception of Miss
Verne s last performance, every item on the pro-
gramme was encored, and one of the encore guns
had a double barrel. Madame Alva is a
singer of the dramatic Italian school, with
a fine capacity for tragic emotions, a large,
strong voice, very thin in parts, but passion-
ate and executively skilful enough to almost
cover up the thinness. Miss Adela Verne,
the pianiste, is a gifted performer, with a little of
Ketten’s electricity, and, at times, an uncommon
breadth of expression. This varies, however;
witness her superficial interpretation of Chopin’s
Polonaise in A fiat. The tenor, Samuel Masters,
contributes an ordinary tenor voice with
rather less than ordinary style, and an extravagant
affection for the sostenuto device. Careful study
of this young man revealed what’s in a name. In
Samuel s case, the name tellsagood deal ofthe truth.
. took the wildly enthusing house at one fell
trip, away from “Stars of the Summer Night,’’
out Into the Garden, Maud,” then right off
to the wild remoteness of the “ Bay of Biscay, oh.”
But you could see the Academy all the trip.
Possibly the most masterly member of the co. is
John Lemmone himself, whose only fault is that
he plays the flute. It is only fair, though, to say
that at Mr. Lemmone’s lips the flute ceases to be
a flute, as understanded at the suburban terrace,
?i £ eco,n ® s a mos t desirable instrument, such as
the Seventh Heaven might condescend to play
when an eternity of harp-strings has worn its
fingers to the bone. To sum up, the company is
an evenly-balanced family, all good, none aggres-
sively startling, and none calculated to keep this
paper up too late o’ nights.
** # *
Sydney Lyceum is supposed to close for
two nights, while preparing for an extensive
Saturday night production of a new play, “Is
Life Worth Living ? ” The Lyceum is having no
luck worth speaking of just now ; two dramas and
a variety-show are about all that Sydney seems
able to pay for, and when there are three dramas
the third one is generally a stranded production
mourning on a rock. The last Lyceum drama
also suffered through the absence of Miss Ben-
susan, that pretty and promising actress having
removed her bright presence through illness.
1 herefore Diver, when he slouched on the stage
as an ominous and dirty old man with a Past, had
to shake a lady who, despite her various attrac-
tions, didn t compensate for the one who should
have been “shook.” “Is Life Worth Living?”
sounds very like the heart-wail of the manage-
ment inquiring of the universe in general whether
it is worth while keeping the theatre open if the
leading lady doesn’t come back promptly.
** * *
A Cuban war drama, built on patriotic
Bullanjonathan lines, is to wave the star-spangled
banner this week at the Royal Standard. There
is very little room on the stage for the two armies
to manoeuvre, but they intend to do their best.
Un Saturday night America will wipe up the
sacred soil of Cuba with Don Ramon and Don
Jose and Don Jaime and Don Juan, arrangements
having been made that all the thoroughly bad
people will be Spaniards, while all the Americans
will be persons of the very best character. General
Blanco begins to be struck by just retribution in
act lb), and in act 111. the full consequences of a
horrid life of sin plug him full in the weskit, and
he is doubled up. Then there is more star-span-
gled banner, and all ends gloriously.
** * *
The Poluski Brothers throw themselves
into the deadly breach at Sydney Tivoli this week
with a bustling military entertainment which has
taken the public fancy violently. There are two
five brothers in this act, and a quantity of dead
artificial brothers make up the rest of the army’
and the wild evolutions of that force are calcu-
lated to throw a completely new light on the art
and mystery of warfare. Sundry pairs of sisters
of good musical capacity have also added them-
selves to the bill, and the names of Winton, Wood
Marion Ainsworth, and Peggy Pryde continue to
lend grace and promise to that document.
** * *
The greasy emphasis with which the Melb.
dailies— after the event—announced that W. and
M. had nothing to do with “ The American Girl ”
fiasco, was somewhat sickening. Each paper
seemed anxious to “ state that Messrs. William-
son and Musgrove are not responsible for the pro-
duction, ’&c., &c. Paragraphs before the event had
not uttered any warning to that effect. Inad-
vertently or otherwise, the advts. in the Saturday
morning papers gave the impression that “The
American Girl ” was to some extent a W. and M.
show, and a lot of people undoubtedly paid their
money (ordinary Princess’s prices) on the strength
of the Firm s name at the top of the advt., and
the names of the Firm’s scene-painters, G. and J.
Gordon, at the tail end. Manager Williamson sat
in solitary state in the managerial box and
beamed upon the show—for a little while.
Ihen he withdrew, and on Monday morning ap-
peared two specia! advts. The first stated that
W. and M. had leased (large type) their theatre to
lemple and Moulton, and the second stated that
,7 • and , "b would resume active management on
the 23rd July. Under the circs, there was no oc-
casion for the dailies to apologise so profusely on
tne Firm s behalf. If the production of the
Australian native ” musical comedy had been
even a small success, would the Firm have rushed
in with such emphatic disclaimers of any connec-
tion with it ? Temple and Moulton pay a
week for the use of the Princess’s, it seems.
** # *
Last week The Bulletin wondered what
would happen to the tail-end of “The American
Girl ” season at Melb. Princess’s. The tail-end
was mercifully amputated, and on Tuesday last
the Firm were to resume business on their own
with “ The Prisoner of Zenda.” The story of the
crowned tourist runs for four nights, and on
Saturday the Early Christians will return with
their holy clothes-props, and the new Nero will
gnash his pagan teeth at some more “Sign of the
Cross,” which is a giddier entertainment than the
late unlamented musical-comedy, anyhow. More-
over, it is a large and glowing spectacle, calculated
to keep the souls of the righteous warm these
foggy nights.
** * *
Melbourne’s rooted suspicions re any show
that smacks of Yankee female took further
root on Saturday, owing to “Miss Chicago” at
the Bijou. “ Miss Chicago ” started with every
appearance of rivalling “ The American Girl ” in
utter foolishness, and nearly succeeded in the at-
tempt. If the two hopeless productions were
matched to run against one another, “Miss
Chicago” would probably win by half a week. It
is said (on the programme) to be hilarious, but
the writer didn’t notice any genuine merriment in
front of the house, except when a stout lady in a
brief blue skirt was called up to dance a hornpipe
duet with a thin lady, and neither lady could
dance worth a cent. For the rest, a comedian
who frequently cried “ Ditto ! ” was a better joke
than the damsel, who continually cried “Rats ! ”
in A mer i ca p Girl.” Also, the comedy at
the Bijou had a thin streak of plot visible amidst
its padded irrelevancy, to the effect that a nice-
looking young clerk married the niece of his em-
ployer by strategy, and secured all her real and
personal estate. This triumph of love brought
much soulful joy to an Angelina in the dress-
circle, who had been leaning heavily against the
shoulder of her best boy all the time, but the
Public at large didn’t find it thrilling. “Miss
Chicago ” is a vain and empty matter for such as
have nothing special to think about in the next
seat; therefore, if Miss Elsie Lander and co.
desire to fill the house for a long period they must
set up a new piece, and act it on an improved
system.
#m * *
“The Idler” is bringing the Cartwright
season to a close at iVLell). Royal. On IMonday
next the co. will make its last appearance in the
big house, and all local admirers of good stage
work will be very sorry to see it go. Business
for “ The. Middleman ” last week was so big that
Cyrus Blenkarn might as well have gone on dis-
covering the secret of the old original Fatlow-ware
till the season expired, but Haddon Chambers’
drama is equally acceptable, and the change of
bill should serve as another reminder that Melb.
ought to have given better support to the same
co. when it showed at the Bijou, a few months
back.
** * *
Biondi, stated to get the highest salary
ever paid to one man this side of this planet, is un-
doubtedly the biggest success on record at the
Opera House since Rickards took over the lease.
Nobody can swear that Biondi is an unimpeach-
able impersonator of 12 musical composers—some
of them dead, very dead—but everybody admits
that the rapid Italian gives a dozen flashlight
changes of men, each man’s style harmonising with
a bit of the music which bears the name attached
to him. Biondi is a variety entertainment to be
seen often, and appreciated more and more. His
range of contrasts, notably from the wildly
humorous Suppe to the cold, dandified Arthur
Sullivan, is quite wonderful. As for the question
whether he sports a full mask for some
of the pictures, The Bulletin doesn’t care
how he works his changes—it is enough to take
them as they come, and there isn’t time to analyse
the miracles if one wanted to. Also, it is certain
that the artist introduces Mascagni without any
make-up to speak of, unless he borrows a pair of
rolling, frenzied eyes and sticks them into his
countenance for the occasion. Biondi, who was
the first performer in his line of business, and has
not been equalled by his imitators, is a thoroughly
deserving boom. Other new items at the Opera
House are Foreman and Fannen, and the Terry
Sisters ; and a “Musical Mystery” will make its
debut next week.
** * *
Newbury’s Pop. Concerts resumed their
success at Melb. Town Hall last Saturday, so it
would appear that the melodious Philip has created
a great winter want and can reckon on being able
to supply it at a profit for some weeks to come.
He didn’t sing his best the other night, but his
second best voice was very pleasant, especially in
a duet with Madame Spada—the giddy garden-
scene duet from “Faust,” which they sang in
English. Organist Wiegand, with his'“Storm ’’
and clever Miss Nina Schlotel on the violin
swelled the list of encores for the evening.
At Poverty Point.
There is very bad blood—visible from the auditorium—
between Julius Knight and his understudy, Gaston Mer-
vale.
Bright young George Majeroni, just back from the
Brough campaign in India, was re-engaged by manager
Williamson to play the little part of Fritz in “ Prisoner
of Zenda” at Melb. Princess’s. When “The Sign of the
Cross” takes place again on Saturday brother George
will, presumably, step into the Early Christian sandals
of Mario Majeroni, who travelled on to London with the
Broughs.
Unlucky tenor Rose, who was made the scape-goat of
“ The American Girl ” failure by being dropped out of
the cast after the first night, contrived to get a neat ad.
for his trouble, anyhow. He wrote rational letters to the
Argus and Age, quoting, in one of them, from the stuff
that he had to speak, and asking whether it wasn’t hard
luck for a light tenor to make a first appearance in Mel-
bourne under such awful circs. He had to start by re-
ferring to one of the characters as “ a carrot stuck in a
pot of ice-cream.” Afterwards, moaned the victim, “I
spun the ancient yarn of the evidences of a terrier’dog
being found in a boarding-house sausage ! I had to talk
about the filthy state of a lavatory towel, and speak a
few other subtle and refined inanities, and then it was my
painful dutj T to sing (with a cold in the head) in an in-
congruous costume a romantic song.” Rose may not be
the most brilliant actor in all the world, but he certainly
deserved a better show.
Ugo Biondi’s imitations of prominent composers are
wonderful enough as examples of characterisation, but as
portraits they are absolutely lost to Australian audiences.
A few local men would bring down the house that Biondi
has crowded every night since his arrival-say, for in-
stance, George Reid, Marshall Hall, Bishop Goe, Dr.
Bevan, and C. J. Madden.
“Holdar M.” : Sydney D.T. proudly alludes to the
“ patriotic outburst ” of a Saturday-night music-hall
audience, evoked by a stage comparison of the probable
conduct of a British crew as compared with the foreign
behavior at the Bourgogne wreck. A ventriloquial
dummy’s remark, that “ ‘ there’d have been more than
one woman saved if British seamen had been there,’ was
the signal for almost deafening applause.” This may be
in order as concerns the 8.5., but it recalls a recent
frantic rush at a false fire-alarm in a Melb. music-hall,
during which several women and children got badly
mauled by the escaping males. This in the hope that
the mentioned applause will ease off a little for the re-
mainder of that gag’s innings.
Biondi, the “quick-change” wonder, promises to get
much too fat for some of his business before he is a
middle-aged man. At present he can rush through it
without “ hanging out signs of distress,” as the horse-
reporter would say, but already his female impersona-
tions measure about 40 inches round the waist, and when
Biondi the lover con es mashing Biondi the wife, he has
to remark that she is “ nice gell—but too fat.”
Said that a temporarily-high personage is said
to have sent_ a message to Pattie Browne, ex-
pressing surprise and grief at Pattie’s having “ de-
scended ” to play such a part as Saucers in “A
Bit of Old Ohina.” Which was rather absurd, con-
sidering that the favorite actress ascended a ladder to
play some of her part. And, anyhow, the mawkish trifle
was written by a woman—the authoress of “ Little Lord
Fauntleroy.” Hence its awful improperness, no doubt.
When a woman tries to skate over thin literary ice, she is
pretty sure to slip and fall through.
“ Haresfoot ” : The crash of the “ American Girl ” was,
I think, simply due to the exigeant type of audience
created by the perfect staging of Williamson and Mus-
grove. Next day the alarmed firm put in explanatory
advts. that the theatre was let, and possibly they were
sorry, for nothing like such an affair ever happened at
the Princess’ before. At the Alexandra, or even at the
Bijou, the opera might have gone well enough. Why
did not the entrepreneurs take the Alexandra?
Madame Alva, who was only 18 when she appeared as
prima donna, under the late Sirgustus Harris’s manage-
ment, is married to an influential man, Edward St. John
Brennan. Hubby has edited one or two London weeklies,
is a well-known musical and dramatic critic, and the
author of some bright verses and Home-rulish literature.
He once stood for the Gloucester city seat in the House
of Commons.
Edwin Geach writes from Shanghai re China and the
Carl Hertz show : “ This is a gorgeous country for popu-
lation, stenches, bribery and wholesale corruption. We
have caught the wily Chow by the pigtail, and things
prosper with us. We have twice delayed our exit from
this city.” Hertz appeared at a Chinese (as well as the
local British) theatre, and there he had to give a money
guarantee that no woman should appear on the stage, so
Mrs. Hertz stood out of it, and the “ vanishing lady” was
represented by a vanishing man. Even cinematograph
pictures of women were prohibited. As soon as the cur-
tain had fallen on the second night’s performance, the
Chow proprietors fired a string of hundreds of crackers
secretly planted near the footlights. This by way of
driving the devil off the premises.
Maggie Moore and Roberts have pioneered Kanowna,
the Westralian deep-lead field, and at present the most
prosperous centre in the province.
Kalgoorlie welcomed the Firm’s comic opera co.’s first
visit to the goldfields, under Bracy’s management,
by a gorgeous “citizens’ welcome,” in which, of course,
Wallace Brownlow took a leading hand.
Question to be asked Premier Reid :
Where do you get all the blankets from ?
Colonel Mackay, of the “Real Mackays,”
is having an easy fight for the Burrowa seat in the
N.S.W. Assembly. A man named Kenneth Mac-
kay d’ye ken—who can induce a constituency of
Irishmen like “ Burrowha ” to elect him, and
stick to him, is something out of the common.
But the present generation at Burrowa, though
Irish by blood, is very Australian in sentiment,
The inhabitants of Burrowa are mostly
Michael Ryans, while Denis Burkes and Pat
Maloneys and Andy O’Connells are fairly plenti-
ful. In the old days, when the mailman drove
into Burrowa, the local inhabitants all gathered
round the cart and came up for the letters as he
sang out the names. One day he read out,
“ Letter for Michael Ryan,” There were 10 men
killed in the crush.
The gilded Hughes, M.L.C., announces
that he has quitted the Barton party and cast in
his lot with the Reid crowd of provincialists.
Barring Barton and Dick O’Connor, the Fossils of
the N.S.W. Council are rallying round Reid
almost to a man. A politician is known to a great
extent by the fossils who rally round him. The
gathering of the Rich Old Men to the Reid stan-
dard began when Reid announced that he rever-
enced the nominee House, and had a great regard
for the ancients who threw his Democratic
measures out on their joint and several ear, and
was going to preserve the Fat principle of
Nomineeism as a Holy Thing. And now the Tons
of Fat march unanimously behind the flag of the
self-styled “ Liberal party” of N.S.W,
For a whole week Premier Reid has given
up explaining what are the alterations he advo-
cates in the Federation Bill, and apparently he
will end by going to the country with no Federal
programme at all. His demand that the Federal
Constitution should contain a p. rpctual cast-iron
guarantee against the cutting-uD of any of the
huge, unwieldy States hasn’t been heard of for at
least a fortnight. He proposed to federate the
debts and to partly federate the railways, retain-
ing all the worst features of the present provincial
system; then he weakened and said that the
“last word” had not yet been spoken on the
financial problem ; and then he dropped that
Eainful subject also, and now no one knows what
is Federal financial policy may be. His later
speeches are mere furious bounce about how
he is going to demand concessions in the
name of “ the people ”of New South Wales. Yet
the majority of the voters polled and the majority
of the constituencies solemnly declared on 3rd
June that they were content with the Bill ; and
the lost minority is divided into the six different
minorities which want six different kinds of new
Bill, and the seventh minority which wants no
Bill at all. And Reid, thes licked and disgraced
champion of one of these minorities, demanding
amendments in the name of the people of N.S.W
is a very beautiful bluff indeed. As for the exact
nature of the financial concessions that are to be
demanded in the name of the small minority, that
is a matter which has disappeared into mist.'

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Westralian revenue for the year ended 30th
June, 1898, was £2,754,746, and the expenditure
£3,256,912, making a fine deficit of £502,166. There was,
however, a surplus of £315,362 left from previous years, so
the total shortage is only £186,804. Premier Forrest says
the situation doesn’t cause him any great anxiety, which
seems probable enough ; when a politician has been eight
years in office he will almost certainly have to go out soon
and leave the anxiety to his successor. Westralia’s debt
is now £54 per head (£9,204,507).
* * • *
Queensland spent 148,341 of loan-money in
1897, and 1 937,006 in 1898, so in proportion to population
the Northern province riots a great deal more violently
than any other of the seven, Westralia only excepted.
Only about £60,000 of the money, however, went for
wholly unproductive purposes, against about £700,000 in
N.S.W. The worst-looking item of the lot is £9926 for
immigration. The Government which borrows money for
the purpose of paying people to come to the country is a
pretty low-down affair.
* * • *
The affairs of the Bank of New Zealand have
been steadily improving ever since the State took the helm
out of the shaky and paralytic hand of Private Enterprise.
The year ended 31st March, 1898, shows a net profit of
£74,900. This is after paying interest at 4 per cent, on
£2,000,000 of State-guaranteed capital, and interest at 3J
per cent on £500,000 preference shares held by Govern-
ment, and after writing £10,132 off the Bank’s holding of
the Estates Co.’s debentures. The last four years’ results
are as follows :
March 1894, Profit £41,079 I March 1897, Profit £39,391
„ 1895, Loss 37,356 „ 1898, „ 74,900
„ 1893, Profit 35,325 I
As this is the amount that remains after paying the fixed
dividend on guaranteed stock and preference shares it
equals the splendid return of over 19 per cent, per annum
on the ordinary capital (£393,042). The annual payment
of £50,000 is made to the Assets Realisation Board to help
along the weary process of cleaning up the old losses, left
behind by the ancient private management, and £24,900,
being arrears of the said payment, are also cleared off. In
future the Bank has only the annual payment of £50,000
to face, and some day, when the Estates Oo.’s wreck is
cleaned up, that will cease also —though exactly when it
will cease it is difficult to guess.
The dubious assets of the B.N.Z. are also being
steadily reduced. The following is the list of doubtful
securities as they appeared in the two last balance-
March 1897. March 1898.
Assets Realisation Board De-
bentures £2,680,285 £1,880,285

Therefore the results of a year’s work are : an increase of
£35,000 in the profits; £74,900 paid off the old losses of
the B.N.Z. Estates Co. ; and the dubious securities in the
bank’s balance-sheet reduced by £852,000. The B.N.Z.
has now £2,893,000 paid-up capital ; £23,474 reserve-
fund and the State’s security behind that; and it holds
over £3,600,000 in cash and liquid securities, so it has a
record of progress and increasing stability that any in-
stitution might reasonably be proud of.
• * » *
Another streak of unspeakably bad luck appears
to have fallen on the Bank of North Queensland, which
closes its half-year to 30th June, 1898, with a profit of only
£587. The last four years’ record stands thus :
Dec.,’94 .. Profit £1674 Dec.,’96 .. Profit £1717
June, ’95 .. „ 1736 June, ’97 .. „ 2003
Dec.,’9s .. „ 1182 Dec.,’97 .. „ 1559
June,’96 .. „ 1703 June,’9B .. „ 587
The present half-year’s profits are equal to 11s. 9d. per
cent, per annum on the nominal capital of £200,000, or £1
3s. 6d. per cent, per annum on the capital of £lOO,OOO,
that being the amount to which it is to be written down,
as soon as the necessary arrangements can be made. So
the painful fact sticks out conspicuously that, unless
things improve materially, the B.N.Q. will be just about
as unable to pay dividends after the proposed writing-
down as it is now. The accumulated profit of £10,928 is
also put to a contingency fund, and this, with the pro-
posed £lOO,OOO, will make £170,928 of losses provided for
in the last five years out of the shareholders’ money, which
is a tremendous affair for a little institution which started
with only £250,000 capital.
The trouble with the B.N.Q. seems to be that it
has too small a business. Also it seems very probable
that when the management took £50,000 of deposits fixed
for 10 years at 4J per cent, it undertook an unprofitable
liability, for apparently the money doesn’t earn 4| per
cent, nowadays. The bank has paid no dividends since
’93, and even when it writes down its capital one-half its
profits require to be nearly three times the present half-
vear’s figure before it can pay a very humble 3 per cent,
ihe bank has £85,659 cash and liquid securities, which is
equal to nearly 29 per cent, of all liabilities to the public ;
both its cash and its deposits show a good increase ; and
from a depositor’s point of view the B.N.Q. looks secure
enough. But from a shareholder’s standpoint it looks
next door to awful. If the original founders had
bought Government securities with their money, and
never had any Bank of North Queensland at all, they
would he about 15s. in the £ better off than they now are.
If they .could sell their hank for £lOO,OOO and buy 3 per
cent. State securities with the money it should be a happy
release.
• * « •
The Queensland National Bank’s half-yearly
balance-sheet to 30th June, 1898, is the second since the
institution got fairly started on its reconstructed career.
It shows very little improvement on its predecessor. The
gross earnings (£44,237) are £2200 less than they were,
but considerable economies have been made by cutting
down expenses. Last half-year the profit was set down as
£16,635, and, after £8635 had been set aside towards
writing down old losses, the net profit was called £BOOO.
This half-year the profit is £17,795, and £8795 is set aside
towards old losses ; net profit, £9OOO. It is admitted,
however, that £4994 of this half-year's money is not ordi-
nary business profit, but recoveries from debts previously
written off ; but it is impossible to say whether any acci-
dental receipts of the same kind were included in last
half-year’s returns or not. On the whole, the general
result seems to be that the Q.N. Bank’s profits are no
better, and may he a little worse. The £9OOO which is
called net profit for the half-year equals £4 6s. pe r
cent, per annum on paid-up capital—which is good at
present rates. One-fourth (£2250) goes to reserve making
ft £4250. Another £2250 goes in reduction of that quai tei
(£456,331) of the Government’s reconstructed deposit
which is payable out of profits, and at this rate it will be
liquidated i'n 100 years. The remaining' £4500 goes in re-
duction of that quarter (about £1,000,000) of the 3
reconstructed deposits which are payable out of pronts
and at this rate they will be all paid in 111 y e a,rs. 1 he&
payments don’t reduce the interest burden and thus in-
crease profits, because the deposits in question carry n
interest. _ „
The balance-sheet doesn’t show the true position
of the Q.N.8., inasmuch as one-fourth (over £1,000,000) of
the private deposits was written off and doesn’t appear
among the liabilities. Nevertheless this sum has got all
to be paid (though only out of profits) and as long as it
has got to be paid out of anything it is a liability.
The Q.N.B. merely gets an indefinite extension of
time without interest and its liabilities are thus
£1,000,000 more than its alleged assets. And of its
alleged assets £731,000 is a very dubious something called
“ Debts in suspense pending Idealisation of Securities.
During the last six months this rather hopeless-looking
item has diminished by £12,000, whether by writing it
down out of profits, or by realising some of the securities,
doesn’t appear. Anyhow, this item evidently requnes to
be wiped off in some way before the Q.N.B. is on a
really sound footing, and at the present rate of progress
it will disappear in 30 years. During the halt-year
£46,222 has been written off capital on account of
forfeited shares, though where this money has gone to
doesn’t appear. It hasn’t gone visible reserve,
and it hasn’t been written off the £/31,000 of Debts in
Suspense”; possibly it has gone towards depreciation
somewhere else. The net result is that the Q.N.B. s
profits are moderate, and seem half-inclined to diminish ,
the private deposits (which have precedence over the old
liabilities, and are therefore perfectly secure) have in-
creased by £19,000 ; the bank is stronger than it used to
be in cash and liquid securities ; and it seems to he
paying its way and reducing its old losses. But at the
present rate of progress it looks as if it would take about
90 years to get really square, unless a new boom comes to
breathe on the dry bones of the old securities. And,
barring a new boom, the next dividend looks about that
distance off.

The Trustees, Executors, and Agency Co.
(Melbourne) shows signs of remaining just about the same
in its own small way. The half-year’s profits to 30th
June, 1898, are £1723, and the usual 5 per cent, dividend
absorbs £l5OO, leaving £223 to the good. This makes the

During the above period £70,000 of the co.’s reserve has
been written off (leaving only £40,000) to cover losses,
and on present values there is another £l2 000 loss, or
possibly more, to provide for on account of £32,000 worth
of Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank securities,
which are still included among the assets at their face
value The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. has two
serioiis drains on its funds. It owns a leasehold in Queen-
street Melbourne, which costs about £650 more per
annum in rent than it produces ; the rent paid was fixed
in boom-time and the rent received is whatever the dull
times will produce. Also it has a £25,000 mortgage on its
freehold and £35,000 invested, most of it m the very
dubious securities above mentioned, to meet the mortgage
when it falls due; and theco. pays about *620 a.year more
in interest on the £25,000 than it receives for the £35,000.
When it gets rid of these two melancholy speculations,
and writes off its loss on the Australian Deposit and
Mortgage Bank investment, it ought to prosper again,
unless it rushes into another frantic career of big divi-
dends instead of rebuilding its shattered reserve fund. At
present the £1 shares are worth about Bs. on the market,
which, considering the 5 per cent, dividends, looks as if
public confidence was pretty low.
A deputation of pawnbrokers waited upon Vic.
Attorney-General Isaacs, the other day, to endeavor to
persuade him to “ let up” on the poor pawnbroker, who
is a philanthropist and a godly man. The strong reason
given why the profits of Israel should not be curtailed was
that such action would put it beyond the power of the
three-ball evangelist to do good deeds. When the Usury
Bill is passed, says Oppenheimer, the poor, pale widow
who wants to raise £lO on her worldly possessions that
are only worth £5 will have to be refused by the distracted
Hebrew. At present his profits are big enough to enable
him to bestow largesse on the widow and the orphan in
this way, but Isaacs is warned that the widow will get
nothing but the tears and sympathy of the sorrowful
Ikey if his measure becomes law.

B H North Central the other day lit upon a few

Some weeks ago The Bulletin invited the ex-
pert officers of the Mines Department inN.S.W. to con-
sider whether the system of underground open-cutting
lately adopted in the Broken Hill mines, and especially
the British, is sufficiently safe. The officers, apparently,
didn’t answer ; but they may be a little more inclined to
do it now that another part of the roof has fallen in, kill-
ing a shift-boss, maiming a miner, and getting so near as
to scratch two or three others. As a matter of fact, the
shift-boss on this occasion dodged one fall only to run into
another. + , * *
It doesn’t seem as if there will ever be an end
to the modifying of the machinery used in the dressing of
B.H. sulphide ores. Block 14, whose ore becomes harder
and harder (in which respect in doesn’t differ at all from
that of the other mines) finds it necessary now to intro-
duce new stone-crackers.

* * • *
Cobar Gold-Mines (old Fort Bourke) N.S.W.
By the reconstruction of this co. the old shareholders
have been called on to stand a liability of ss. a share or
drop out altogether. Every share held in Australia has
been taken up under the first call of 25., and a cable says
the London end of the scheme has been completely held
up. A further working capital of over £40,000 is thus
arranged for, and there is no doubt that four-fifths of it
will be called up. If this, under careful management,
does not put the old Fort Bourke mine on a div.-paying
basis it will be about time to give low-grade gold-mining
in Cobar best. The chances are all in favor of success.
Cobar has a lot of hard luck. It got a bad
set-back in the mess made by the Cobar Gold-
Mines * which mess helped bo knock the bottom out oi
the sale of other similar properties. The big mine of the
camp (Great Cobar) is growing apace, but the directors,
in their wisdom, have determined not to publish details
of the returns, hence these have to be guessed at, from
the reported sales of copper and the divs. declared from
royalty. The tributors say it is not their business to
publish either production or profits. Therefore, neither
mining men nor newspapers are able to make compari-
sons between one great copper-mine of which the public
hear everything—viz., Mt. Lyell, and another of which
they hear next to nothing. If it could be made, it would
be found that the Oobar mine, though a good way behind
in production, is very close up to (if, indeed, it does not
exceed) Mt. Lyell in profits. Be it also known to persons
who talk as glibly of opening up N.S.VV. copper-mines as
of opening a banana store, that the Cobar tributors had a
big struggle for a long time, though the ore was there as
big and as rich as to-day ; at times it was almost a toss-up
whether a mine, to-day worth more than a million, was
not thrown up.
* * • *
Gibraltar, N.S.W., for one month, 20700 z.,
tonnage not given, which is absurd. This mine is now
about the most consistent gold-producer in the province.

“ Arbouin ” to The Bulletin from Chillagoe:
Re the furious little bluster of six or seven lines with
which “ S.J.” wipes out Chillagoe and the Herberton
District in a late issue. Chillagoe has been worked for the
last nine years, he says, but “we ” have abandoned it.
That’s quite true. Chillagoe, since its discovery, has
never been abandoned except by we, which, viewing the
present outlook of the district, must be a distinct disap-
pointment to we. Presuming, as “ S.J.” and one or two
other late paragraphists must, that all men connected
with Chillagoe are liars and villains of no shallow dye,
would it pay an able man in Stewart’s position to make an
absolutely false report, which he must be doing if “S.J. is
correct ? Also, what about Geologist Jack who, although
his report is not out, has admitted that it is extremely
favorable; and what about John Moffat, and those
associated with him in work on Chillagoe, sticking to it
for the last nine years and from time to time, m spite oi
the carriage and price of copper, making individual mines
on the field pay? Do they know nothing, all of them !
No, the trouble is that Moffat’s foresight and courage have
landed him very much on top. We wouldn t dig on
Chillagoe; he would; now we dig at him. It is improbable
that he has either time, necessity or inclination to dig
back.
Sixty men are working for the Linedale West
Chillagoe Co. (late Linedale Sj ndicate), at Bedford, or
Klondyke, or Arbouin (it will take its pick of the names
soon —said that the mining-commissioner has called it
Arbouin, certainly the most appropriate name of the
three, viz., that of one of the prospectors). Development,
though of course in its infancy, is going on vigorously.
The Spaniard, a shaft down about 20ft., shows a 2ft. lode
of solid copper ore ; lowest estimate of the few tons at
grass, 25 per cent. The Lamington, an open cut, 15tt.
high, shows from top to bottom 4ft. wide of ore of
quality. Laverock, Macao, Ladrones, Yankee, andbt.
Patrick are other good shows of same syndicate. M Clel-
land Bros.’ show, on Noonday Creek, three miles S.E. or
Arbouin, is a boomer on top, with no work done on it.
Four to 6ft. wide, it stands up out of the ground, solid
copper ore, and shows thus for about three chains along
its line. The copper is not all found yet—only yesterday
a Linedale West Chillagoe prospector located another good
surface show. I fail to see that this co.’s £lO shares (1500 of
them) were anything but good property at the £lOO at
which some of them changed hands. They are now re-
constructed into 3300 £'s shares. P.S.—This place is
looking first-class. I haven’t got a share in it, more s the
pity, so am not much prejudiced. “ S.J.” should pay it a
visit. . .

Another Chillagoe man : Joseph Palmer and
Sons, Sydney, give some very fine and large information
in tbeir monthly share-list. In the same issue they say of
the Chillagoe Proprietary : “We have been favored with
reliable information about this enterprise, and learn that
the cost of constructing the railway will be far greater
than indicated by the promoters, as the country presents
great engineering difficulties, the line crossing mountain
ranges and spevvy ground. We also understand that there
is very little wood or water in the district.” This is all
right except for a few trivial mis-statements. The country
does not present great engineering difficulties—the Gov-
ernment line covers all the mountain ranges on the way-
excepting only the sharp pinch known as the Feather-
bed at Koorboora. There is no spewy ground.. The line
passes through one of the richest timber districts m Aus-
tralia. There is always plenty of water to be got by easy
sinking in the driest months, and in the rainy season the
hundreds of creeks are swimable and dangerous. Other-
wise the cocksure Palmer is perfectly correct.
» V * *
“ Old Miner ” : Randolph Bedford wants to
know what the Griffith, Girofla, Harper, Atherton, and
Mt Garnet are if not true lodes? Now, having “been
there,” let me tell Mr. Bedford that there are no true
lodes on Chillagoe ; all the so-called rich mines are sur-
face-deposits, and many have been clean duffered-out.
Mt Garnet is a big formation of low-grade ore, and lies
about 80 miles from Chillagoe. I have visited many of
the “ mines ” pegged-out recently, and can safely say that
most of them are as free of copper as a hog is of feathers.
* * * *
Be Chillagoe: “Stewart’s report on the C.P.
Co.’s properties has been described as a plain, straightfor-
ward, workmanlike statement of fact. A perusal and
examination of the plans accompanying it does not bear
out this description. Mr. S. states that owing to the
enterprise of the Q. Govt, these districts have been
thrown open to private enterprise, and must shortly yield
their latent mineral and other resources. He has over-
looked, or forgotten, the fact that long before there was a
railway over the range, when most of the transport was
by team, or pack animals, via Port Douglas, fortunes
were made out of the mines on the Herberton district,
and that Chillagoe and other centres were opened up and
worked over ten years ago. If there is any reason
for the Chillagoe copper-boom on account of the
increased facilities offered by the railway, why,
in the name of common - sense, is there not
also a tin boom? The mines in the past proved highly
remunerative, the tin-dressing methods are even now
antiquated, and present prospects in many local tin pro-
perties look decidedly better than those of most of the
copper-mines, with the great desideratum up to date-
economical mining treatment and management.
“ The rich copper deposits of Cobar, Mt. Hope,
Cow Flat, Milburn Creek, and others in N.S.W., and Peak
Downs, in Queensland, paid well very many years ago
with further land carriage, worse appliances and methods
of treatment than are available now at Chillagoe, and alto-
gether under worse conditions than Chillagoe ever had to
bear up against, so that contention has no weight.
With regard to the railway, Mr. S. says the accompanying
plan seems most favorable. Just so : ‘lt seems. Every-
thing ‘seems’ right through the report. What sort of
phrases are these to convey any idea of the value of a
mining’ property to intending investors: ‘lt is not un-
reasonable to believe . . . Large deposits, more or less
impregnated . . . What appear to be most favorable in-
dications . . . With a proper system of exploration, great
results may be expected.’ The only definite statement
which, again, ‘ it only seems desirable to give,’ is the esti-
mate of ore in sight, and this, on Mr. S.’s own showing, is
merely a guess, as it is impossible to measure up any single
body of ore, or to sample it. Moreover, if, judging from the
heaps at grass, all of which are well sorted, Mr. S. reckons
his estimate of 500,000 tons in sight will go 10 per cent,
copper, it is the opinion, not of a professional pessimist,
but of one who does not care to bite off more than
he can chew when other people have to suffer for it.
He had to go round and examine a lot of picked
heaps of ore alongside inaccessible workings, together
with well-executed, sanguine, and apparently correct
plans. Surely no human being could possibly verify the
correctness of the supposed formations as shown on the
plans, in the time spent by Mr. S. on the ground, even if
the whole place had not been covered with long grass
armed with irritating spear-grass seeds. So far from any-
thing having been as yet proved, this present writer con-
tends that the whole group of mines are in a worse stati
than when absolutely virgm.
“ In the synopsis at the end of the report why
has 4064 tons lOcwt. been put down for 20-61 per cent,
copper, while a discreet silence is maintained as to the
contents of the other 6339 tons said to have been treated!
Why, again, has the system been followed in the report
of naming only the groups of leases, and referring to
the different properties by numbers, thus making
it difficult for even one who knows the mines to
follow the description properly, when it would have
been so easy to have taken the principal mines by name
and said yea or nay to them and their capabilities, and
left the scores of ‘ shallow excavations ’ and ‘ small de-
posits which do not call for further special a'tention’ to
take care of themselves? Of lease No. 15, Redcap group,
Mr. S. says it is to be regretted that the 200 ft. shaft is not
accessible, as it is the deepest in the district. Perhaps it
would be as well to ask Hon. J. A. Wallace, or the old
Queensland Prop 00. who worked it, what they think of
it—or perhaps the assay of the sample of pyrites taken
from the dump, 2 - 94 per cent, copper, 4oz. i6dwt. silver
per ton, is sufficient information. The par. relating to
lease No. 25, which Mr. S. reckons to rank as probably the
largest in a series of very large properties, is so lukewarm
and indefinite that if nothing better could be said of it its
flotation in the best district in Australia would be impos-
sible at any other than fever-heat boom-time.”

Mt. Morgan Gold Mining Co. dividends for
year, £300,000 ; wages, £139,000 ; new west works:,
£39,000; new mundic works, £15,000. Quantity of ore
handled during year has kept on increasing until in May
last it reached 17,455 tons. When new mundic works are
completed it will reach 19,000 tons. 24,704 tons of mundic
ore were treated for 90,5510 z ; 61,313 tons of oxidised ore
yielded 53,8440 z. ; 64,721 tons of low-grade ore gave
24 3040 z. ; and 2459 tons of mundic tailings were treated
for 16250 z. Prom ores sent to Aldershot 5230 z. were re-
ceived making a tdtal of 153,297 tons of ore and
170 860 oz. gold —giving an approximate average of loz.
2dwc. 7gr. to the ton. As dividend-duty returned £15,000
and royalty £BOOO, Mt. Morgan can claim to be a decent
contributor to Q. revenue.
* * * *
The Llanberris (Ballarat East) tributors con-
tinue to rake it in. Immediately after smelting a 1270 z.
find on 6th inst. they dropped on another mass of stone
matted with gold and good for about 150 oz. This is all
from the old Indicator lode (which is not a lode at all,
really) from which the Ballarat leads were probably fed —
poor in the main but flecked with such patches, so the
miners hang on eternally.
• * * •
Westralian mines and markets The Ade-
laide market continues very dull, without much business
doino- though prices in the leading stocks keep fairly
firm.” Lake View Consols had a fine output for June ; the
Ivanhoe cyanide plant is now running ; Hannan’s Brown
Hill plant is just getting under way; Horseshoe s 20
stamps should be running by the end of Aug. ; and de-
velopments at the Associated are being pushed on with
much vigor. They have one shaft down now 540 ft., and
will soon be cross-cutting simultaneously from two
shafts at 500 ft. and also at 300 ft. and 400 ft. There may
be a big stir in this stock within the next couple of
months, as they are also increasing their milling power,
and will resume shipping telluride ores again directly.
Generally the prospects of the big mines are good. The
question of the hour is the Boulder milling con-
tract, which looks like a monstrous job; it must be
detrimental to the interests of the Boulder shareholders ;
because the Boulder co. should treat its own tailings
iust as easilv as the Lake View or any other co. per
cyanide ; it is costing the Lake View co. at the most 9s.
per ton, and this, no doubt, will be materially reduced ;
it will be costing the Boulder co. from 9s. to 15s. per ton
more in accordance with the amount of gold extracted !
In London strong opposition is being made to this con-
tract with the object of forcing the directors to annul
it, and it is probable a big change will be made on the
Board, and more than likely that ‘ Billy * Jones will be put
on the Board by the dissatisfied shareholders. One thing
is certain—the Boulder directors have not yet heard the
end of this contract and have a very nasty time in store
for them.
“ Outside of Kalgoorlie, every mining-centre
pretty well has one or two mines, in some instances more,
giving really good returns of gold. The prospects of the
fields generally have never looked better, though the
market is so bad. However, bedrock seems somewhere
near, and, within the next six months or so, Westralia
will receive a great deal of attention ; not only Ival-
goorlie, but many other centres. Kanowna alluvial-
diggers are turning out an immense amount of gold. This
may be another Johannesburg ; it is quite on the cards
that a banket-formation will be found. lam assured
that there is one, but, under existing laws relating to
alluvial, it would be useless taking up any country to go
in for big work.
Three great W. A. producers: Lake View Con-
sols 5792 tons, 92730 z. : Ivanhoe, 1895 tons, 29030 z.;
Brown Hill, 1040 tons, 24090 z.

Brown Hill Central (Kalgoorlie) is improving in
London, it having been officially declared that Bottomley
hasn’t anything to do with it. At one time, only two
months back, the slightest rumor that the Bottomley
Group had anything to do with a mine sent the shares up
—and false rumors to the effect that Bottomley wasn’t
in it had to be instantly denied. Things are upside-down
now.

SOCIETY
As illustrating the ill-digested "jumble of
opinions which constitute N.S.W. law, the case of
a Ked tern man lately sent to prison for disobeying
an order of the court,, commanding him to pay £3,
may be cited. That man will have to stay in
prison, if he does not pay for 12 months. If,
on the other hand, he had, when the money was
due to the court, stolen £3 from a shopkeeper or a
lawyer and paid it over to the court, he would
nave escaped prison on the order racket, and if
discovered and convicted as a thief, would cer-
tainly not have been sentenced to more than a
couple of months, and would probably have got off
with a fortnight. The law, therefore, unwittingly
makes it well worth a court-debtor’s while to
thieve from someone near him as much as will
satisfy his blind creditor’s claim.

Private school teaching generally involves
more abject poverty than any of the other
genteel professions; but in S.A. the poverty is
more abject than usual—due, possibly, to the
strength of the denominational institutions. Of
the principal private collegiate schools which have
existed in the province during the last 15 years,
nearly every one has, at some time or other, been
forced to “arrange” with its wretchedly-paid
professors” and teachers. One of them “ar-
ranged ” four times in three years ; and things
had been cut so fine.at the end of that time that
the teacher who was preparing University candi-

dates was supposed to be actually getting 10s. per
y e ®k- As a matter of fact, he drew about 7s.
Another batch had to take their claims into court
the other day. The general explanation, no doubt,
is that this is the last profession at which men
With any business knowledge or foresight look
Yet most of them profess to furnish “a good
sound commercial education.”

The Licensing Bill now before Maoriland
i arhament contains one noble feature : It pro-
vides that when the publican is fined for allowing
men to drink on his premises after hours, the
drinkers shall be hneable m exactly the same pro-
portion. Which is decent justice, anyhow. When
a red-nosed individual prays for Sunday drink till
-IT? supplies it, he should take part of the
responsibility as well as the whole of the pleasant
feeling in the stomach. The publican is really the
less guilty of the two. The red-nosed Sundayper-
wifh V the f' rS r fU ?y Sl<le h , is ke yhole coerceJ him
with the standing threat that if he doesn’t get in
Sunday he will take his week-day custom else-
it mtn L i lB hI : S , atfcitl A lde ’ even if he doesn’t put
it into actual words. And, as the drinker is the
individual who incites to the crime and corn-
mits a good half of it (there are three acts in-
Jn i V ?h ie f Sel IllR ’ the . bu >’ ln -'- and the drinking
and the customer commits two of them), the old
law whereby he took none of the risk was out-
rageously one-sided. Moreover, the drinker, by
reason of the fact that his nose is a blot on the
scenery, calls for no special consideration.

which had, eight years before, been blown into
him by a bursting bottle of gunpowder recalls the
curious M.L. case in which a woman charged her
husband with having several times attempted to
stab her whilst warming his feet in the small of her
back! ihat locality, upon examination, showed
sundry small needle-like punctures, but a surgical
investigation of this luxurious hubby’s feet made

sailors needle which was working out between two
of the toes. His mother then remembered that
when a baby, he had been suspected of having
wallowed such an article.

Vic. Factories-Inspecfcor Ord reports that
many women have to work very long hours for a
pittance of about 10s. a week, and that one case was
that of “ a lady in reduced circumstances making
nightdresses, whose earnings for a hard week’s
work amounted to 6s. These wretched prices ”it
is added, are paid by one of the largest firms in
the city, and it can only be hoped it is done bv
the managers without the knowledge of individual
members of the firm, who figure largely in religious
and charitable work.” Sanguine Mr. Ord 1
Onlys : How’s this for a run of bad
luck ? It was told me by a veracious and super-
stitious old man. “I went into a Melb. hospital
° n a was put into a ward with thirteen
beds in it; the wall was decorated with peacock
leathers ; had a cross-eyed nurse attending on me;
1 smashed a looking-glass ; the first thing I
saw when I looked out of the window was
an undertaker’s advt. ; a drunken
trombone performer played the
-Dead March ’ one afternoon
outside the hospital gates
until arrested by the
police; was operated on
on the 13th of the month
(my predecessor had died
on the operating - table
under chloroform); and
I—^— recovered splendid-
ly.” I thought such a
catalogue as this should
be set to impassioned
drinks, and invited him
to that effect. As we
were walking down street
he stopped in front of a
sign-painter’s ladder, and
then skipped into the
middleof the road. “You
don’t catch me walking
under ladders,” he cried,
as he rejoined me on the
footpath.

The congested state
of Maoriland tows is the
outcome of godless educa-
tion. Thus : Secular educa-
tion lessens the number of
churchgoers. And thereby
the number of parsons and
tnemanufactureof wool-work
slippers. The demand for wool
is thereby diminished, sheep-farming- ceases to be
profitable, and thousands of country workers are
driven into the towns.

A powerful address on the danger of a
woman getting married to her best “ boy ” was
delivered in a Victorian court recently. A
woman was suing her husband for ‘'main-
tenance, and—‘‘she stated that, though then
married to another man, she was improperly inti-
mate with hubby for live or six years, and bore him
two children. In November, 1897, she obtained a
divorce from her old husband, and continued
living with the new one until April, 1898, when
she was married to him. From that time his
treatment of her changed. He beat her, treated
her like a dog,” &c., &c.
Never whacked, and seldom chidden,
Long as they are fruit forbidden
Edwin’s darling feeds her lover, suiting then his palate
well.
Marriage makes the mateship lawful,
Then he thumps her “ something awful ’’;
Passion holds him in subjection till the parson breaks the
spell.

A strange fatality occurred in a Melbourne
suburb recently. The story illustrates a fact
long patent to many men how unlit women
are to be mothers. The child of this particu-
lar woman was taken ill with bronchitis, or some-
thing of the kind, and the doctor recommended a
steam-bath, but did not think it necessary to ex-
plain the difference between a steam-bath and a
fire-escape, so the woman was left to carry out her
own idea of what a steam-bath ought to be. She
fastened a piece of hose to the spout of the kettle
and placed the kettle on the fire. The other end
of tt S e hose she introduced under the blankets of
the cradle in which the baby lay. Then the
mother went away, and thought no more of the
pauent for some time, and when at length she
htted the child from the cradle it was so horribly
sea dec! that portions of its body remained at-
af ter t 0 blankets ’ and ifc died ver y shortly

A Brisbane clergyman remarked the other
day to his flock that the worst enemy the churches
had to contend with was the 3d.-bit. Writer
denies the truth of this statement. The worst
enemy of the churches is the respectable citizen
who doesn’t even pan-out a threepenny-bit. Some
months ago a Brisbane sky-pilot sti uck the aver-
age contribution of his flock as totting up to about
twopence-farthing apiece. Evidently the three-
penny man wasn’t the worst by long chalks.

A Sydney public institution lately received
an application from a country town for a railway
pass to Sydney, for a hospital patient, accom-
panied with a request for a ticket for an attendant
—both tickets to be first-class, and to carry
sleeping berths. This request was made by the
local medical officer, who conveyed the im-
pression that he would not be answerable
for the consequences unless such tickets were
issued. Eventually a compromise was arrived
at. A first-class carriage was reserved, but no
sleeping berths. On reaching Sydney it was
round that the 4 attendant, '* on a man who did
licit appear excessively weak, was the medico him-
self.

CUPID’S COMPLAINT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Boy Cupid long had sought in vain
To win a maiden to his train,
By many a deep-set cunning plan
With amorous youth and learned man.
At length one day he saw her sit,
Eyes deep in thought, and brows close knit,
And chuckling softly in his glee,
“ I have you now, my love ! ” quoth he.
But Cupid’s face fell in dismay,
For, coming .-lose, he heard her say,
“Beginners’ Latin class at two
And of my Euclid girls, a few
Will be at Statics ; with the rest
Gen’ral revision would he best—
Those copy-books should ordered be—
I must enforce strict French at tea.
... A paper for my junior-class—
I wonder if that girl will pass ? ”
Poor Cupid, struck with shame and grief,
Turned to his mother for relief—
“ Scold not thy son,” was his complaint,
“ If Cupid’s power is growing faint,
But rather blame the Fates above
That maidens have no time for love ! ”

Lately a well-known passenger steamer
was sent, to dock for repairs, and replaced by a
vessel which —well, it wasn’t “At at Lloyd’s,
and itcaused the ordinary passenger to think that,
after all, travelling by rail was not so bad, though
more expensive. Said that one morning the

Dear B.—Beside the loss to Govt,
(you and mo) from free newspaper-postage
there’s loss to newspapers, especially in the
country. I know four of a syndicate who
read and repost newspapers one to the
Other; some of these stolen, some cadged,
some perquisites, and some purloined ex-
changes. Now, there are plenty of bush
readers and town readers of country papers,
who hunger for some particular journal.
This would have to be bought or stolen,
as before, but it is more difficult and
dangerous to appropriate three separate
halfpennies or postage - stamps. Also,
stamps and pennies are Beer. Add to
all this that daily papers several weeks old
and weeklies months ditto are passed on
without inspection. Free education can
be had even with stale literature, just as
non-Federation can vote with rotten eggs.
—S. the W.

“ P.” : At a recent Sydney Technical Col-
lege exam, for pupil teachers, the candidates
started at 9.30, took an hour for lunch, and finished
before 4. Seven subjects ! The arithmetic paper,
with four lengthy sums, had to be completed in
38 minutes, and to gain the aggregate marks every
question had to be answered. No wonder we’re a
fast lot 1

What’s in a name ? The winner of the
second prize in a recent Brisbane newspaper baby-
competition was one Ray Macgillicuddy.
Quite a troupe of old Brisbane identities
passed over to the other side last week. They
stand the heat better than the cold up North.
And so in Sydney, where dead-Chinaman influ-
enza-pneumonia has been striking down well-
known and other citizens right and left.
* * * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph reports an in-
teresting discovery made at Newcastle, N.S.W.,
on July 3, by Lloyd’s surveyor when going over
a barque. He had removed the close lin-
ing in the after-hold, and was applying the
hammer to the cement and plates, when the jar
of the blows revealed a rivet-hole, three-quarters
of an inch in diameter, in one of the plates about
3ft. from the keel. The water rushed in as the
frail stopping of cement was knocked out, and the
aperture had to be plugged up. A minute inspect
tion of the plate led to the belief that the hole
must have been a false one, originally filled up by
a blind rivet, which had fallen out. Had the
cement, which alone kept the ship dry, been
knocked out of the hole at sea, the vessel would
probably have gone to the bottom without anyone
knowing the exact reason why. And another
score of sailors’ lives might have been added to
the formidable list accumulated by Newcastle
coffin-ships.
“ J.D.” : Re “Southerner’s ”
par. on confessions. An R.G.
friend of mine relates to me that
last week he was attending, and
was kneeling with seven or eight
other people near the confessional,
when a male believer, hard of hear-
ing (and therefore loud of voice),
went in to divulge his sins. He
commenced to retail his indiscre-
tions, which were crimson and
many, in such a loud tone that
three lady-penitents fled and one
fainted. It wasn’t only the lurid
character of his sins that made the
trouble, but also his tendency to
call a spade a spade, and to pound
in the fact that it was a spade and a very awful
and violent spade at that.

Dear Bulletin. —About four years since,
in Tasmania, a man got gaoled over a girl under
the age of consent, age only being considered—for
the consent was there right enough, as per usual
with her. Accused faced Justice Adams (just re-
tired on a pension), who went sour on him, and
treated him to ten years’ hard, leaving a wife and
six children to shift for themselves. A little later
one Horsley brutally beat to death the paramour
who was tramping about the country with him,
for which Judge Dobson sentenced him to five
years hard. How beautiful are the grand old lag-
time traditions of Vandemonia, — July Fogg.

Faith-healing at Melb. hospital. Girl ad-
mitted suffering from alleged hip-disease. Ex-
amination showed nothing of the kind, and
hysteria was diagnosed. Surgeon after a time
thought he’d try psychical treatment, and assured
girl of cure by such a date. Result —rapid im-
provement, and on the day of promise the crutches
were discarded.

A suspected Melb. abortionist, whose case
is now sub judice, dropped to the game of the de-
tectives, and, in reply to a “ put-up ” letter asking
for his services, remarked :
You ask me to assist you in committing a crime, and,
in your Crotty-like munificence, you enclose the gigantic
sum of seven shillings and sixpence ! ! . . . Demosthenes
compares such a wretch as you to ‘ a viper which men
ought to crush wherever they find him, without staying
till he bites them.’ And Lord Ooke compares him to a
poisoner, the meanest and most dangerous of all mur-
derers.

Rochefoucauld. ”

Concerning the frequency of suicide
amongst Chinese prisoners in Australian prisons,
“ Timi ” writes to The Bulletin
The Chinese, of all the alien races in Australia—and I
include European foreigners in this category—show the
lowest per centage of criminals. Those who do come into
conflict with the law are thus, from their very fewness, .
isolated from others of their nationality, and I have no
doubt that it is the distressing strain of miserable loneli-
ness that is chiefly accountable for the proneness to
suicide of Chinese convicts. This isolation increases the
terrors of imprisonment ten-fold, and it seems to me that
it would he a simple act of humanity to permit the
Chinese to serve their sentences in some central prison
where they would not be cut off entirely from their
compatriots.

at his recent Cinderella ball, when the clock

home at midnight.”

Melbourne’s legal profession is feeling dis-
gusted just now. The Holy Roman Chinch has
mad“ terms—very modest terms, by the way - with
Mrs. Crotty ; and now it appears that the will of
John Matthew Smith (a quarter-millionaire, who
died lately) was drawn up in very simple form, and
doesn’t show one little peg for a lawyer to hang
his wig upon. J. M. Smith was a solicitor, there-
fore his sinfully simple last will and testament,
free from complex provisions and strange clauses,
is regarded as a breach of professional etiquette.

Good, old Tory Senate of Sydney Uni*
versity (writes a Bulletin correspondent) still
holds out in not conceding degrees to Queensland
students who cannot attend lectures. But a more
unkinder out happened lately to a Sydney-born
youth. This was a member of a family left badly
provided for, and there was great joy in the house
of Blank when it became known that the son and
heir had won at matriculation an Exhibition
which would provide a free University education
for three years. The boy, who inherits a talent
for mining pursuits, decided upon following
the engineering course, bought all his books, and
enthusiastically embarked upon his student’s
career. Then the Senate met and pondered.
“ Has anyone ever entered the engineering school
“VACANCY WITHIN.” —SHE DECIDED
TO TRY ELSEWHERE.
before with this exhibition?” “No, never—only
arts or science.” “Then, of course, no one ever
can or ought to.” So the young exhibitioner was
given his choice of arts or science. Decided upon

than the other, and now spends the time he

Woman on a Bicycle.
Since the fair sex has taken to bicycle-riding,
much has been written and said as to whether the
practice is injurious or not. We do not intend to
go into the scientific details of the subject, but it
is self-evident that a healthy woman, with a
throughly regulated system, is much better able
to indulge in such exercise than one whose general
health is not so good. Bile Beans for Biliousness
are just the medicine to keep the whole body in a
healthful state. They keep the liver in regular
working order, purify the blood, and clear all
waste matter from the system, thus reducing to a
minimum the chance of the delicate female organs
becoming deranged. They are an undoubted
specific for influenza, colds, biliousness, indiges-
tion, costiveness, piles, and all liver and kidney
complaints. They are made in one of the largest
and best equipped laboratories in the world, at
Detroit, Michigan, U.S.A., and have been used
by the best physicians in that country for years.
They are for sale by all chemists, and in purchas-
ing be sure you get the genuine. Whether you
ride a bicycle or not, you will find Bile Beans an
invaluable specific for all the above ailments.*
