A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 19, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
The Sydney Ladies’ Sanitary Association began
its winter season with Prof. Anderson Stuart’s
lecturette on “ Brain and Brains.” Similar little
hygienic homilies are to be delivered on five subse-
quent Mondays, for the benefit of enquiring
feminine minds. Hot-haired Dr. O’Neil holds
forth on fevers next week, and later on Drs. Camac
Wilkinson, Dagmar Berne, and Reuter Roth are
to give advice gratis on their own pet diseases.
As far as one can judge from lecture No. 1, the
grade is not going to be too terribly steep. Dr.
Anderson Stuart’s information was translated into
the very easiest English, adorned with just such
funny little anecdotes as a schoolmaster employs
to animate a class of schoolgirls, and had the
generally-debilitated air of medical information
laid down by a scientific man for lay ladies’
information.
The old, old frog that kicks its leg at so many
similar functions appeared again, and everyone
brightened up when Lady Hampden tried her
hand at rousing one kick more from the dead
beast. Have you ever wondered at the mesmeric
effect Her Ex.’s simplest movement has on a female
audience ? Everyone smiles with delight, and
the back row cranes over in wild terror lest it
should miss some of the genuine Govt. House
doings. This because Lady H. has turned to look
at the brain chart on the wall behind her. On
Monday, however, Her Ex. valiantly withstood
all this artillery of smiles and stares, and put her
little questions to the lecturer with heroic in-
difference.
The latter part of the lecture dwindled away
into a dissertation on the management of babies.
The lecturer owned up to the truth that a little
Anderson Stuart had set his mind working in
this direction, and gave utterance to a maxim that
sent cold shivers down many maternal backs. To
tempt a baby to laugh with a dangled finger is
worse than wicked. Maternal devotion must now
cease from distributing the philosophical reflec-
tions of its six-weeks-old offspring.
But here’s a touching story of maternal de-
votion for you. A bride and bridegroom left
N.S.W. one recent day for a honeymoon in Mel-
bourne. Next day the bride’s mother, wasting no
time, began hysterics. She uttered one cry con-
tinuously, “Icannotlive without her.” Sameafter-
noon she caught the express, and followed her pet
lamb to Melbourne. And from then, till the end
of the honeymoon-k-trois, wherever they went she
went too.
An engagement is announced between two jolly
good fellows, Mrs. Wolstenholme, our advanced
suffragist, and Professor Anderson, of Sydney
University.
And that reminds me that the newly-wedded
Earl of Seafield spent his honeymoon in Sydney
last week, and left on Saturday for London. The
Earl is an auburn-haired youth with nothing but
his big nose to point to his descent. His romance
runs chiefly on the startling impecuniosity of his
clan. The title is said to be the poorest in England.
Sydney University is agog with excitement
over the engagement of the Professor of Anatomy
to the daughter of an eminent Q.C. The young
lady attended anatomy-classes to further her pur-
suit of art. An engagement, expected to be an-
nounced shortly, is that of yet another professor
—to an ex-student, this time. The University
will probably be too small to hold all the women-
students who will desire to enroll themselves
next year.
Mr. Henry Gullett, acting-editor of S.M.
Herald, though he has not the rose in the shape
of a literary son, yet comes very near the rose next
month, when he acquires the clever Mr. Thomas
Heney, of the Herald staff, as a son-in-law.
Next month will be a great one for weddings.
Miss Lilian Manning, niece of the late Sir Wil-
liam, is to marry a remote connection of the same
name, who deals in “chilled meat,” and retire to
Bega (N.S.W.) for a season. Mr. Lister-Lister,
the artist, is also to be married soon.
Spite the ads. that heralded Madame Alva as
the greatest living dramatic soprano, her fame had
not previously spread across the Pacific. And it
appears to have only partially penetrated into
London smoke, for several musical Londoners
doing “ the Kawlinies ” appear to have known
little about Madame until now. It seems that you
have to come to Sydney to learn all about the
world’s greatest wonders.
The Alva concert co. arrived last week and
opened at the Town Hall on Saturday night.
Alva herself is a fragile, poetic-looking Jewess with
a small, dark head, a willowy figure,and a delicate,
intelligent face, that assumes a surprisingly im-
passioned expression as she sings. Her attractive
appearance was deepened on Saturday by an
Oriental robe of scarlet silk, brocaded heavily
with golden flowers, the biting scarlet kept away
from her face by a creamy confection of the cor-
sage. The fragility of her physique gives you no
warning of the intense abandon she is going to put
into her song. Her voice is a peculiar organ, finely
cultured in the true Italian school, fluent, expres-
sive, extremely dramatic, but lacking in velvet,
and a little doubtful in attack. Given a great
organ to sing with, Alva has feeling, ability, and
courage enough to be the greatest living dramatic
soprano, but her present voice does not take her
quite there. She put a tremendous lot of meaning
into her Wagnerian and Italian arias, and the
public quite understood. Then, too, translations
of the Italian and German words were printed on
the programme.
The pianiste, Miss Adela Verne, was 19 some-
time, but I should hardly think it was last year.
It may have been the year before. Why is youth
supposed to make good playing better ? But a re-
action is against premature performances, and
soon we shall be at the other, and more rational
extreme, where one asks of a pianist, “ how old ? ”
instead of “ how young ? ”
Anyway, Adela Verne is a magnetic, vivacious
player, with a masculine breadth that would have
made a magnificent artist of Elsie Hall, but with-
out the gentle Elsie’s superior scholarly technique.
Several sparkling modern compositions were very
racily rendered. Her gowning agreed with her
advertised 19 years—a white young frock with a
waist and a sash, and baby sleeves, and a lace neck-
frill. The other Miss Verne has so little to do
with the programme that I imagine she was
brought from home expressly. You know it is
quite up to date just now to send to England for
little artists, and put them into little blanks,
that innumerable colonial artists, just a little,
are pining to fill.
The rest of the company comprised another
blank-filling member, Samuel Masters, a mediocre
tenor, hardly possessed of “the grand manner,”
and John Lemmone himself, who stands so quietly
and smiles so modestly that you hardly realise he
is one of the greatest living flautists. Even before
Albani came, we had grown to expect all greatness
to be branded with a dazzling, smiting smile. I
long to hear again the lovely impromptu of Lem-
mone’s own compositions, given as an encore on
Saturday.
Another musical event of the week supported
that fine old adage, “The Fewer the Few, the
’igher the Art.” This was Madame Kellerman’s
Soiree Musicale, when M. Wiegand was presented
with the “palms” of Offieier d’Academie, and a
speech tied up in flowery French ; and the audience
was given many local compositions to soothe the frac-
tiousness that might follow on a French recitation
and several French speeches. The soiree squashed
in an unusual hall at Church Hill, hung round
with colored pictures of the Agony and the Atone-
ment. And the soiree matched the hall. I mean
to say it was unusual. Madame Kellerman
frisked round in blue satin with her hair hanging
down her back in little curls, and her youthfulness
more pronounced than ever, and all went well
until one fiddler wanted to alter the programme
and play before another fiddler who was billed for
an obligato to a leading baritone’s song. Then a
storm began to brew, and hot winds prevailed be-
hind the scenes. Perfesh’nal variances are not
worth ink and paper, but I record this one for the
sake of the courteous baritone who was dragged
into the discussion, got upset with the fiddling
squabble, and lost his voice in the midst of a local
ave.
But the funniest part of that soiree happened
with the medal and a bow (please pronounce this
bo). The razory Frenchman faced the big, bulgy
Belgian, and a series of bows began. Biard
d’Aunet’s curtseys were as French as Wiegand’s
were Flemish, and the quaint study in bobs en-
dured for over a minute. Wiegand then coolly
drew his reply from his pocket and read it aloud
with Madame Kellerman peeping over his.
shoulder. It remarked that recent experience
had taught silence was golden, and he simply
thanked everyone for the honor they had paid
him. A rumor got abroad that all the organists
had been united in the bonds of friendship that
same evening, but it seems unlikely.
Yet another musicale was given by various
fowls, dogs and canaries at the Exhibition Build-
ing during the week. Owing to the superiority of
the feathered tribes, this soiree lasted three days,
and was as loud on the last day as the first. It
isn’t everyone who can rave over a fowl, but surely
the most cosmopolitan fancy will thrill to a
Brahmapootra hen two feet high, walking about
with the most exaggerated elegance in thick
white feather bloomers. I also fell in love
with an immaculate white owl, placarded the
finest in the show. But it made no response what-
ever to my suit, and after that experience I’m in-
clined to think owls are not the Solomons they’re
painted.
When Madame Alva is not gushing about
Donizetti to press interviewers, she wanders by
the seashore, marvelling at the beauties of “ Our-
arbour.” Armed with a kodak, she inspected
Manly one day last week, but to the Manlyites,
sated with the charm of the surrounding sea and
landscape, there was infinitely more to gaze at
and admire in the prima-donna’s chic Parisian
fawn-and-rose-pink-costume, her remarkably tight
white lace veil, ravishing toque, and dainty
parasol.
A visit to Pattie Browne revealed a libel on that
lady’s physique. She looks slighter than of yore
in her walking dress, and infinitely more graceful.
She has also lost her old firm faith in Australia’s
critical capacities. Six years in London, with fat
salaries, and the prospect of fatter ones, have
turned her head the other way, and she now
frankly expresses a contempt for her old home-
made paper-cutters, butter-knives —even jam-
spoons. In fact, finds the press deteriorating, and
the public taste nowhere. In the light of day, she
is as young and healthy-looking as behind the
footlights. She has acquired a large amount of
chic as regards her frocking, but suffers from a
stiltedness of expression that sits ill on merry
Pattie Browne, the born soubrette. The Court
Theatre, or the Adelphi, claims her when her six
Australian months are over.
By the way, it was a bit of bad luck for Pattie
that she returned to Sydney while Bland Holt
was showing off Miss Fitzmaurice Gill as Lady
Godiva at the Royal. Mankind is the same
old George—l mean Adam.
Another Miss Kerr (the youngest) has joined
the Bland Holt co. The family consists of four
daughters, two of whom are married and accom-
plished musicians. All are good-looking and have
the “well-set-up” look that smart Melbourne
girls so much affect. Miss Annie Kerr is a dis-
tinct addition to the pictorial effects of B.H.’s
drama, “ White Heather.”
Bland Holt has also acquired a first-rate musi-
cian as leader of orchestra —Mr. Percy Kehoe.
The descriptive overture played to the “ White
Heather” is a very fine study, containing a tuneful
canon in three parts, and winding up with a
march in which most skilful contrapuntal effects
are again employed. This overture deserves to be
heard soon and often by itself in public.
Thus the self-satisfied innocent who chatters in
the woman’s column of the Sunday Times (July
17): —“ The elections. My advice has been taken
about Women Suffrage. The politicians have
dropped it, and no voice of protest is heard.”
After all, why spoil a pretty little theory for the
sake of a fact ?
Society dames, who have lately returned with
a Hinglish polish, no longer give curt little nods
to their friends, but bow slowly from the waist.
“ She bends her back, and bows her head,” as the
author of “ Dorothy ” put it.
Now that women have taken to riding bikes and
mending them with hairpins, the supply of the
latter implement will soon be no longer equal to
the demand. At any rate they seem to be antici-
pating such a state of things in England already,
for an Australian visitor writes that the ladies
there no longer confine their tresses with hairpins,
but with a kind of long comb that bends back-
wards and forwards catching up all the hair, and
finally sticks out on top as an ornament. This is
the lucid description of a mere man, but it sounds
very pretty.
Erom Messrs. Paling and co. comes a cata-
logues of pianos, well-illustrated with pictures of
their finest stock. The time-payment list ap-
pears to be enjoying huge patronage from the pub-
lic, and I believe that the testimonials are endless
from those who are satisfied with their bargains.
But what about a testimonial from the man next
door ? Now, he is the one whose word would be
really invaluable. Yet the music-firms never in-
vite him to testify.
The same firm sends Maud Fitz-Stubbs’ latest
composition, “ The Premier Waltz.” It is dedi-
cated to George Reid, and, for Miss Fitz-Stubbs’
sake, I hope his sun does not set too quickly. The
waltz is a bright, loud measure of the same type
as the famous “Heather,” and I see no reason
why it should not be as popular. Another local
lady, Miss Annie Summerbelle, sends me
a waltz, “The Almida ” (Nicholson and
co.). This is marked with a tuneful
second movement, and has already caught
on in Sydney ball-rooms. From W. R. Fur-
long comes a new song, “Babbie,” inspired by
“The Little Minister” and dedicated to Pattie
Browne. A winsome picture of Pattie adorns the
cover, but the song nowhere comes up to that
picture. The words set out to tell some strange
story in still stranger Scotch, but what the music
has to do with the words I am incapable of dis-
covering. And what either have to do with print
is a still more impossible problem. I have also
received a copy of a conscientious little magazine
calling itself the Australian Musical Times
and Magazine of Art. It is unusually capable
and interesting, hot too technical for the public,
but sound in its criticisms, and full of a lot of
bright stuff about local artists and musicians.
Some smart little portraits from the pen of Rupert
Hale decorate various articles.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“In the opinion of the fashionable person who
thinks everybody else is dying to get an unob
structed look at her £lOO dress, the Maddens
ought to be relegated to Siberia, at least, for
asking such a crowd of the educated un-
wealthy to their last ball. It was bad enough the
time before, but at No. 2 the pretty in-
expensive home-made frock was everywhere
in evidence. Numerous family parties met
together and arranged their dances before the
band struck up. Many amatory couples found
the cosy fires in the drawing-rooms more attrac-
tive, and settled themselves down to a night’s
flirtation on the plea that the ballroom had grown
too small for dancing. The special dance for the
21 debutantes was the showpiece of the night.
The fair Sylvia was nice enough to lend her be-
trothed frequently through the night to forlorn
wallflowers and sit herself, a study in pink, all
forlorn upon the platform, while her sister Ruby
flashed about like a star in silver-spangled black
net.
“Fun, wild and uproarious, for those on the
top steps, struggling as sheep in a pen for those
lower down, laughter, threats, groans, reproaches,
and over all the hoarse voices of the orderlies
roaring to the drivers, was the lively order of
things at the getting-away from the Maddens’
ball. All the cabs in Melbourne seemed to have
got muddled up in the drive. The rain came
down in torrents from 7 p.m. till 2 a.m., and so
the economical tram was not in requisition, and
every family had ordered its cab for the same
hour. From 1 till 3 this went on, and, but for the
orderlies seizing frightened ladies and bundling
them pell-mell into any cab that came first, every-
one would have been there until the milk came
round in the morning.
“ Melb. fashionable society has its last new
clothes upon its train. It can’t go to the simplest
entertainment without arraying itself like Solomon
proceeding to the Temple. The other n l&ct a
little play was being done at Christ Church Sun-
day-schoolroom, South Yarra, for the benefit of
some one of its charities. Toorak came down like
the Assyrian, gleaming in ‘ purple and gold
satins rustled, jewels flashed, and the feathers
of the elderly society dames waved over
their puffed-out hair as they caught the
draughts from the different doors. Which
all looked very funny against the whitewashed
walls, wooden rafters and bare boards, and made
a special object-lesson for the little gaping Sunday-
schools who stood up at the back. In the very
first seat, like a stolid rebuke to modern fr ppery,
sat the white-haired mother of a very fashionable
family, in a voluminous black cloak and a goodly-
sized black bonnet ; and then Canon Tucker
wandered in, in his old coat, and seemed thankful
that at least one person hadn’t put his shabbiness
to the blush. ‘ A Cup of Tea,’ chief parts taken
by Mrs. Alfred Cornish and Col. Bingham, went
smartly enough for a bigger public. Mrs. Cornish
was such a vision of grace one could even under-
stand her stage-husband forgiving her tantrums.
“ The cycling gusher for a Melb. daily says : -
‘ A friend of mine, who probably does more
cycling than any other lady in Victoria, told me
recently that within the last couple of years she
has been able to wear shoes and gloves nearly a
size smaller than before she took to the wheel.’
Plump girls of the biking persuasion are ad-
vised to view this paragraph in all its bearings.
If the wheeliste in question has so reduc' d the
flesh on her hands and feet, how much has she re-
duced her chest and hips measurements ? A good
many of the present scribe’s lady friends have
hiked their extremities two or three sizes smaller
through anaemia.
“ At a recent meeting of the Melb. Women’s
Hospital Committee, the appointment of ‘ En-
gineer ’ was considered. The choice fell upon a
comparative ancient of 54 years, and when he re-
tired, humbly bowing, Mrs. Thornley pronounced
him a ‘nice old chap,’ and another dame said he
had such a ‘nice old face,’ and yet another that
he was ‘ all the better for being old.’ The ‘ En-
gineer ’ who had possibly been thinking of getting
his hair dyed, no doubt reflects that there are some
circumstances in which length of years counts for
righteousness. In a woman’s hospital job, for in-
stance.
“ A Melb. restaurant was some time ago refitted
at much expense, but to the horror of the pro-
prietor, the attendance began to wane instead of
expanding. At last, the mystery was explained.
The new electric-lights used were very powerful
and not artistically shaded, and their radiance
gave away the ‘ make-up ’ of every woman in the
room. Nothing short of an actress, and a care-
fully decorated actress, anyway, could appear to
advantage in the tell-tale glare.
“ Cartwright’s daughter, Edith Morley, nas
been under an operation, to have removed from
her face the asphalt which got embedded beneath
her fair skin through her horse accident in Syd-
ney. The operation has been successful, and
Edith is no longer in danger of being perpetually
patched, like a Georgian belle.
“ A parcel of new, or nearly new, dance music
from Allan and co. is weak in waltzes, but strong
in‘square’ pieces. The ‘lrresistible Lancers’
(Alfred Lee) introduce sundry classical airs from
the ’alls at opportune moments, and are full of go.
Likewise the ‘ Vivacity Lancers ’ (Theo. Bonheur)
deserve their name. The waltzes to hand are,
alas ! wanting in soul and backbone—they belong
to the class of music that one composes in one’s
head whilst riding on a tram-dummy. ‘ Allons
Nous ’ is, perhaps, the best of them, though Bon-
heur’s ‘Continental,’ which has a bright air or
two, compares favorably with a lot of last season’s
goods.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
“ Elector in doubt ” : Other qualifications
being equal, vote for the candidate who supported
the Convention Bill.
The Reid Govt, has provided 50 pairs
blankets for the poor of Petersham, for which
Curly Jones stands in the N.S.W. Ministerial in-
terest. In a word, just before the general elec-
tion, the Ministry sends to Petersham a bribe of
blankets in order to help Jones’ return. But
what a brutal insult! A mere hundred bla- kets
offered as tjie.price of such a deed as the •eturn
of Jones ! Such an affront, offered to a respect-
able, self-respecting electorate like Petersham,
can surely be only wiped out in Blood ! The Bul-
letin wouldn’t return Jones, or Alfred Allen, or
Knapp, or Ghould, or Brunker, or anybody like
that for 5000 pairs of blanky blankets.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A Queensland Labor-member struck out
on a new taek, the other day, by attending a
Government House levee.
Sons of three N.S.W. Chief-Justices
reached the Bench of that province—Forbes and
Dowling the District Court Bench, and M. H.
Stephen the Supreme.
Rev. W. Egiinton, aged 80, died in Bris-
bane, the other day. He was a railway man in
England before he took to the cloth, and it is
claimed that he originated the staff system.
James Crotty, the mining speculator whose
will has created so much surprise, is said to be the
son of a corset-maker in Dublin. Both his father
and brother are stated to have been in that
business.
Her daring “Woman Who Did” experi-
ment does not seem to have completely obliterated
Miss Edith Lanchester. Her name appears among
the Socialist candidates for the recent London
Vestry elections.

STATIONMASTER: “ I must give this man in charge,
constable; the porter saw him try to throw
himself in front of the S.lfi down train."
THE MAN : “ Give us a sandwich, boss."
“Diamond” Brown, the architect, so well
known in Melbourne in his days of abounding
prosperity, and later on in Sydney, is now a
familiar figure in Hobart. D.B. built the Theatre
Royal, Melbourne.
Melb. tramway secretary Sprigg, whose
liberal ideas of treatment of employes are now
amazing the Melb. Oomniissic n, is brother of
Premier Sprigg of the Cape. The Premier begun
life at Capetown as a greengrocer. A “ sprig ”of
Democracy.
An Irish-American paper lately included
P. O. Fysh, Tasmania’s Treasurer, among a list of
successful Irishman abroad. His name figured as
“ Fatrick O’Fysh.” And an English provincial
paper lately spoke of Tasmania's Premier as
Edward Bradawl.
Gov. Lamington went exploring a little
“on his own ’’ on his recent Papuan visit, and
came upon three natives who knew not Maegregor
nor the Hetnpire. Result: Raised spears, much
yabber by native policeman attending his Ex.,
and graceful retreat to boat.
Gladstone’s sublime faith in the House of
Lords is evinced by his understood wish that his
grandson is to become Lord Liverpool, on attain-
ing majority. For this, greatly to the chagrin of
the family at large, all the estate is massed, to
secure the income of a lordling.
Director Holtze, of Adelaide Botanical
Gardens, is one of the most cosmopolitan person-
ages in Australia. His father was a Hanoverian,
his mother a Frenchwoman, and his grandmother
a Spaniard. He married a Russian, and his
daughter has wedded a Welshman.
“Vandal,” to The Bulletin: Names
count for little now-a-days. Had it been a son of
the American Bayard who performed the feat
of blocking the channel at Santiago, the name
would have seemed in keeping with the deed.
Had it been an incident of fiction told by Clark
Russell, the hero of it would have been a Rodney
or a Collingvvood. But such an impossible name
as Hobson, perilously near to the ’umble grocery-
man Hobbs, of the Fauntleroy yarn, would have
utterly destroyed the dramatic touches of the
story.
A recent Melb. Police-court case disco-
vered an astonishing person, who swore he had
not been in Melbourne city for 30 years, though he
lived in the suburb of Fitzroy. Had never had
any occasion to make the daring expedition, ho
said, and wasn't going to take any chances with
the gentleman whose rich uncle had just died in
Fiji. A year or two back there died in West
Melb. an old Irishman who had not, since his
arrival 37 years before, been east of Queen-street,
except every Sunday as far as Lonsdale-street to
church.
Dear Bulletin.—Why so dolorous in your
9th of July par about Victor Daley and his photo ?
Why the insinuation that the latter is the picture
of Woe ? In one part of this issue we are favored
with a one-third column item quite in the snivel-
some vein of a Methody in memoriam, and over a
page or two we find this same sorrow-stained
V.J.D. chortling hilariously, and cavorting like a
colt all up and down a couple of columns of wild
comedy. No doubt Daley has had his allowance
of undrownable cares, possibly a care or two over,
but he is a humorist and can wear them jauntily.
The book ! Yes, but that is not the whole Daley
—there is another phase of him, a phase I and
others would like to see published in a volume of
rollicking rhyme. Daley has no more reason to be
ashamed of his laughter than of his tears.—E.D.
In. spite of electric frogs and glasses of
water, Dr. Maloney is thought to have a good
chance of capturing a Melb. City Council seat at
the coming election. He will no doubt succeed,
if elected, in keeping the minimum-wage question
prominently before the public, and the City
Council meetings will not be unproductive of
press-copy, at any rate, during his term of office.
Purves, Q.C., was obviously “ pulling the
leg ” of Orange organisations at the Melb. Town
Hall “ Twelfth ” celebration. All James’s re-
marks indicated that he had his tongue in lus
capacious cheek, and his eagerly-anticipated
speech, expected to knock the stuffing out of the
true believers, turned out to be a mere string of
Federation anecdotes with no bearing on Dutch
Billy at all.
A Melb. identity writes that none of the
street nomenclature, within the square mile of the
city proper, has any reference to Queen 'Victoria.
The streets were named after she came to the
throne, but nobody knew it. Prayers went on
for King William, in the Melb. churches, until
four months after his death. The chief western
streets were named after King William and
Queen Elizabeth.
Bruce Smith was caught napping at an
election meeting, last week. He had silenced an
interrupter who claimed to be well-educated with
the remark that education did some persons more
harm than good. A little later on something re-
minded him of Socrates with a lantern looking for
an honest man. “ Lor’,” ejaculated the squashed
one, “ I always thought it was Diogenes. Just
see what education does for some people ! ”
“ King Mickey,” last monarch of his tribe
in Illawarra (N.S.W.), obtains, at the age of 70, a
warrant to have himself clothed at Government
expense for the rest of his life. The old man’s
hope was to be dressed in the cast-off garments
of some Blue-M'>l4ll l. a,in-parrot-looking cavalry
officer, bnt the sedate Brunker kicked at such a
use of martial duds. “ Mickey ” is, however, sure
of a coat and trousers for the rest of his days.
When J. C. Williamson first saw Miss Ada
Reeve and Bert Gilbert, they were acting together,
in an English provincial theatre, without any
matrimonial intentions, as far as the lady was
concerned. Williamson, anxious to fix up an
Australian engagement for the lady, suggested
that Gilbert, should come with her, and make it
their honeymoon trip. Now, possibly, the manager
feels sorry that they didn’t come to hand in an
unmarried state.
Papuan Gov. MacGregor is so enthusiastic
a philologer that lie immediately hooks-on to a
speaker of any alien tongue —bar Chinese —and is
specially delighted if the lingo happens to be the
one he is himself delving into at the time. Re-
lated of him that once, when studying Dante’s
melodious numbers on the beach, he fell into such
“rapture by the lonely shore” that he came very
near being stalked by hostile natives and in-
continently knocked upon the vice-regal head.
Mr. Joseph Co wen Syme is a leading
shareholder in Crottyland, Mount Lyell. When
Uncle David bought him out of The Age, for
£140,000, few believed that he would go one
better, in anything like a Mount Lyell. The
present writer once asked Mr. “Joe” to take
part in starting a paper. “ No,” replied Joseph,
“The Age is good enough for me. I find that
whereas it rewards us for all the money we like
to put into it, in outside ventures I always lose.”

A brief residence among belted earls and
haughty countesses seems to have done little to
quell the naive bonhomie of a well-known Vic-
torian lady. At the opening of Parliament the
nice old thing affably shook hands wits all the
dignitaries assembled in the Council Chamber as
she passed through; and afterwards, completely
carried away by her natural pride in the occasion,
she picked a neat little boutonniere from the Par-
liamentary flower-beds, and coquettislily pinned
it in the coat of Melbourne’s bachelor politician
(Duncan Gillies), whose sphinx-like expression as
he received the unsolicited attention was a perfect
comedy to witness.
The lamented demise of “Good ’Evans”
(Gowen Evans) in England last year has brought
about more changes in Melb. Akgus staff than
people at the time could foresee. Mr. Willoughby,
although editing the paper on strictly “ London
lines,” and leaving the small details of office to
others, has thought it necessary to discontinue the
weekly “Timotheus” article. “Ithuriel,” his
successor, by no means adequately fills his shoes,
and the loss is keenly felt. Ted Cunningham,
instead of being chief of staff, now makes an able
lieutenant to the editor, while at least three
members of the staff wrestle with the sub-editing
at different periods.

Rev. ex-Premier Shirley Baker still worries
the yam-eating Tongese. He tried to heal the
religious schism over there, but his treatment has
resulted in further all-round dissension and he has
once more shaken the dust of Tonga off his boots.
He also threatens an action against the matrimom-
ally-dazed young king.
The original editors of the Melb. Labor
weekly, the Tocsin, resigned on 30th .June last on
account of the business committee having decided
to take away from them, for commercial reasons,
the censorship of advertisements which was given
to the literary staff at the inception of the paper.
They still continue, however, to contribute.
London Times is publishing a series of
articles by Charles Lowe (one-time Berlin cor-
respondent) on “Our Future King,” thereby
meaning Albert. Isn’t the Times a bit previous?
It is by no means certain that Tummy will out-
last Her Gracious. Writer is prepared to wager a
trifle that the next king’s name will be George V.
Rev. Dr. Brewster, an early Australian
identity who died lately at Capetown leaving a
personal estate valued at .£83,729, had the cheek
to bequeath £5OO for the conversion of .Tews, and
about the same sum for the conversion of the Irish
to liis own brand of Protestantism. Brewster was
an LL.D. and sat for a while on the judicial
Bench in Melbourne.
Aid. Amess, just dead at Melbourne, laid
the foundation-stone of Howard Spenselev’s Royal
Arcade, 30 years ago. He was then Mayor, but
he never forgot lie was a stonemason. That day
he made a speech, and said he didn’t know any-
thing about oratory, and hadn’t much education,
but he knew what a square day’s work was, and
that was how he got anything he had. Which
was rather a jar for Howard —who had got all he
had, including the Royal Arcade, by marrying a
rich wife.
The case of young Desailly, just given 12
months for cattle-duffing in Gippsland, recalls one
of some old-time squatters of the same name in
Riverina, who had a regular run of bad luck.
After working for years and years, and expending
about .£lOO,OOO in extensive dams, canals, &c.,
so as to make their place drought-proof, they
had about three years of dry weather without a
drop of water to moisten the dams. Of course the
inevitable evictor from the Bank took possession,
and hunted them out of house and home; but
such was the cruelty of Fate that, before they
reached the boundary-gate of the run they were
expelled from, a tremendous waterspout came
down, soaking the whole country and filling the
long-useless watercourses now lost for ever to
them.
The sum of £7BOO now
tands to the credit of the fund
or the Woolley scholarship in
Sydney University ; the prize
will be immediately thrown
open to competition. It is over
30 years since D \ Woolley’s
death (he went down with the
London, in the Bay of Biscay),
and, bit by bit, the fund has
been growing till the bequest of
Edwin Dalton brought it to its
jiresent amount. Woolley had
been an educational enthusiast
while filling a chair in Sydney
University, and, at his death,
the N.S. VV. public raised £2OOO
as a token of sympathy with his
widow. He was the originator
of the annual public examina-
tions which connect the State
schools with the University,
and are still in vogue. Woolley
and ex-Governor Bowen com-
peted for a University chair, in
Greece, on one occasion ; Wool-
ley won, but was disqualified
on the ground of being a cler-
gyman, and the after-time Aus-
tralian Governor was given the
post.
O’Hara Burke, the ex-
plorer, who died at Cooper’s
Creek, was in love with Julia
Mathews, the actress. On the
eve of his departure from Mel-
bourne he bade her farewell in
her dressing-room at the old
PrineessTheatre. “ Isthereany-
thing I can do for you,” he said, “ before I leave?”
She thought a moment, then she said : “ You
might send me in a few oysters and a small bottle
of stout from the corner shop.” And even that
did not cure him.
William Long, brother of the distinguished.
Scottish artist and himself a man of considerable
abilities, committed suicide at Alexandra (Vic.),
the other day. Narcotism appeared to be
the cause—or it may be that it was mere
nervous failure—and the jury returned the
usual “ temporary insanity ” verdict. But a par-
son up there knew better, and showed his sym-
pathy with the old cross-roads-and-stake method
of suppressing suicide by refusing to officiate at
the grave. A friend undertook the duty and
astonished the mourners by beginning with the
Church of England service and ending with self-
slaughtered Lindsay Gordon’s “Dirge” from
“ Ashtaroth,” the first verse of which runs :
With earth to earth, and dust to dust,
Ashes unto ashes go ;
Judge not; he who judgeth just,
Judgeth merciful also.
Earthly penitence hath fled,
Earthly sin hath ceased to be,
Pile the sod on heart and' head.
Miserere, do mine!
“Melb.” : Demise, this week, Hoey, who
preceded Davidson as Coroner Youl’s orderly
policeman. A cheery philosopher was Hoey. I
have dropped in upon him at the Morgue, down
among’ the dead men and women, on a hot day,
and found him with a short clay pipe, bowl down-
wards, in his mouth, while, in his shirt-sleeves, he
played the refreshing Yan Yean hose on half-a-
dozen corpses of ladies and gentlemen round the
shelves, and all he would say was, “ Business
brisk ! ”
Alex. Rawlinson, private secretary to Tas.
Gov. Gormanston, has been knighted by the Pope
because of his civility to the Cardinal and Aus-
tralian R.C. bishops at Govt. House, Hobart, in
’96, when they went to celebrate Archbishop
Murphy’s episcopal jubilee. A.R. is son of an
Anglican parson. The Gov. of Tasmania and its
Agent-General are R.C’s, and if its Premier isn’t,
his sister is. Miss Braddon went over to Rome
some years ago, and is now one of Father
Bagshawe’s most devout parishioners at Rich-
mond.

John Scott, lately deceased, won /nuch of
his popularity in the early days of Queensland
through his relationship with Walter Scott, the
novelist. He was ail authority on the writings of
his great ancestor, and one of his favorite presents
to young people who attracted him was a Scott
volume in prose or verse. He was the quietest of
men, and as nearly as possible ignorant of the
game of politics. But, in his day, to be a squatter
commanded the votes of most inland constitu-
encies, and Clermont, Springsure and Leichhardt
effusively welcomed his appeal.
Ben Tillett’s final Melb. send-off—ne has
had several—was a wildly hysterical function. The
ecstatic party assembled at Spencer-street in full
force, and smothered the departing agitator with
valedictory enthusiasm. Poems were recited at
intervals, songs were chorused, speeches made,
and cheers periodically punctuated the proceed-
ings. There were lots of ladies, who vied with one
another to present Mr. Tillett with articles of
personal attire. There were gifts cf handker-
chiefs, etc., and one blushing matron came along
with “a beautifully embroidered nightshirt.”
“Q.” : I recall the late David Henry serv-
ing his four years for embracery in Melb. gaoL
He was allotted the “soft ” billet of hospital dis-
penser, and in that capacity managed tolerably
well under the medical officer’s supervision. His
prison-life made him very “fatand jolly,” although
his health was all the time supposed to be broken.
He had better luck than the Abrahams, for his
term was shortened on the plea of shattered con-
stitution. A fair knowledge of and slight skill in
fencing I owe to David’s tuition during this period.
He was an adept with the single-stick and rapier,
and was also very ingenious in contriving fanciful
designs in paper, and beguiled his plentiful leisure
in that occupation. Taking him all round, in or
out of gaol, he was a genial old man. But he
separated business and sentiment relentlessly,
never allowing personal gratitude to temper com-
mercial greed.
To the fate of bushranger Hall the veteran
Captain Battye—in his early days a page of honor
to Queen Adelaide —who died last week at North
Sydney, used frequently to refer with regret.
Battye was, in the Gardiner days, police superin-
tendent in the West, and sometimes for a week
together hardly left his horse’s back in pursuit of
one or another of the gang. Hall was originally a
sort of squatter and a personal acquaintance of
Battye before he took to evil courses. Good
nature first brought him into association with
Gardiner, Dunn and others, and when, later on,
he espoused their style of life, he every now and
then repented and endeavored to turn back.
Finally, in one of these fits of repentance, he went
to a friend with whom he had deposited over
.£lOOO and, stating that he intended to clear out
of the country and live honestly, requested his
money back. The friend pretended to get it
ready, detained Hall over night, apprised the
police of where he was camped, and got them to
shoot him as he emerged from the door next
morning. Thirty-four slugs were found in his
body. And Hall’s “ friend ” received half the
reward—£soo.

Aboriginalities.
Re the influx of Chinese into Queensland :
*' I saw twenty-eight live and three dead Chinese,”
writes a Northern traveller, “between Avon Downs
and Brunette Downs. The first I found dead was
a Chinese boy—dead in the gidya, from fever and
cold. At Conolly’s Lagoon, while looking for
wood in the dark, I stumbled over a dead China-
man in a dungaree suit—no swag. Probably lost
his road, wandered about, and died. I expect to
find more dead on my return journey.” Grim
enough, isn’t it ? Northern Territory Chows must
be pretty hard-up when they’ll risk all this. It
takes a lot to make John go exploring on his own
account.

“Werrimee” : Noticed lately that a sow
and her litter were very restless on cold mornings
running to and fro between the blacks’ camp
and the wood-heap, squealing all the time. I
watched, and soon noticed that when two gins
came over according to custom after sunrise to
make a fire at the wood-heap, the four squealing
pigs met and followed them back, and as soon as
the fire was made gathered around it with much
appreciative grunting. Piggy’s head is screwed
on the right way.

The snake-yarn, the bunyip legend, and
the tick-lie have had their day—to say nothing of
the rabbit mendacity and other trifles. The pig
is having a turn now in the North. Cooktown
paper tells how a small white Berkshire pig fol-
lowed a milk-cart from Killarney to Cooktown,
accompanied its owner on his rounds, and then re-
turned trotting close behind—a distance of 30
miles. The plucky grunter was only a little foot-
sore. Perhaps this anecdote may stir up a few
similar reminiscences of porcine feats in country
districts. The pork-Ananias is invited to send
along his best pig-inaccuracy forthwith.
the new school of duffing.

risk and more money.”

“Werrimee”: Nearly every squatter of
consequence has an aboriginal name, referring to
some personal peculiarity. Donald Campbell, ot
VVooroowoolgen, N.S.W., was known as “Mo-
bung— good old man.” One M'Donald, whose
mouth was mostly agape, was called “ Mooginja”
at qnyoatcher and Bates, of Connulpie Downs,
iN.a. W., who was bald-headed, was nicknamed
Yannaburn ’ —a “clay-pan.” Another, who
was wont to come upon his black men unawares,
Quebie ’—“sneak.”

P ear Bulletin, — Re murder by explosives.
When on a M.L. station I wanted to get rid of a
superfluous dog. Took him into a paddock, and
tied him up to a gate with two dynamite plugs
and a piece of fuse attached to his neck. Ran up
the side of an adjoining hill to watch the shower
of dog. Result : loud explosion, gate blown to
atoms, and dog home before me without a hair
turned. — Rata.

Dear Bulletin. — Re your par. about
German Charley. Two days after his death a lusty
young fellow came to Bedouin and said that
Charley was to be envied, and that he thought he
would hurry up and go after him. We all thought
he was fooling, but one day we heardjthe report of
a gun just outside, and when we went to see who
was crow-shooting we found him lying with his
brains blown out. Only the previous day he had
seen a cartoon in Bulletin showing man with gun-
muzzle in mouth, fishing-line attached to trigger,
and bait in water. He said it was a good idea
where there was a waterhole handy.—J.W.

rously offers an explanation of the origin of

“Maginnis”: Re swaggies and tobacco;
met one on Liverpool range once, and, in response
to the usual request for baccy, gave him a hunlc
on my tramp’s plug, whereupon, taking a huge
wad out of his hollow molar, he placed therein the
pjece 1 had given him. Then he wrapped the
dislodged piece in paper and placed that in the
lining of his hat. Next selected another screw of
paper, of which there were several, from same
hiding-place, and filled an old clay with the con-
tents (which had already been chewed). Finally
rolled up the ashes from his pipe and stowed that
away with the rest in his hat. “ Yer see, boss,”
said he ; first I chews it, then I smokes it, and
arter that I snuffs it. That’s perlitical economy,
am tit ? Good-day, boss.”

Mac Merino was coming down from his
station, t’other day, bearing with him a small
bottle containing what he believed to be the eggs
and half-developed specimens of the youthful tick.
Lncountered two frisky commercials on the train
in whom he confided his mission to the nearest
tick-expert. Showed his bottle also. Whisky
was freely imbibed that night by the party, and
the bagmen finally put Mac Merino to bed. That
doughty squatter sent in his bottle of vermin to
the scientist next day, and had the satisfaction of
nndmg a little later that the specialist had adju-
dicated thereupon and pronounced his specimens to
b q common fleas. There’s no joke too mean for a
bagman.

“L.G.W.”; The city takes at least some
care that its residents shall not promiscuously
swallow diseased meat. But in the country 1 Let
me illustrate. _ A beast was “down ” for a week
so they killed it, and, as the uppermost side wasn’t
bruised, it was sent to the house kitchen ; but the
underneath side, which was of a gorgeous purple
was sent to be used in the hut. “At once ! ” was
the order, which was reasonable, for it would not
jjfve kept 24 hours. Why did the men eat the
filth ? Well, when there’s nothing else to eat on
top of a hard day’s graft—what then? Also
when a cancerous cow died—half for the pigs, half
for the hut!
Heard at Glebe Island :
“Ever see ‘Georgie Porgie’
knockin’ about under the piles?
’E won’t look at bait, but he’d take
a bullock or a man if yer could git
a hook big enough. Got a bingy
like a whaleboat, that’s why we
call him Georgie.’ E’s fond of kids
n’ dogs ’n’ muck. I hit him on
th’ snout with a brick one day;
yer could have heard him sneezin’
at Manly. Th’ push makes it will-
in fer him sometimes. Ever meet
Barney Dwyer, th’ flash rider,
from Albury way? ’E got a job
at.the abattoirs lars’ summer. ’E
was a holy terror—red hair, red
eyes, V six-inch spurs. When ’e
was drunk ’e’d leap high V stick
’em inter yer. Fair cow was Bar-
ney. _ (Lars Good Friday we was
waitin’ fer th’ boat ter take us ter
th Pint. Me ’n’ ‘Jonah’Davis
was vvatchin’ old Georgie Porgio
cruisin’ in ’n’ out th’ Blood-hole,
rootin’ like, a pig. Barney was
watchin’ him, too —Barney had
been drinkin’ fer three weeks, fair
balmy ’e was with th’ horrors, ’n’
that thear black fin cuttin’ in ’n’
out th’ Blood - hole seemed ter
amuse him. ’E hopped ter th’
plank end, ’n’ we thought ’e was
goin’ fer a bath. ‘ Come away,
yer fool! ’ says Jonah. ‘ Can’t
yersee thear’s ashark underneath ?’
Barney looked back at us V kicked
up his heels. ‘ Ter hell vvid yez ! ’
he sez. l lsit me that’s afraid av
a white-bellied dog-sthaler ? Hoo-
roo ! ’ Afore Jonah could block

Georgie Porgie was loafin’ right
underneath, ’n’, s’elp me, Barney
plumped right across his back.
Thear was a commotion : Barney
was goin’ up ’n’ down ’sif ’e was
sitting a buck-jumper Th’ fish
didn’t know what ter nmne of it;
’e cut here ’n’ thear floggin’ like
a stuck whale. Barney had him
by th’ gills, ’n’, strike me fat, ’e
was givin’ him th’ spurs ter th’
tune of ‘O’Donnell Aboo.’ Yer
never see sich a corroboree : round
’iT round, up ’n’ down, ’sif all
the water was bein’ whipped outer
tli’ harbor. Then th’ fish got an
idea’n’started fer th’Heads. We
got sight of Barney’s head rushin’
along at 20 knots. ‘ ’E’s a goner,’
sez Jonah, ‘ifth’ shark happens
ter turn.’ Barney was too good a
rider ter fall off a fish. Pity ’e
hadn’t a bridle in his hand when
, e went over. Th’ boss sent a launch ter hunt
em up, but nobody seed Barney again ’till
lars week. Then th’ boss met him at Clontarf
a-nghtm three butcher blokes on the beach.
said afterwards that th’ shark rubbed him off
against North Head. Barney’s a terrible liar,
anyhow, but ’e was a-fightin’ three butcher blokes
at Clontarf.”
* * * *
Two young fellows were out shooting
lately down the N.S.W. South Coast, and about
dusk discovered a bees’-nest in a very big tree. As
it was on private property, they waited till dark
before setting to work to fall it. All night they
pegged away laboriously, and a little after day-
break, just when they were able to see the bees
buzzing in and out of the nest, the tree fell. The
reward for their night’s labor was a very dead
possum and an attendant swarm of blowflies !
Neither of them will associate now with a fool of a
cove that doesn’t know a bee from a blowfly.

“ D.” : The ram is a cute little thing. We
had one. I’ve mentioned him before. We also
had a Timor stallion who spent some pleasant
moments, when time hung heavy on his shoulders,
in annoying the ram by giving him a gentle run
round the paddock for exercise. The ram was fat
and disliked running, so one day, while the pony
vvas grazing with his hindquarters to the ram,
the latter put his head down, charged, and broke
tlm pony’s hind leg just above the fetlock. He
had to be shot, and after that the ram never
moved out of a walk.

to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S.W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Bewareof imitations. Only address:
Senior, chemist, 246 George-street Sydney.*

When Croydon (N.Q.) was passing through
the usual experiences of the out-of-the-way, over-
rusted goldfield—typhoid fever and ague, lashin’s
of grog, and no flour—a gentleman who had put
in pegs in that country from which they are never
shifted had been duly nailed up. When his health
had been drunk several times he was dumped into
a spring-cart, and the procession, consisting of a
goodly array of horsemen, started for a spot a
couple of miles away that had been fixed upon
for the planting. A good-sized chunk of the tail
of the procesh stopped several times while getting
through the town to refresh itself, galloping after
the corpse after each halt. Two of the tail were
followed by their respective kangaroo dogs, and a
discussion arose as to the merits of the canines,
ending in the making of a match for <£s. Both
dogs to be sighted on a kangaroo, and the puller-
down to win ; to be decided on the following
Sunday. A little further on the horse attached
to the temporary hearse, proceeding at the ortho-
dox funereal pace, walked clean over the top of a
wallaby, and the marsupial, gathering himself
together, sprinted for the top of the ridge. “There
you are,” shouted Bill, “ have it out now.”
“ Right,” said Jim ; “ sool him, Spieler! ” “Sool
him, Fairy ! ” yelled Bill, and the dog and slut
started in a fair run, with the refreshed portion
of the procession full pelt after them. The staider
portion near the cart stood it until the wallaby
disappeared over the ridge, then, to a man, they
broke away and followed full cry. Now the driver
of the cart was only human, and a somewhat
whiskied human at that, so, with a cry of “old
Tom” —meaning the corpse —“and me must be
in this ! ” he wheeled the horse for the ridge and
flogged him into a gallop, adjuring his defunct
companion as he went “not to fall out of the
b’anky cart!” But when the cart arrived at
the top of the ridge, and the driver was franti-
cally offering 5 to 1 on Spieler (apparently to
“ old Tom ”), the rest of the procession with most
of its head down was returning, having failed to
kill. Then a very dejected funeral went silently
upon its way.

Ed. Bulletin.—Kangaroo never touches
the ground with his tail when travelling. The
thump heard is the sound of his landing. Uses
his tail to move along when feeding; also to sit
on when standing up. —Chump.
» * • *
S.C. : In the old coaching days on the
Dunstan (M.L.), both passengers and their be-
longings were weighed. Sometimes the tariff was
as high as Is. a lb., so that stores on the diggings
used to fetch fabulous prices. Passengers’ packs
would be weighed first (there was never any furni-
ture ; that used to be made out of anything they
could find when they got their tents up). Next
the parents, then the children ; if the latter were
small they used to stand them on the scale two or
three at a time. Once a lady, under the impres-
sion that only goods were weighed, smuggled two
bathbricks (for knife-cleaning) into her pockets,
but they weighed her as she stood, and, just as the
weights were being adjusted, she sadly brought
out and flung down one brick at a time amidst
much laughter.

He was a wrinkled old whaler with two
solitary teeth, long and yellow, and ho smoked a
short clay pipe with many pieces of ancient rag
wound round the stem. Also he had a tendency
to reminisce. “Yers,” he said between his
puffs, “things is a bit dry about ’ere, but yer
should jest ’er seed it in ’Bl. When I come along
’ere then there wusn’t a bit ’er veggertation to be
seen, and yer could ’er flogged a flea from Con-
dobolin to the Mitchell and never lost sight of
’im wunce. Why ! many a time on this ’ere place
I’ve seen a ’orse lying beside a little blade of
grass watchin’ and’ waitin’ for it to grow. In
those days the stock uster eat all the post-and-rail
fences ” he was continuing as we disappeared
round the corner of the stable and made for
safety. Ten minutes later we reconnoitred care-
fully, and he was still pulling thoughtfully at his
nose-warmer and telling lies about the bad old
days.

“Gordon Downs”: Be blacks’ corpse-
planting customs. On Peak Downs, the small
“ Combo Combo ” tribe always smoothed the top
of the grave, and watched it carefully for tracks—
saying the deceased spirit had gone away in the
shape of the animal whose track was first seen on
the grave. In those days (’77 to ’80) it was mostly
wallaby. One night, hearing of a “planting,” I
went down and invented a track—a cross between
a kangaroo’s and an eagle’s. The old gin came
in the morning to look, and one glance was
enough —in ten minutes the tribe was on the
travel. I asked why, and was told “ Debbil-debbil
sit down.”
“Long-’Un”: Met a hard case
on the West Coast some time back; son
of a lately-deceased and wealthy squat-
ter ; educated in England, and sent out
here for experience. Inherited J 21300 a
year ; bought camels and whips of whisky
and started exploring and prospecting a
long way inland. Breaking-in a young
camel, the latter got him down, and
broke his leg above the ankle in two
places. Taken to nearest station ;
manager away. Rouseabout set his leg—
one bone all right, other one overlapping,
making one leg shorter than other. Drank
all the whisky in the place. Got up in
a fortnight, and rode his camel into the
Bay (200 miles) ; got a couple of cases of
“Scotch,” and then journeyed 180 miles
to an old friend’s station. The “ boss ”
was away, so the “ missus” took charge
of the D.O.L. after he’d finished one
case, at the rate of two bottles a day.
He soon worked out the claim, how-
ever ; then got his camel brought in,
and rode into the Bay to order stores for
another expedition. Spent .£5O at the
pub. in three days ; then they packed him
off by water to town, and hospital, to
have the bones re-set. He polished off
all the whisky and beer on board before
half the voyage was done. The leg was
never re-set, and now he’s back in his old
haunts with his old pals —the camels and
the whisky!

A selector was startled, the other
day, as he was ploughing, by a young
Irish woman—a neighbor’s wife and fresh
from the Ould Sod —jumping over the
fence and rushing frantically towards
him. “ Save me ! save me ! Sure the
devil’s after me for marrying a Protes-
tant.” When the astounded cocky went
to investigate he found a monstrous
“gohanna” in possession of the family
hearth.
The craving for a “ stiddy job ” is not a
common Australian characteristic, but it stood
between Hannibal Rowe and a fortune at Fryer’s
Creek, near Castlemaine (Vic.), about 20 years
ago. Rowe was one of four brothers, who opened
up a claim on the creek, and took out so much
gold in a quiet way that, as soon as some city
mining men got wind of the thing, they came
down with an offer of <£30,000. The three other
Rowes —firm believers in Bayley’s, at that time,
unspoken maxim, “Never break a good face ”
agreed to the bargain at once, but Hannibal held
off with the question, “ Whac’s to become of jne,
then ? ” In vain it was pointed out that £7500
would produce an income for life. “No fear,”
said Hannibal, “ you city sharps don’t get round
me that way. I haven’t got a trade, and I can’t
do anything else much but drive the cart from
the mine to the mill, and, if you take that away
from me, what’s to become of me, I want to
know ? ” That syndicate spent exactly six weeks

The Goondiwindi (Q.) Chows do a flourish-
ing trade with the Boggabilla folks (N.S.W.) in
cabbagee. T’other day a celestial cart was cross-
ing the bridge that leads into the N.S.W. hamlet.
Tick-inspector vociferates : “ Grease your horse,
John ! ” John retires, compounds a gruesome
mixture of fat and kerosene, gives “horsee” a
dab on the knee and a splash on the forehead,
yells out “All litee ” and whips up his steed. In-
spector objects again, and John gives two more
dabs and essays to cross once more with the cus-
tomary “ All litee.” Inspector objects a third
time, when Pigtail turns round his cart and goes
sulkily home with the surly comment —“ Inspector
allee same logue ! ” The Chow looks upon
greasing and dipping merely as a charm—he seems
to have no idea of the scientific import.

short fore-paws, long ears, bluish-grey fur, long
and pointed snout, and long claws and tail.

Bard Tomlinson, of Auckland, has erupted
with a Gladstone “poem” of such resplendence
that Tub-Bulletin regrets want of space to repro-
duce in unshorn luxuriance the whole of this
gorgeous addition to Australian literature. A
gem or two, however ;
Probably the news spread quickly all around,
That a'man-child had been born some distance from
“ Plymouth Sound,”
He was not exactly born in a stable or cradled in a
manger,
But for several years he was nursed and kept out of
danger.
However, the child grew on and on,
Until he could put his own clothes on.
Thy next step was to the Eton College,
Where thou didst pick up all sorts of knowledge,
Then thou didst go through the Oxford University,
To fit thee for life both in prosperity or adversity.
And years after thou hadst put away the slate and Noah’s
Ark,
Thou wast first returned as member for Newark.
While some of the Tory members might be called ignora-
mous,
And they thought they could do just as they please,
But no ; with the Axe of thy silvery voice,
The Liberal members in the House of Commons did often
rejoice,
As thou didst cut down some of the old stubborn trees of
Tory fame,
Then thou didst begin to get thyself a great name.
One great measure was the Disestablishment of the Irish
Church,
When a few of thy followers on this Act left thee in the
lurch.
But most of the Tories belonging to the Old School,
Arrived at the conclusion that you were an old fool.
One other mistake some of us are sure thou hast made,
Although thou didst encourage the people to go in for jam
and marmalade,
The mistake we allude to was granting the grocers a
license,
In this might have been exercised far more common
sense.
Instead of the wives ordering tea in packets or a tin,
Tea was put down on the bill, but the grocer sent home a
bottle of gin.
Thy funeral was witnessed by thousands of people,
As they heard the bells toll from many a church steeple,
The crowd was vast and great in the old Westminster
Abbey,
In which were all sorts of people, both well dressed and
shabby,
And none felt thy loss more than the Queen, or the Old
London cabby,
As you see him weather-beaten, and all fat and flabby.
Into the House of Lords thou hast often been ushered in
by the Black Rod,
Thy spirit has now been ushered into the presence of
Almighty God.
Thy name which was known as “ William Ewart Glad-
stone,”
Will be carved upon many a block of wood or stone.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Want at Wool-
lalira : .
A lie which was half the truth was the blankest he ot
all These gentlemen had said he made certain speeches.
So he did They cut certain parts of his speedties out ,
but they had not the candor and fairness to tell the
people that in every one of those speeches made in con
neetion with these gimcrack proposals he pointed out i
the same breath that he was m favor of a fair and honest
Federation. That was their story about him an
anti-Federalist.
Just so ! Certainly ! And here, in his famous
speech in N.S.W. Legislative Council, is exactly
how Mr. Want pointed it out, and his own defi-
nition of a “ fair and honest Federation .
I think it only right I should say that, as one of the
recognised opponents—l might almost say the arch-
destroying angel— of Federation, I feel deeply indebted
to the hon. and learned member . . . these wronv-think-
in<>- enthusiasts who are trying to force Federation down
our throats. ... I, as well as others, had always pro-
nounced myself an anti-Federationist pure and simple.
... I, who oppose Federation. ... He will find a
Federation of those who, having been enemies before,
will now stand shoulder to shoulder and fight, side by
side to brush away this interloper on our Constitution, to
destroy this fashionable vermin, and to leave this country
untrammelled, unfettered, free and independent, and far
above all these fal-fals of Federation which, by its proper
name, ought to be called “faderation.” . . . The greatest
intellects of this colony have always opposed measures of
this kind. . . . Now we see it (Federation) in all its nasti-
ness. ... If we could get a Federation t n which we
SHOULD HAVE THE PREPONDERATING VOICE, IN WHICH WE
SHOULD HAVE THE CONTROL OF THE REVENUES AND RAILWAYS,
AND THE CONTROL OF THE PARLIAMENTS OF THE OTHER COLO-
NIES, I WOULD be A Federationist. . . . Instead of one
flag, it will be one folly ; instead of one people, it will be
one poorhouse ; instead of one destiny, it will be one
damnation for the whole of this colony. ... Is there any-
thing in this question of Federation more than a mere
fad ? Is there anything in it but vain glory, bombast, and
trumpeting?
Jack Want’s idea of a; “ fair and honest Fede-
ration ” is only another version of the Southern
planter’s idea of Liberty—the right of every
man to wallop his own nigger.

It is instructive to attend a police-court
sittings in any of the piebald districts of Banana-
land. Two-thirds at least of the cases in dispute
concern Cingalese, kanakas, Chinese, Syrians, or
hybrids. In witness whereof also John Don- las
testifies thus re his little dominion : “ Population
of Thursday Island, 1287 ; police-court cases for
the year, 354; 293.” Just think!
Nearly a third of the population charged with
law-breaking. The Thursday alien hasn’t even
the poor excuse of drink, for Douglas testifies that
the local Jap., though fond of beer, rarely drinks
to excess. Temperance cranks might note this.
Ed. Bulletin. —It seems to me the
N.S.W. Public Service Board doesn't even de-
serve the faint praise you award it for routing
“ the drink push.” Many drunkards have gone,
but they were mostly 3d.-bar boozers with no
friends. When the trinity was going through one
department, a high official was locked in his room
for three days with enough grog to kill any intru-
sive snakes, and the known tipplers are still to be
reckoned by the score. The Treasury (Reid’s
dept.) has been almost untouched ; so too with
what has always been looked on as an asylum for
incapables, the Lands, where a high personage
is rumored to have said “ hands off! ” If the
P.S.B. is not a political engine, how comes it that
a list of increases, to shortly take effect, and wear-
ing a hastily-considered appearance, is published
a couple of days after the dissolution of Parlia-
ment ? And has not Reid been dangling this in-
crease before C.S’s. for weeks ? Also, as the
P.B.S. is supposed to go “on its own,” and to
make increases or deductions in the interests of
the public, how does Reid’s boasted finger-in-the
pie get there ?—Malle.
The Victorian Government seems on the
verge of making a very bad mess over the pro-
posed narrow-gauge railways. Norrow lines are
cheaper to build and cheaper to work—in theory,
anyhow. But if a lot of cheap, narrow-gauge ex-
tensions are built on to the existing broad lines,
and these extensions have no communication with
each other, each will require its own engines and
rolling-stock. Each set of engines and rolling-
stock will be stranded on its own little branch,
and will have to stay there all its life ; it can’t be
moved to another narrow-gauge line if wanted,
because of the stretch of broad-gauge railway in
between. It will have to remain where it is,
whether there is work for it or not; and it will
have to be repaired there, because it can’t run
over the broad-gauge line to the regular repairing-
shop. And the loading of goods on to the narrow-
gauge trucks, and the unloading at the junction,
and the re-loading on to the broad-gauge rolling-
stock, will mean expense and delay and aggrava-
tion, and squashed fruit, and murdered cattle, and
produce ruined by exposure and handling, and all
manner of other troubles.
Hobarb Mercury lifts up its wneezy voice
in defence of Wm. M‘Guinea’s gift to Somers
Vine and co. :
If Sir William had desired to aggrandise himself out of
native land dealings he need not have gone from Fiji to
do it, for he had all the reins in his power there. Yet he
protected the Fijians and their interests, as he has since
protected the Papuans and theirs, and his countenance
of a scheme for the expenditure of a large sum of British
capital on the island and the influx of suitable population
there is indicative of its suitableness to the surroundings.
Sir William will not. have changed his skin, though he
mav have changed his spots, and though the merchants
of Brisbane and elsewhere may see interlopers on their
supposed Tom Tiddler's Ground, in the capitalists Sir
William proposes to introduce into the island, they should
not forget that it was their pettifogging proposals which
have made the Administrator keep them at arm’s-length,
in hopes of something more favorable in its scope turning
up. And that more favorable offer he seems now to
have found to his own mature satisfaction, at any rate.
“Indicative of its suitableness to the surround-
ings,” isn’t too bad. “ Something more favorable
in its scope ”is better. M‘Guinea’s “own mature
satisfaction” is distinctly best.

The Girl Missionary in China.
A London paper publishes the squalid, sordid
truth about the girl fanatic who goes to spread
her alleged gospel in Cliow-land :
From the first moment at which I ever gave the matter
serious thought, I had grave doubts as to the advisability
of sending single women and girls to China as missionaries.
To spread truth and civilisation, to inculcate sanitary
principles of both mind and body, is, even apart from
religious considerations, in the ideal a noble work. But
that women are fitting instruments for it, and more es-
pecially for pioneer work in the East, has always seemed
to me to be open to very serious question. Now that I
have had some practical experience, and many oppor-
tunities of learning the experience of others who have
been decades in the East, all my doubts have vanished
like mist before a gale. lam satisfied that to send single
women and delicate girls to do pioneer work in China is a
hideous sin, and those who send them are either densely
ignorant or fanatics. To grasp something of the awful-
ness of the position of a woman or girl so sent out here it
is necessary to form some idea of the untutored wayback
Chinaman’s modes of thought. In the first place, woman
holds no very high place in his respect or social economy'.
She is respected only in her child-hearing capacity, and un-
til she has produced a son she is of little account. Secondly,
a woman with natural, unbound feet is practically' in-
variably to him of low moral character —generally, in-
deed, of no moral character whatever. We cannot blink
the fact that many of our girls’ feet show no signs of
having been bound in their early youth. The coolie
naturally draws his deductions from his own erroneous
premises. Thirdly, the Chinaman, not unlike the law of
England, assumes that, given opportunity, immorality
may be at any rate suspected. When he sees missionaries
of both sexes bundle higgledy-piggledy together into a
native house-boat or junk, and travel so for perhaps
weeks together, he forms his own opinion of their rela-
tions. Under these conditions, what value can the teach-
ings of either the men or women possibly have? None—
or worse than none, I think !
* * * *
I will describe sketchily the general experiences of a
girl coming out for pioneer work on, say, the Yangtsze.
She lands in Shanghai, fresh from home, with roses on
her cheeks, a heart full of enthusiasm and determination
to do good, clean clothes, spotless linen and a skin pure,
clean and soft as velvet. At Shanghai she is put into a
bastard Chinese dress—the upper half correct, and the
lower half of the country from which she has come. She
is then taken on a river steamer, and placed in a room in
the Chinese portion of the boat. The match-board parti-
tions around the room are very' roughly put together, and
afford inadequate privacy, and practically no protection
against the fumes of opium and vile tobacco being smoked
by hundreds of men all around her. Here she begins her
first and mildest experience of the life she is going"to lead.
At first the opium and tobacco fumes cause her head to
ache violently, and make her sick and miserable. Before
long she finds the room alive with vermin of all kinds.
She is soon covered with lice from head to toe. Very
probably before she leaves the steamer her clean, pure
skin will be roughened and abrased with loathsome dis-
ease, and her bright eyes dimmed with ophthalmia.
Luckily she does not yet know enough of Chinese to un-
derstand the foul remarks made about her by' the swarm-
ing almond-eyed celestials on every side. But their
lecherous, leering looks must convey to her mind a feeling
far from pleasant. When she reaches the river port of
debarkation she is probably bundled into a junk or native
house-boat, together with her male and female com-
panions to the number of perhaps half-a-dozen. The
accommodation she will probably find to br scanty for
two and not fit for the proverbial pig. Her journey in
this boat may last days, weeks, or even months. Her
mental anguish at the end of it can be faintly
imagined, but her physical condition is simply too
awful for description tVhen she has reached her
final destination she is l..«st likely planted—literally, for
there is dirt enough in a native house—in the midst of
sanitary conditions that would give a rhinoceros typhoid
fever. And then she commences her great work.' How
utterly unfit for this she .j one can easily imagine, when
one considers her miserable physical condition, debilitated
as it must be by foul stinks, prying vermin, and ill
nourished by unaccustomed and,' perhaps, insufficient
food. Her mental state is, if possible, worse. She has
been brought up to believe that personal cleanliness and
general respect are almost as necessary to a decent life as
morality and religious belief. Of the two former here she
P° sses ses neither. Her personal condition will not bear
thou ffht, and by now she is beginning to realise that the
respect she commands among those she is sent to teach
is something represented by a minus sign. She struggles
? n through experiences that make her heartsick and turn
her hair grey. Often she would drag her festering body
and tortured mind home to rest in despair were it not for
very shame and fear of the finger of scorn that is too apt
to be pointed at the one who turns back from the plough
once grasped. Eventually she dies, and is buried in a far-
away prove, while to her friends at home a glowing ac-
count is sent of the good work she has accomplished. Or,
s ” e return s home a broken woman, consumed with bitter,

tNDIGESTION, BAD STOMACH, RHEUMATISM, AND

BILE BEANS FOR BILIOUSNESS.
THE GREATEST SPECIFIC OF THE PRESENT

The following interview with a Newcastle Herald reporter shows
the value the Australian press place on this remedy :
“They would almost save you from your coffin,’’ said Mrs.
Shergold, of Wilton-street, Merewether, when asked by a New-
castle Herald representative as to the genuineness of her cure from
the use of Bile Beans for Biliousness. “I shall ever be grateful,”
she added, “to the lady who advised me to try them. I had been
suffering for four or five years more than I could describe, and these
Bile Beans have cured me. lam now able to do that which I could
not think of attempting before, and I tell you, from the very bottom
of my heart, that I experienced no permanent relief until I com-
menced taking these Beans.” With this the reporter was asked to
step inside. Then it was that he was convinced of the marvellous
effect which the Bile Beans had upon the lady’s constitution. Mrs.
Shergold told her story in a way that was convincing in itself, and
she had evidently been a sufferer in the strongest sense of the term,
and so acute had grown her complaints that her father had despaired
of her life, and her husband, who is well-known as a laborer at the
Newcastle Company’s B Pit, had become aceu,-turned to finding the
poor woman ill in bed on his returning from a hard day’s work.
Now Mrs. Shergold is a standing memorial of the efficacy of Bile
Beans, and it is a pleasure for her to relate what they have done
for her. Mrs. Shergold has resided in the district for upwards of
10 years—three years in Merewether, two years in Adamstown, and
four years in Dudley, the rest of the time being spent in various
places. She is also well-known at Clarencetown, where she was
born in 1855, her father being now at Port Stephens. He, too, has,
she says, been able to enjoy life by having resorted to the use of
Bile Bean 3 for Biliousness. After realising the lady’s genuine
gratitude, the newspaper-man made inquiries into the nature of her
case, and, to use her own words, she said : “ I had been bad off and
on for nearly five years, suffering from general weakness and a bad
stomach, and for 19 years I had suffered with my head. A little
over a year ago I consulted a doctor, and he gave mo a prescription
for indigestion. This gave me no permanent relief. Then I tried
another doctor in Maitland, who said I had rheumatics in the
head. He, too, failed to do me any real good, and as month suc-
ceeded month I grew worse, and the more I tried to rest the more
tired I became. My nights were a perfect misery to me, for the
want of sleep, and when I got up in the morning I could not eat,
and on one occasion I ate next to nothing for a whole week, and
never could I take a breakfast until very late in the day, my stomach
always feeling sick when I first got up, and many a time my husband
has come home and had to prepare his own meals. I got so bad
eventually that it was not only a difficulty for me to walk, but it
was painful to even bow my head.” The newspaper-man could
hardly imagine that the lady had been such a sufferer, for to see her
to-day she looks the picture of health for one of her years. He
ventured to inquire if Bile Beans had worked this wonderful change
in her constitution, and then it was that the wholesale sincerity
of the patient was manifested. “ I commenced,” she said, “ taking
the Beans about eight months ago, at the recommendation of a lady
friend of mine. I felt a relief after the first couple of doses, and in
a short time I felt completely well: so well, in fact, that I could do
as much washing in one day as I could before in three days, and all
those disagreeable sicknesses in iny stomach left me, and those ex-
cruciating pains in my head disappeared also. I can now eat and
sleep splendidly, and it is a pleasure for me to take a walk out. My
father, Mr. Timothy Foley, of Port Stephens, who used to live a
life of pain and misery, and my husband, who suffered severely
from an internal complaint, are now quite as grateful to the makers
of Bile Beans as I am. Yes,” added Mrs. Shergold, in reply to
another question, “you are at perfect liberty to publish my testi-
mony. It’s the turuth I have told you, and while I tell the truth I
don’t care if it is placarded all over Merewether.” Mrs. Shergold
mentioned the names of many other poor sufferers who are now
grateful for having taken Bile Beans. These cases will be inquired
into. Meantime, anyone who is in doubt about the wonderful re-
cuperative power of the Beans should avail themselves of a personal
conversation with the subject of this interview.

SEVERE SUFFERING FOR SEVEN YEARS.
DOCTORS UNABLE TO HELP HIM.
A FEW BOXES OF BILE BEANS COMPLETELY CURE HIM.
Mr. Harry P. Stewart, the well-known pictorial artist and signwriter, of No. 77 Elizabeth-street,
City, has a few words to say in praise of Bile Beans for Biliousness. Although a very
busy man, Mr. Stewart was glad to make time in which to give a special reporter particulars of his
case. We will let him speak for himself “ Seven or eight years ago, while I was living in Victoria,
I commenced to suffer severely from frequent bilious attacks. They came periodically about every
three or four weeks, and would often keep me in bed for two or three days at a time, much to my an-
noyance and the detriment of my business. These attacks were always accompanied by the usual
symptoms and effects of deranged liver. Violent headaches gave me no end of trouble. I was unable
to sleep, and my appetite was very poor. I would get up in the morning after getting little or no sleep
during the night and be utterly unable to partake of my breakfast. At lunch time I might be able to
take a little food, but in the evening I never had any inclination for dinner. While in Melbourne I
was under two first-class physicians, and while they did me some temporary relief the biliousness re-
turned shortly after, and I found myself in a worse state than ever. About this time I came to Syd-
ney, and instead of getting better, as I hoped, I continued to grow weaker and weaker every day. The
headaches became severe, especially in the back part of my head, and my heart became affected ; this
latter, I believe, was through an enlargement of the liver. My heart used to flutter in an alarming
manner, and I was greatly troubled with shortness of breath.
I became run-down and altogether dispirited ; life seemed a
burden to me, and my business suffered accordingly.
“ Now, about Bile Beans. About four months ago I, hav-
ing noticed Bile Beans pretty well advertised, thought I would
try a box just as an experiment. I went religiously through
the first box, and was quite surprised to find what a lot of good
they had done me. I continued to take them, and they have
completely cured me. I don’t look much like a bilious subject
now, do I ? I’ve taken about seven boxes all told, and there
has been a decided improvement right along. My appetite is
exceedingly good, and I can sleep like a top—why, I slept this
morning until half-past eight. -There is no doubt that Bile
Beans have completely cured me, and I have no objection to
your publishing these facts as you see fit.”
