EflnS
FOR
INFLUENZA AND COLDS,
Influenza is not generally understood by the greater part of those who suffer from it. It is in
reality an aggravated form of cold, supplemented by a general weakness of the whole system. _ Ordi-
nary colds, it is well-kuown, are caused by the congestion of the blood, which, as a rule, is most
prominent in the head or throat. Influenza can be traced to the same cause, and irregular or insuf-
ficient liver action is, in most cases, responsible for both. When the liver, through any derangement
of the digestive organism, ceases to act in the proper manner, a certain amount of bile finds its way
into the blood, and, at the same time, the blood created from food consumed is of a decidedly poor
quality. In this manner, the blood becomes congested, the circulation sluggish, and a cold results.
The circulation of the blood now being much slower than usual, the entire system is affected. The
nervous organism becomes unstrung, the appetite poor, and the rest disturbed. The body is extremely
susceptible to chills, and the slightest draught causes a violent attack of coughing or sneezing. Many
so-called cures for influenza fail to get at the root of the trouble, and only afford temporary relief. A
medicine that will keep the liver in thorough and regular working order, and the digestion right, will,
without doubt, prove the most effective preventive and cure for influenza and colds. This is what
Bile Beans for Biliousness will do, and we maintain that no better remedy can be found for these
ailments.

POLITICAL
POINTS
How comes it, The Bulletin wants to
know, that no N.S.W. politician—Parkes, for in-
stance—has ever thought of fixing a general-
election for the glorious Twelfth of July ?
Premier Kingston has promised the As-
sembly that if it passes the Bill for the railway to
Innamincka (Queensland border) he will agree to
the repeal of the stock-tax on the day that it is
opened.
Should Sammy Lees bo beaten in his
attempt to enter N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
he will be re-appointed to his Upper House seat.
If Barton or R. E. O'Connor be defeated will
Premier Reid send them up again?
O'Malley, M.P., asserts that S.A. officials
summarily dismiss workmen who dare to express
obnoxious views on political questions. The
Labor-membei-s cheered this statement, but Pre-
mier Kingston declares that it is absolutely un-
true.
The pension allowances paid by N.S.W.
Govt, last year amount to .£137,743 ss. Bd. In the
year ended June, ’97, two pensions—Williams’
£ll3 6s. Bd., and Goodchap’s .£937 14s.—fell in,
and one—the Parkes Family Grant £5O0 —was
entered up.
What on earth doe' Premier Reid care
about majority rule, anyhow, when he is going to
preserve the N.S W. Nominee Council ? So long
as the N.S.W. elector is only allowed to choose
half of his own Parliament, there can be no
majority rule.
In Yic. Assembly, the other night, a peti-
tion was presented from John Robertson, Moonee
Ponds, praying the House to investigate his dis-
covery of a “general and exact scientific theory
of Right, and a natural standard of value.’’
Which was about the largest order that House
ever met with.
Q. Premier Byrnes : “ The object of the
annexation of Papua was to take possession of a
country very like Australia for purposes of settle-
ment.” Which goes to prove that a man need not
know much about geography, geology, or natural
history generally to become a shining light in
Australian politics.
Ex-Labor membei, the Rev. Smailes, when
offering himself as a politician to Granville,
N.S.W., declared that he felt a divine call to
politics as he had felt years before to preaching.
Now he steps out of the political uorld feeling,
and receiving all-round corroboration, that his call
to politics was a hollow hoax.
The N.S.W. Governor and his private
secretary are the only persons outside the Govt,
departments permitted to frank all their letters
through the post. This was a little privilege
granted by the Reid Executive to the heads of
Government House in ’95, and not notified at the
time either to Parliament or the press.
Edwin Lilley, - son of Queensland’s old
Democratic chief, Charles Lilley, is going to run
for Oxley in the Democratic interest. The name
of Lilley is something to conjure with even now,
and if son Edwin has the grit and brains of his
father, or even a little of them, he may yet prove to
be the leader that the Queensland Democracy has
so long waited for.
Reid and Barton have already met as rivals
at the ballot-box in the electorate of East Sydney.
In the first instance Reid won by 96 votes ; in the
second Barton won by 500 and in the third by 700.
These were the days when East Sydney returned
four members. In the Referendum ballot when
all N.S.W. went to the poll Barton again tri-
umphed by thousands.
Opposition-leader John Downer (S.A.)
says he doesn’t care whether or not he conies back
to politics again after the general elections, as he
has lost all the ambitions which once burned in
his breast. With a fine show of bowing cheerfully
to the inevitable, he exclaimed : —“ I would
sooner Mr. Kingston had the troubles of the Pre-
miership than I just at present! ”
Melb. Trades Hall Council proposes to
frame a political programme and organisation to
take the place of the practically defunct Labor-
party. Better hasten slowly; for a hostile Minister
of Lands inclined to read the trust-deed of the
Trades Hall land strictly is quite a possibility of
the future, with serious consequences to the
present trustees in certain events.
At Premier Reid’s Protestant Hall (Syd.)
meeting was an interjector who was able exactly
to reproduce George's unmelodious croak, and
who, every time the fat man paused for breath,
went instantly ahead with the speech in such
fashion that the audience was bewildered into a
dreadful uncertainty as to when the real Reid was
orating and when the sham. The chucking-out
was fast and furious, but Georgius Secundus was
about the last to be fired—in company with the
pleasing consciousness that he had done more to
break the Wriggler up than all the rest of the
enemy put together.
S.A. Oppositionists were badly beaten over
their no - confidence amendments. Downer’s
amendment was rejected by 19 votes, and Glynn
was in a minority by 21. When the amendment
by Glynn was first tabled there was a chance,
although remote, of a fusion with the Laborists,
but by the time the vote was taken the Opposi-
tionists were completely disorganised owing to
internal quarrels. The Kingston Ministry emerges
from the crisis stronger than ever. From the start
Premier Kingston absolutely declined to do any-
thing to propitiate the deserting Laborites, who
now begin to kick themselves.
Premier Reid’s little scheme to nobble
N.S.W. Public Service vote by yearly increments,
has only increased the Service’s resentment to Mr.
No-Yes ; more especially since he announces that
the general, clerical and professional divisions are
to share alike. A certain outcome of the scheme,
which no one believes is the Board’s own idea, will
be a return to absolute patronage for the perman-
ent heads and so indirectly to Parliamentary
influence. Under the scheme part of. the
Service will increase .£lO per annum, and to
counteract the annual increase it is feared that
promotion in any other direction will be abso-
lutely stopped. Anyway, the rank-and-file of the
civil-servants are currently supposed to be thirst-
ing for revenge on July 27.
The local road-and-bridge interest is being
urged to rise as one culvert against R. E.
O’Gonnor in (N.S.W.) electorate. The
four things most strongly urged against the Aus-
tralian-born Dick O’Connor are that he is an
Irish Roman Catholic, a disguised Orangeman
and a Freemason, and that he won’t snatch publio
money for the electorate as the old member, J. O.
Watson, does. If Dick O'Connor, who is as
straight as he is able and patriotic, does go down,
it will be the culvert that will do it. And thereby
Young will show itself about the last possibility
in the way of a mule. If his party comes on top
Dick O’Connor will be second man in the Minis-
try, and will have more bridges and culverts and
roads at his command than full private Watson
ever heard of.
The following circular, issued to Reidito
candidates, and with N.S.W. Premier Reid’s sig-
nature attached, is interesting when compared
with the Premier’s lofty and independent state-
ment which precedes it. It will be noticed that
each candidate is to write his own biography and
state in full what a noble and brainy and
patriotic character he is, and how the people
unanimously called on him to come forward and
save the country, and these things are to
be circulated at the taxpayers’ expense
under the N.S.W. free-newspaper-postage sys-
tem ! The value of these biographies, when
they appear, can therefore be fully estimated.
Also, the biographies don’t prove, as they may
seem to do, that the fame of the bush candidate
lias spread to Sydney—only that the candidate is
being pauperised by a gratis distribution of litera-
ture :

“ But I am not going to be the puppet of any
journal in the world,” declared Premier Reid at a
recent Sydney public meeting.
The stars have been fighting for Premier
Dick Seddon of late. After the downfall of ex-
Treas. Ward, King Dick’s winner, declined awhile,
and the Opposition talked joyfully of his approach-
ing downfall. Then Provi-
dence, or luck, or the cus-
sedness of inanimate ob-
jects. took a hand,
and smote the Op-
position. Its leader
was led away to
England on ur-
gent private bu-
siness. Stout
retired. His
successor, Dut-
hie, also had to
hustle off to Eu-
rope on busi-
ness. Good old
“Dismal Bill”
Rolleston, act-
ing-leaderof the
Opposition, fell
off a horse and
waslaidup. An-
other member
of the party
followed Duthie
and Russell to
Europe, and is
detained there
by illness in his
family. The
Tory member
for Mataura
went broke, and
his seat was
snatched by a
strong Ministe-
rialist, M’Nab. And,
finally, a vehicle ran into
the Government’s painfully can-
did friend, Pirani, who is more trouble-
some than the worst avowed Oppositionist,
and he is also in bed.
North Brisbane is a-weary of the two un-
speakable nobodies she elected, chiefly in a fit of
bigotry, at last election. Fraser, in particular,
has become a burden to his constituents. Dull in
the House, and duller out of it, he’s a shocking
reflection on the folks who put him in. Byrnes
himself would desperately like to win the premier
constituency again, could he only yoke himself in
with a popular partner.
The result of recent Queensland bye-elec-
tions should be sufficient to convince the Labor-
party wire-pullers that success lies in leaving
candidates an absolutely free leg, unhobbled by
any platform-signing or other superfluities. Jen-
kinson, who two months ago won Wide Bay from
the Govt., and Ryott Maughan, who did ditto
with Burnett, were distinctly running as Inde-
pendent Labor-men. In fact, neither was endorsed
by the Central Executive, and neither signed the
platform. It seems clear that capable men run-
ning as Democrats with Labor support have a far
bigger chance of ejecting the Govt, than undiluted
Labor apostles, who, being mostly fire eaters of
street-corner notoriety, disgust the philosophical
Radical.
A PROMINENT CENTRE-FORWARD.
(Football note.)
“ S.” : TnE Bulletin is hardly correct in
saying that Queensland Chief Justice Griffith
voted hi 3 salary as such. Oh no 1 Though a
member of the House, he was absent from his seat
when the second debate was in progress. When
the second reading was carried, he returned to his
place on the Ministerial bench, looking blissfully
ignorant of what had been going on. Griffith
was head of the Govt, which passed the measure
adding .£lOOO to the screw, and it was pre-arranged
that he should take the billet.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne, who is
steadily growing a policy, now declares for a
State Land Bank in addition to woman suffrage,
old-age pensions, Local Government and some
kind of Federation. Also, Lyne says that he
wants to put his abundant blucher down on the
system by which women have to work 14 to 16
hours a day while the demand for a legalised eight
hours is mostly reserved for the already better-
treated male toiler. Lyne apparently means well
in this matter, but he has picked a pretty large
grievance for a smooth-bore of his calibre to
tackle.
The Brisbane publican has banged his pint-
pot on the political counter with a loud bang in
view of the next election,
when, the Bung President
says, the publican interest
is to be felt.
HAIR CUT WHILE YOU WAIT.
O’F LANNIGAN: "Oi want me hair cut. Oi’ve to go
down the road an’ see Dooley, but Oi’ll be
back by the toime ye've finished. If Oi’m not
back when ye’ve finished cuttin’ me hair, Oi’ll

Shriek from Sydney Telegraph : “The
men who are out under the Federal Conservative
Protectionist banner are hostile to genuine Upper
House reform.” _ Barton, the head of that party,
says his idea of N.S.W. Council reform is to make
the Council like the proposed Federal Senate (the
proposed Federal Senate was to be a paid institu-
tion, elected by the province, voting as one con-
stituency, on the manhood suffrage basis.) That
apparently isn't genuine Upper House reform !
And the genuine reform of the Reid “ Liberal ”
party (a Liberal party which consists mostly of
Want, Heydon, Salomons, and the bulk of the
present obstinate Tory Council) is to preserve the
unpaid, unelected, unrepresentative Fatman
nominees, merely appointing them for a term in-
stead of for life. God bless the cause of Genuine
Reform!
One of the angry scoffs which Reid threw
at Barton and O’Connor was that, after his first
anti-Billious speech in Sydney Town Hall, they
ran after him into a room to congratulate him.
Barton replies that they didn’t run after the fat
gentleman, but that Reid sent for them and said:
‘‘l made it rather strong against the Bill, hut I
am not so far bound down now that I can’t speak
more favorably of it later on. ”
Noteworthy that in none of his speeches
does Q. Premier Byrnes pay the slightest tribute
to his predecessor, Nelson. The new Premier
prays that the past be forgotten and the future
taken as read. It is now no secret that Nelson
and Byrnes had a violent quarrel in England, and
that if Nelson had had lus way Dickson, and not
Byrnes, would now be at the head of the Cabinet.
But Tomjoe was too strong, threatened a cave—
and conquered.
Labor - member George Black wants to
know: “If Protection is to benefit the workers,
why had the workers of Victoria flocked to N.S.
Wales for a crust ? ” Well, when did they “flock,”
except in the columns of the Shoddy press ? The
only record which suggests any information is the
list of arrivals and departures overland to and
from Vic., Queensland, and S.A., all three pro-
vinces being lumped together. And in the nine
years for which statistics are available N.S.W.
received 503,378 people from these provinces, and
sent them 504,331. By way of a side issue, it is
a curious thing that when the Briton—like George
Black—flies from his country it only shows the
vigor and enterprise and spirit of adventure which
grow up under Freetrado. And if the Victorian
lights out for the goldfields of W.A. it shows that
Protection has miserably driven him out in search
of a crust. Good old crust!
J. C. Watson, who is opposing Dick
O’Connor at Young (N.S.W.), alleges that Bar-
ton, M'Millan, O’Connor, Walker, Wise, and
Abbott voted at Melbourne against the Referen-
dum and the simple majority in case of deadlocks.
But the Referendum and the simple majority in
case of deadlocks were rejected long before that ;
it was at the second Convention in Sydney that
the three-fifths majority was adopted instead of
these. And Reid, Carrnthers, and Drunker voted
for it, while Wise and Abbott didn’t —the vote ia
recorded on page 974 of the Convention Hansard
for 21st September, 1897. Also, it was Car-
ruthers who first suggested the three-fifths
majority. And now J. C. Watson and Car-
ruthers, the inventor of the three-fifths majority,
and Reid and Drunker, who supported it, are cast-
ing the odium of their own allegedly infernal de-
vice at Wise— who didn’t help it with his vote,
anyhow.
Bit of eloquence from N.S.W. Attorney-
General Want :
Mr. Barton has an army under him who are only wait-
ing to sneak in Protection.
The phrase “ to sneak in Protection” came into
N.S.W. politics in the days of th ■ Jennings-Dibbs
Ministry eleven years ago. That Ministry, which
was elected as the old kind of Freetrade or
Revenue Tariff Government, put on 5 per cent, ad
valorem duties, and the Telegraph heaped a curse
and a new phrase on it—it became known as the
Government which “sneaked in Protection.” The
two leading members of that Government were
Dibbs, now helping .Tack Want to wreck Federa-
tion as boss of the N.S.W. anti-Bill league, and
Jack Want himself 7 Barton wasn't in it. There-
fore Want, as the man who sneaked in Protection
(we have the solemn oath of his organ, the Daily
Telegraph, that it was a sneaking-id of Protec-
tion) charges Barton, who never did anything of
the kind, with his own political crime (Want’s
own organ has alleged over and over again on its
immortal oath that it was a crime). The spectacle
of Jack Want, the reliable Freetrader who sneaked
in Protection, heaving that old phrase ».t the above-
board Barton, is very beautiful.
Carruthers, of the N.S.W. God-fearing
Ministry, the author of the three-fifths majority
clause, made this bad break in a recent speech at
Rockdale :
With regard to the three-fifths majoncj, it had been
stated that Mr. Reid voted for the proposal, and now, as
Mr. Bruce Smith alleged, had the effrontery to advocate
a simple majority. He and Mr. Reid voted for Mr. Lyne’s
Referendum, but knew they were in too great a minority.
They had to get the in st. thing they could. Thev failed
to get the Referendum. Then came his (Mr. Oat ruthers’s)
amendment for the three-fifths majority, which Premier
Turner wished to make into a simple majority ; that was
only for the purpose of wrecking the whole proposal, for
the other representatives of South Australia and Tasmania
were entirely against it. The proposal of the three-fifths
clause was in order to obtain the host compromise that
could be obtained among them. They advocated «ow the
simple majority, as they desired then'.
What a beautiful mixture ! When Turner- advo-
cated rule by the bare majority it was “for the
purpose of wrecking the whole proposal ” ; when
Reid and Carruthers advocate the same thing,
it isn’t. The fact that Reid and Carruthers voted
for the three-fifths arrangement shows that they
sincerely wanted the other thing ; the fact that
Turner voted for the other thing shows his utter
want of sincerity. And Reid and Carruthers
didn’t advocate the bare majority because they
knew it was impossible. So presumably they ad-
vocate it now because they know it is impossible.

The coming election in N.S.W., following
directly,on the Federation campaign, should yield
a record in the number of votes polled. The
apathy of the average elector in asserting his
manhood at the polling-booth is one of the most
disheartening features in the government of this
country, What every man seems to need is a
sure and gentle stimulus to thought. If every
man, for instance, smoked “ Our Chief ” tobacco,
he would think with more clearness, coherence
and facility on the potentialities of “ one man one
vote. ”*
Paste it in your hat, and rejoice in the
fact that Australia has instituted sanitary legisla-
tion :
The Ravages of Tuberculosis.— The scarcity of prime
cattle in N.S. Wales suitable for trade-requirements has
the effect of raising values foranything approaching good
beef, and, as a consequence, speculators and others
anxious to thin out their stocks rush them into the mar-
ket. There is always plenty of competition for all sorts
among the various buyers, for the worst classes, provided
they pass the eye of the Government veterinary surgeon.
A case occurred last Thursday where a consignment
consisting of 40 head were yarded at Homebush to
be disposed of by public auction. The inspector,
Mr. Stanley, ordered 10 of these to be destroyed at the
yards, as, in their live state, they showed outward
developments of tuberculosis. The balance were con-
signed to the abattoirs as doubtful, without being offered
for sale, when 18 more on being slaughtered were found
to be suffering from various diseases in advanced forms.
The cattle had been purchased from different dairies by
a speculator, and although the cots were “ dry” the dis-
ease in most cases must have been seated for some time,
and it is hard to estimate how much of the milk has been
circulated.— Sydney Daily Telegraph.
Purchased from dairies by a speculator ! Hor-
rible !

Significant that the Riverine and N.S.W.
Border electorates have only two supporters of
the Reid Govt.—Ball, of Albury; and Lee, of Ten-
terfield ; nor has a Ministerial candidate of any
weight been nominated in any of these con-
stituencies. Most of these seats would be carried
by the Bartonites unopposed, but for the nominees
of the Labor-party. The promises of the Reid-
want Ministry of “ a speedy Federation ” don’t
count for much in the ultra-Federal districts.
THE BOBBY AND THE BATON.
It’s mooted in the lobbies
Whether all the stalwart Bobbies
Will vote for Reid or Barton —
For Barton or for Reid.
For the Reidites say, “ The devil 1
The poll will be so level
That the man they set their heart on
Will likely have a lead.”
But the Bobbies, while patrolling,
Have their optics on the polling,
And they whisper, “ Sure and sartin,
We are all of us agreed.
For we calculate with fervor
That our trusty life-preserver
Is ‘ Baton,’ always ‘ Baton,’
We don’t lean upon a Reid.”

The Swift pacing-teams easily secured the
Queensland records put up by Bill. Walne and
Parsons between them took all the records, from the
half to the twenty-mile point.*
♦ • » *
N.S.W. Wheelmen League’s Road-race last
Saturday ended in a great victory for Swifts, Humbers,
and Dunlop Tyres. J. Elliott, on a Swift, won first place ;
J. Stuart, J. Cliffe, and P. W. Drinkwater, on Humbers,
won second, third, and fourth place ; and R. W. Lewis,
on a Swift, won fastest-time prize. Each machine had
Dunlop TyresJ*
The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin ”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the dep6t, 257 George-street,
Sydney.*
Seventy-three more Japs reached the
Japanese settlement of Thursday Island last week
—including nine “ women.” Q. Leg. Council Pres.
Nelson’s (salary, £lOOO a year) yarn about the
stoppage of immigration is branded fictitious every
Jap mail-boat; and Premier Byrnes, who drank
success to the Jap line of steamers, doesn’t seem
the man to mend things.
Sixteen consuls, most of them representing
European countries, celebrated, by handshakings
and joyous chatter at Biard d’Aunet’s in Sydney
last week, the anniversary of the taking of the
Bastille. Yet, when the Bastille fell, the repre-
sentatives of the monarchies and empires now so
gracious howled execrations or loaded up with
shot and shell to resist the new force.
When they hit Premier Reid with an
ancient egg, the other night (on the similia simili-
bus principle), Jollyanfat, to make the best of the
situation, observed that bad eggs became good in
the course of their flight towards him. Very likely :
even a rotten egg changes its principles, turns its
coat, as it were, in the presence of Wriggler No-
Yes. Wriggling is a contagious disease.
S.A. Labor M’sP. brought one charge
against the Kingston Govt, which was certainly
justified. Premier Kingston appointed the Board
of Health inspector, who is compelled to travel all
over S.A. in the course of his work, to be also
Inspector of Eactories without additional salary.
The result is that the latter work is practically
neglected, and Adelaide employers snap their
fingers at the Act whioh no one has time to
enforce.
Rumors of strange horrors come from the
Queensland lazarette at Peel Island. They are
mostly the story of a girl-patient recently removed
to the Isle of Living Death, and included in her
accusation are stories of brutal language used by
officials to the sufferers under their control,
and of strange orgies and drunks, and
attempted assaults. And when due considera-
tion is given to the utter helplessness of
a leper-girl in her exile, and the strong, riotous
whisky - breath which pervades most public
affairs in Queensland, the charges look quite
enough to call for the usual hollow show of inves-
tigation. They never really investigate any bad
public stench in Queensland, but there are times
when it is advisable to look like doing it.
Apparently, one of the first results of Pre-
mier Reid’s circular inviting the Provincialist
candidates to send in their portraits and
their biographies (written by themselves, if they
like), for insertion in Sydney Daily Telegkaph
(2000 copies of the paper—paid for, pre-
sumably, out of the Patman fund—to be sent
to each candidate), is the upheaval of little
attorney Jones. Solicitor L. C. Russell Jones
is repr< se ited, in his published biography (with
photo), as having contested Petersham in ’94 “and
though there were seven other candidates he polled
as many votes as all his opponents put together.”
Which is howling bosh, for Jones got only 627
votes out of 2477, and his opponents mustered
close on 1200 votes. Then—just listen to this
shriek—“ by special invitation of Mr. Cham-
berlain, he (Jones) attended the Record
Reign celebrations in England, being treated
(Jones was treated) during his (Jones’s) stay
in London as a particularly honored guest ”
(Jones was the honored guest). Apparently it is
intended to be conveyed that Chamberlain speci-
ally sent for little Jones, of Petersham, to come
and adorn the Queen’s Jubilee. Also it is men-
tioned that Jones once met the Prince of Wales,
and that Lord Roseberry once wrote to Jones.
And yet the Reid Government had to distribute
50 pairs of blankets in this tremendous person’s
electorate to help him (Jones, the friend of Joseph
Chamberlain) to get in again. Are the Petersham
voters blackfellows—or what ?

A WORD OF CAUTION to buyers should be offered
In buying a Bicycle, as in buying anything requiring
supreme skill to produce the highest and most endur-
— ing results, remember that the influence of the mass
of makers and agents is enlisted against the leading article of its kind, and
that inducements other than the intrinsic merits of the goods themselves are
fully used to force the sale of a cheaper line. “Under the best apple tree are found
the most sticks and stones.” It makes us smile to hear the pitiful stories circulated by
our friends (?) in the trade, to injure the standing and reputation of RED BIRD Cycles.
Cyclists of common sense are not led astray by false yarns circulated by dealers. One
way to judge a cycle is by its sales. If it sells well against competition, it must be
good. If it doesn’t sell well, it isn’t worth considering. More Red Bird Special,
Red Bird No. 1, and Black Bird Cycles are sold than any three makes put
together in Australia. This says something. Why not send for list of New Models?

Harris-st., Wellington, N.Z. Factories—Brantford, Canada.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Handicapped by the heaviest of going. Floater
won V.R.C. Grand Nat. Steeplechase easily with 12ist.
up—one of the best jumping performances ever regis-
tered at Flemington. The respectable Songster was
placed fourth, other top-weights in Gladstone and Doon-
diah having fallen by the way, whilst Hayseed settled his
chance by clouting one of the fences very hard.
Sturdy Pirate, who had only a “ feather” (9st.), to carry,
was sailing away in front of the field when he struck a
fence, as in the Maiden Steeple the previous Saturday,
and this time the jockey lost his seat. Pirate, seemingly,
was the one that might have beaten Floater if he had
stood up. Good judges, though, took warning by his
mistake in the Maiden event, and it wasn’t the money of
the cognoscenti which made the Ballarat’chaser first
favorite at the start.
Flemington course was so full of water last
Saturday that some people backed Floater for the sake of
his name.
Mud racing is a bad guide to “ form ” under
normal conditions, otherwise the Manifold’s horse, Way-
farer, would look certain to take a big handicap in the
spring. This big, strong bay won the mile July Handi-
cap just as he liked, beating a smart performer
in Bunyan. The victory was impressive, but it is
impossible to say how much the winner may,
or may not, have been favored by the weather. There
is plenty of negative evidence against the brilliancy of any
flat racer that can romp home through a quagmire. Many
of the fastest cattle, from Carbine downwards, have been
unable to act up to their reputations on soft ground—
ergo, the horse that appears to like mud is probably not
among the best of movers when he can hear his hoofs
rattle.
Whernside, the gigantic, failed to hump his
13st. 101 b. burden nearly to the fore in the Open Steeple-
chase, so the V.R.C. meeting passed without one win
(from eight attempts) for the Miller stable. Yet there
have been winter-times when the red-and-white jacket
couldn’t go wrong. The “glorious uncertainty” of the
Turf!
* • * *
The addition of an “ off ” day to the V.R.C.
Winter meeting was not a large individual success, but
the attendance seemed good enough, and this extra day
gave new importance to the Grand National, anyhow. A
feature of the Wednesday racing was the success of two
3 year-old sons of the imported Pilgrim’s Progress, viz.,
Bunyan and Pilgrim. Two smart geldings, these.
■* » * *
“Fogo”: Tom Curran has just died in Melb.
Champion pug. of Australia, in the days when he fought
with such as Joe Kitchen, Mat Hardy, Bob Fee, Black
Harry Sellars, and Jack Carstairs. Bell’s Life in Mel-
bourne, under the hands of Graham Carrick, Dick Hamil-
ton, and others, used to publish the gaudv and classical
accounts of the fights, on the London model, of the
Pierce Egan school.
* » * *
With Tom Curran passed away the last of the
old English pugs who came to Australia to pick up
shekels on the diggings. Curran is one of the few pro-
fessional scrappers who have gone through life without a
single act of brutality or blackguardism being alleged
against him, and when it is considered what a fierce light
beats upon the ring, this leaves Curran with a reputation
like a dove. On Dunolly rush Tom made his first rise and
coined money in the pub. he ran there. It was during
this tenancy that he lost his finger through splitting it on
somebody’s tooth. Old Tom Curran leaves but one son,
Bill, who has appeared in the ring—only to show that the
old man’s mantle had not fallen upon him.
* * * •
Four Melbourne “bookies’ have formed a
syndicate for laying tempting odds against the Caulfield
and Melbourne Cups double—£looo to £1 first and first,
plus the “ place ” contingencies of £3OO for Ist in the
Caulfield and 2nd in the Melbourne Cup, and £2OO for a
Ist and 3rd. This introduction of a Tatt.’s sweep ele-
ment into double-event betting is a desperate attempt
on Ikey Mo’s part to weaken public demand for the Tote.
But the machine is bound to come, all the same. A lime-
light of suspicion will be turned upon certain members
of the anti-Tote crowd in the Vic. Assembly when Jack
Murray’s little Bill is next debated (in August), and some
of them, finding themselves under close observation, will
probably withdraw their support from dear old friend I.
Moses.
By the way, Tatt. is at last going to try the ex-
periment of drawing a sweep on the day of the race.
• * * •
Melbourne parsons fight the Totalisator Bill
because, they say, the legalisation of the machine is a
recognition of the betting evil—“ an adoption of the evil
by the State.” This from the institution that has recog-
nised and adopted the Devil !

Horse journalism will soon be reduced to
chronicling the silver wagers of fraudulent shop-boys
with the man in the street. Melb. Age, last Friday, pub-
lished this stirring item of sporting intelligence : “The
feature of the betting at the calling of the card of the
G.N. Steeplechase,at the Victorian Club, last evening,
was t hat £4O to i. 10 was taken Hayseed, and he was then
installed favorite at 3 to 1.”

Mr. Jos. Brown and his Northern following had
a good day out at Canterbury (Sydney), last Saturday,
when he won the double, Stewards’Mile and Canterbury
Handicap with Zeno 11., and the Park Stakes with the
Fusilier gelding Guardsman, Marsden having the mount
on each occasion. \
\* * »
Surprising the number of horses that bob up and
win a two-year-old race about Sydney, have great things
predicted for them by all hands, and then disappear and
are never heard of again. What becomes of them ? Am-
pier (by Far Niente), jvho won at Canterbury Park,
should, however, prove an exception to the rule.
m * • *f * *
There were fully 40 punters in that Sydney
tote-shop on Saturday when the police attacked, but only
15 were collared. Most of those who knew the secret
escapes got clear, and one really clever gent, got out by
grabbing a brother backer and hauling him out after the
manner of the “ tecs.” In the excitement he passed for
a plain-clothes trap, and both escaped.
* » « *
The entries for the trotting events at the Dubbo
Club's meeting at Kensington, Syd., to-morrow (Friday)
are very satisfactory : Maiden Trot, 10 ; One-mile handi-
cap, 20; Free-for-all, 14 ; and Two-miles Handicap, 16.
Amongst the Free-for-all there are some old and speedy
horses Kola, Prince Harold, Silver Cloud, Syren, St.
Louis, Viva, and the returned Hattie S. ; but, if Huon
Junior is well and in the humor, his driver will be able
comfortably to look round and see them fight it out for
seen nd place.

Well-known bookmaker walking up Pitt-street
with friend, after a successful day’s “punting.” “See,
Paddy, I’m as good a judge as there is in Australia. I
can pick ’em every time.” Lurches, and falls up against
a newly-painted shop-front. “ There you are 1 What did
I tell you? 11l bet there ain’t another house in Pitt-
street getting painted. A million to one chance—and I
picked it first pop ! ”

What an appalling thing it must be to have a
new Knight for a newspaper boss ! Here, for instance is
the opening slab from the horse-reporter’s account of
Port Augusta races: “The recent rains have so much
improved the prospects of the whole colony . . . that,
instead of going to the sister colony for the V.R.C. Grand
National meeting, I was advised by Sir Langdon Bony-
thon to take a trip to Port Augusta.” Next the police-
court youth will armonnee (and S’langdon will publish in
the ’Tiser, edited by S’langdon) that, acting on S’lang-
don’s suggestion, he attended at Vic. Square yesterday
morning with the undermentioned results.
The Appeal Committee of the M.L. Racing
Conference has just disposed of its second case. The
institution is an admirable one in every respect (writes a
Bulletin correspondent), owners standing more chance
of getting justice, if they have a good case, than they did
aforetime. Its constitution and methods: —the Confer-
ence, representing all recognised clubs, sets up a com-
mittee of three to hear appeals of owners dissatisfied with
stewards’ decisions. Substantial deposit is required, and
the objector and club can each be represented by counsel
and oall witnesses.
* * * *
New Zealand Cup entry-list this year contains
eight Australian-born horses, such sires as Abercorn, Boz,
Goldsborough, Malua, and Australian Peer being repre-
sented. Dan O’Brien has four listed, including A.J.O.
Derby winner, Bob Ray, who, however, is still in Sydney.
Heritas, Peerage, and Malatua are a trio of untried
youngsters, but Dan has hopes of successfully cutting
against Yaldhurst Stead’s and other dangerous stables
next season.

* 9 • *
At the last Grand Prix de Paris meeting
£166,362 went through the tote on the six events; of
this, £69,558 was put on Grand Prix candidates. Tote
prices are from 4s. to £2o—mostly 45., Bs., and 165.,
though. And you back a horse for a place, too, and not
merely for first past the post; but this doesn’t apply to
fancy-price tickets.
» * • *
At Sydney Cricket Ground, last Saturday, City
beat Country in their fourth annual football match by 20
points to 8, but the game was much more evenly con-
tested than the difference in points would imply. Pre-
vious to this match the next fifteen seniors played a
rattling good game with the combined juniors, seniors
winning by 18 points to 16.
* * • *
Victor Trumper, the young Sydney cricketer
thought so much of in England and by the Englishmen,
now figures well in the football field. Pity that so pro-
mising a cricketer should risk his career with bat and ball
by playing the dangerous game. One wrench of joint
or sinew may put up his shutters so far as first-class
cricket is concerned.
* • « •
“Jimmie”: Be Sydney D.T.’s charge of bru-
tality against the Randwick footballers —two significant
facts. Randwick and University played two draws this
season, and the only players seriously hurt were three
Randwicks—Braund, Henlen, Surinam Also the most
powerful footballer in the province (John Fraser, of the
Randwicks) is the gentlest giant that ever donned a
jersey. Were it otherwise, John could slaughter the
other side by the dozen every match. The Telegraph
man’s partisanship, however, will never let him acknow-
ledge what he must know as well as anybody—viz., that
the Randwick’s success is due first to the three C.’s—con-
dition, combination, and cleverness —and only in second-
ary degree to weight and pace.

» * * *
Adelaide football promises to boom at the end
of the season, owing to the evenness of the three leading
teams, who are now level. Essendon’s recent visit to
S.A. showed, however, that Adelaide football is far below
the standard of a few years ago.
* • • •
During an enjoyable shin-kicking contest in
Hawera (M.L.) district, five men were knocked out and
four others ordered off. A fast horse enabled the referee
to escape popular fury.
* * * *
The death of “ Happy George ” Ulyett, the
other week, recalled to some people’s minds the burly
Yorkshireman’s “happy fooling” in one Australia v. Eng-
land match, played at the Melbourne 0.0. Ground long
ago—about 16 years back. Rumor said (before the visitors
took the field) that some of the pros, had accepted an in-
ducement from Ikey Mo to play loosely, and the particu-
lar looseness of Ulyett’s bowling and fielding gave some
sort of color to the rumor. It might have been due to
Beer, without crookedness aforethought ; or it might
have been that he took an extra loid of malt liquor
aboard as an apparent excuse for his absurdly bad play.
At that time he was undoubtedly the best all-round Eng-
lish cricketer when fairly on the job—a slashing bat, fine
fast bowler, and good field. Three or four of the early
Australian Elevens at ’Ome had reason to fear the Happy
One, but his brilliancy became very occasional as time
went on, and his sun sank down into the sea of Beer some
time before his rival, old man Grace, had ceased to shine.
* * » *
The great Bilmartin is, apparently, not reckoned
so great in his native land. He now stands on the 30yd.
mark in mile events—at the side of John S. Johnson—con-
sequent upon his defeat by Cooper in the L.A.W. Brassard.
* * * *
T.D.S. : Nonsense to say that cycling forbids
drunkenness. Have had no personal experience, but
have, seen many wheelers who, though too drunk to walk
a three-foot plank for a million, could—once across the
saddle and fairly going—ride the proverbial chalk-line.
Same with horsemen.
* * * *
Talk of the. progress of the bicycle! Why,
N.S.W. Collector of Customs Lockyer has just toured the
far West on a machine, making one run from Broken
Hill to Wentworth—over 200 miles of bush-track, most of
which a few years ago would have been thought im-
passable. “Ironbark ” Gibson,inspector of Crown Lands
offices, has been making the round of the far-back town-
ships similarly mounted. But the thing may be easily
overdone; already one Govt, inspector in the far West,
taking to the machine perhaps too late in life, has broken
down and been buried.
* * • * *
The lacrosse boom planned for Melb. in Aug.
promises to fizzle out owing to the probable defection of
S.A. lacrosseurs. Adelaide sent a team to Melb. last year,
and for that reason many players, who have to pay their
own expenses, have withdrawn this year, and a team
cannot be raised. Melb. visits Sydney on Aug. 13, how-
ever.
* * « *
Yic. rifle-team came second in the shooting
for the Kolapore Cup at Bisley—scoring 741 points to
winning Guernsey’s 744. The English team tied at 741,
but Vic. went up a place for having best score at longest
range. Last year the Vic. team won, with MiL. second.
» * * •
The eight from the Man o’ War Royal Arthur,
which met the Sydney Young Men’s Christian Association
team in the tug-of-war last week, indii idualiy looked big
enough to eat the muscular young Christians, but collec-
tively they had no show, the “ boys ” winning two out of
the three pulls. Rumor has it that an Irish team will
shortly be out with a challenge to the Y.M.C.A.

Tweedie and Gaffney did eight rounds at the
Golden Gate Athletic Club, Sydney, on Saturday night.
Though not gory and hair-flving, the set-to was a “ wil-
ling,” scientific exhibition of boxing. Tweedie was fairly
adjudged the winner on points.

the 100yds. amateur championship of England, which was
won by Darbyshire in lmin. 4-sth sec. Lester, a name
unknown in Australia, also finished in front of Hellings.

* * • •
About time the curtain was rung down on the
farce of Mons. Gilbert’s “ walk round the world.’
Between Melbourne and Albury the Frenchman
trudged at the rate of exactly 9 milee a day, and at the
border town he rested his sore feet for three days. Where
are the proofs that Gilbert covered the thousands of miles
he professes to have “put in” before reaching tins
country ?
• * *
The menagerie race in a W. Queensland town,
t’other day, was won by a rooster. When the signal was
given a ruffian standing near the judge ran a pin into a
hen, with immense resultant feminine uproar. Chanti-
cleer fled immediately to rescue the distressed cackler
and canine and goatee competitors were left mournfully
in the rear. Enthusiasm tremendous.
Suffleld and Gould.
T O THE ELEOTORS OF SINGLETON.
Albert John Gould, N.S.W. Minister of Jus-
tice, is seeking re-election for Singleton. Every
man who votes for Albert John Gould will prove
by that vote that he lacks either common intelli-
gence or common humanity.
About Albert John Gould as a private person
The Bulletin knows and cares nothing. In his
public capacity as Minister of Justice he is worse
than a failure —he is an offence to just and humane
people and a scandal to the community.
Gould’s treatment of Thomas Suffield has been
marked by the same obstinate stupidity that has
marked other of his administrative acts and
omissions with which the public is acquainted.
Suffield was tried at Maitland, on Bth April, 1891,
on a charge of criminally assaulting Ann
Chambers, a servant of Thomas Croudaee, at
Lambton. There was no proof that the assault
had been committed by a man. Dr. Staple-
ton, who saw Chambers an hour after
the alleged assault, refused to swear that she had
been assaulted by a man. Dr. Harris, who saw
her two days afterwards , thought she had been
assaulted by a man—and stultified himself
by taking the responsibility of thinking
so, for the evidence on which he relied
was ludicrously insufficient. Chambers herself
told her employer, Croudaee, that she had been
assaulted by a vicious bull, which was there on
the spot ; and this bull subsequently assaulted
another woman in the same way, inflicting similar
injuries, and was shot for it. Chambers herself—to
her employer, his son, his wife, his daughter, her
fellow-servants, and Dr. Stapleton—for several
hours persisted in the story that she had been as-
saulted by a bull. Then she changed her story, and
said she had been assaulted by a man, but did not
mention his name. Later on, she said the man
was Suffield —a man of the highest character, who
had only once seen her, had never spoken to her,
and who two witnesses swore was in his room at
home at the time of the alleged assault. Cham-
bers’s story bristled with other contradictions ;
there was, against Suffield, no corroborative evi-
dence whatever ; but Judge Windeyer, as was his
habit, bullied the jury into giving a verdict for
the woman, and Suffield went to gaol for ten
years.
Section 383 of N.S.W. Criminal Law Amend-
ment Act permits Albert John Gould, “ whenever
after the conviction of a prisoner any doubt or
question arises as to his guilt,” to order an enquiry
into the case. Albert John Gould has obstinately
refused to order an enquiry into Suffield’s case.
But Suffield has on three separate occasions been
informed that his petition for release would be
favorably considered. Suffield —though eating his
heart out in gaol—has persistently declined to peti-
tion, on the ground that he is innocent; that to
sue for mercy would be construed as an admission
of guilt; and that he does not want mercy, but
justice.
If Gould believes that Suffield is guilty, why
does he offer to release him ? If Gould believes
that Suffield is innocent, why does he keep him
in gaol? If Gould has doubts as to Suffield’s
guilt, why does he not authorise the simple enquiry
permitted by law ?
Gould has been talking of Suffield recently—of
this man whom he is ready to let out of gaol, but
into whose case he will not permit enquiry. He says
that fresh evidence should be brought forward. This
is a fiction ; the Act does not demand fresh evi-
dence. But fresh evidence was brought forward.
Four affidavits containing much new evidence
were sent to the Crown Law Department, and
apparently suppressed—they have not been heard
of from the day of receipt to this ! Gould says
that R. E. O’Connor and T. M. Slattery, previous
Ministers of Justice, considered Suffield’s case,
and came to his conclusion. R. E. O’Connor
—six years ago—had not before him the facts
and the lights which Gould has before him : if he
had, he would no doubt have ordered enquiry
at once. There is nothing to show that Slattery
ever went into the case at all. In any event,
Gould cannot excuse himself by the mistakes or
neglect of his predecessors. He has fuller infor-
mation than they; and it is his duty to judge for
himself.
That Gould has reached his present conclu-
sion—in which he offers to release Suffield if
Suffield will in effect consent to relinquish all hope
of proving his innocence—in The Bulletin’s
opinion points to the conclusion that Gould is
conscious of blundering yet too obstinate to
admit his blunder.
The Bulletin repeats that, in face ,ot Albert
John Gould’s departmental record, and particu-
larly in view of his action and inaction in the
Suffield case, whoever votes to return Gould to
Parliament disgraces either his intelligence or his
humanity.

The Church of England authorities in
Sydney have cabled to England for an organising

and several lank Australians who sport chokers
want to know what’s the matter with the sons of
the soil. At a recent important church meeting

was asked or allowed to move or second a resolution.

“K.K.” : Connection between specs and
saintliness. Weak eyes indicate weak consti-
tution. Weak constitution involves inability to
withstand attacks of- religion microbe. Result :
saintliness. Saintliness has to take to glasses
because it catches gimlet-hole cold in its eyes ;
also saints wear specs to enable them to see motes
in their brothers’ eyes, and to prevent their
brothers seeing the beams in their eyes. Like-
wise, saints wear glasses to protect their eyes from
the glare of their own haloes.
Honey-lovers will be inclined to “ sit up ”,
over the information that an article which, to all
sight, taste, and smell, is the genuine “ garden ”
may be a result of the labors, not of the bee, but
of the sugar-refiner—this on the testimony of the
Colonial Sugar Co.’s chief chemist. Even chemi-
cal analysis, it is asserted, fails to detect any dif-
ference : but, as the manufactured article can’t
be “ built ”at a commercially remunerative cost
bee-keepers have no reason to tremble.

Two Rondels of Pushland.
[Train up a donah in the way she should go, and, if
she live through it, marry her.— Proverbs of the Push.]
I.
My bloomin’ donah is the shinest Tom beneath
the sun,
An’ I know a lot er blokes vvho'd like ter own
’er ;
But she rlaresent take ’em on beeos’ I’ve got ’er
fairly won—
My bloomin’ donah !
Fer I’ve knocked art Chisel Watson an’ another
bleed in’ gun,
An’ a perfic’ box er tricks already shown ’er—
Till, Lord strike me pink, ole party, from ’er
’atin’ me like fun
Yew ken see ’er ev’ry ev’nin’, w’en ’er ’bacca-
rollin’ 's done,
Artside “The Mona,”
Meekly waitin’ fer a smile from me who never gi’s
’er none—
My bloomin’ donah!
11.
My bit er skirt! Yew ken bet yer boots, ole
party, she’s all right—
Tho’ the fellers try ter kid me she’s a flirt;
But she’s leanrn’ better manners ev’ry time she
sees me fight—
My bit er skirt!
An’ she knows if she went foolin’ roun’ an’ let me
get a sight—
Well, the bloke would snavel somethin’ that ’d
’urt,
An’ she ’d cop a bleedin’ doin’ that’d stop ’er
lacin’ tight.
Fer the way ter train a donah—so’s she’ll be yer
life’s delight—
Is ter stan’ no bleedin’ nonsense an’ ter take no
bloomin’ dirt,
But ter butt ’er in the basket ev’ry time with all
yer might,
Same as I do when I’m narked with my bloomin’
bit er skirt.
N.S.W.

HERE RASMUSSEN’S
||)ission in the Herbal World.
Herr Rasmussen has devoted his whole life to
the study of medical herbs and manufacture of
Herbal remedies, and his great mission has been
most wonderfully successful.
The great virtues of the Herr Rasmussen’s Alfa-
line Herbal Remedies are so well known that it is
quite unnecessary to say much in regard to them ;
the very fact of their having been before the pub-
lic for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so. many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented in
the History of Herbal Remedies.
Herbal Remedies are gradually pushing their
way forward, entirely taking the place of those
remedies which contain mercury, arsenic or strych-
nine.
The Australian press has fully recognised the
noble mission of Herr Rassmussen, and praise
bestowed upon him from this important source has
not only been most whole-hearted, but also very
generous. Space will not permit to repeat one-
tenth of his best press notices, but few will suffice
to show the public the estimation in which Herr
Rassmussen is held by the Australian press.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” The •
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of testimonials to that
effect.

“ It has been observed,” Newcastle M.
Herald remarks, “that acute rheumatism is
more prevalent in dry than rainy weather.” Iri
wet weather the rheumatwinger hops about almost
cheerfully ; in ordinary dry weather he goes in
terror of inducing a sweat; in muggy weather he
blasphemes and throws things; but when the
damp heat comes that precedes rain, he commits
murder—if he can get about!
Vic. Labor-party held a meeting the other
day, and a motion was prepared, but not actually
submitted, to the effect that Trenwith had swelled
head and was too big for his boots, and should be
deposed from the leadership. After a long dis-
cussion, however, it was tacitly resolved, without
a division, that Trenwith’s head was only, the
natural size after all, and that he should continue
the leadership till further notice.
A Victorian beet-growing district lately
put in an exciting day and got through more pro-
fanity than a beet-growing district usually accom-
plishes in a year. The centre of the raging
cyclone was the local pub. Late at night
the boss beer-merchant was awakened out of his
sleep by a pilgrim who wanted a bed, and he went
down in a tired, partially-dressed state to let him
in. When he had fired the late arrival into a bed-
room, and fallen over the house-cat, he went back
to bed—and in the morning he discovered that
his vest, and his gold watch and chain, and a £5
note that were in the pockets thereof had. evapor-
ated. The late arrival of the previous night had
evaporated also ; nothing remained of him-but a
large blucher-mark outside the door. All that
day the police raged over the surrounding dis-
tricts, and bailed-up wanderers and overhauled
their swags, to no purpose. Blinding gusts of beer
and profanity blew all day in the pub. and
25 different inhabitants discovered exactly how
the robbery was committed in 25 totally different
ways. As the publican’s room-door had been
locked, the most feasible theory was that the
assets were snatched out of his upstairs window
with a long fishing-rod and hook, and the general
opinion was that the whisky-merchant should be
very thankful that it wasn’t his eye which went.
At last the exhausted publican went to bed, leav-
ing the missus to lock up, and as she was casting
out the last batcli of drunks a fearsome cry rent
the welkin. It came from the boss’s room, and
the crowd swarmed up the stairs to investigate.
What they found was a publican with nothing on
him save a vest, making strange, mumbling noises
in the middle of the room. When he got up to
admit the late pilgrim the previous night he had
scrambled into the vest first, and then he had
plunged round in the dark and put his day-shirt
over it. After that he had dropped back into bed
without getting out of his day-shirt, and as, of
course, he didn’t take a bath in the morning, the
vest remained there. The welkin which was rent
by his cry when he recovered the property has
since been repaired, and is now as good as new,
but the district is suffering severely from shock,
and doesn’t assimilate its beer with the old free-
dom.

Competition in Trade.
A COMPLAINT FROM DARLINGTON.
d Competition is so keen in our neighborhood,”
said Mr: John Burgess, of 58 Fry-street, Darling-
tbfi; Sydney, to a press reporter recently, “ that
firilesS 1 daii drive ffiy horse afid cart afound
I lo’se my customers, as they will not come to my
sdiop.” Mr; Burgess, it ifiusfc be explained, is a
well-known greengrocer in the locality men-
tioned. “ This being so,” he continued, “ when I
was a hopelessly bed-ridden cripple, my business
decreased extensively. I was suffering severely
from sciatica combined with hip-joint disease.
But for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills I should still be
in bed. I have l> en an invalid for over nine years.
The cause of my great suffering was being thrown
out of a cab whilst going to Botany, the accident
being due to the defective state of the tramway
line. Falling on my side, I seriously injured my
hip. I became an inmate of Princef Alfred Hos-
pital for six months, and was at last discharged as
incurable. Since then I have had no less than
three different doctors, and they all said my case
was hopeless ; the last doctor who attended me
stated that he did not wish to visit me any longer,
as it was simply taking money out of my pocket
to put into his, without being able to afford me
the slightest relief. Sciatica then set in, with
its attendant agonising pains, first in the hip,
and then it appeared to shoot to the calf of the
leg and to the toot. I had no desire for food,
sleep was a matter of sheer impossibility, and
instead of getting better I grew gradually worse.
I could not move without the aid of crutches, and
I Was forced to take to my bed. As a drowning
ntaii clutches at a straw, I tried all sorts of patent
medicines and specifics. I might as well have
kept the money in my pocket, for none of them
did me the slightest good. I feared I was a
cripple for life, and hopelessly bed-ridden. Re-
lief, however, reached me at the eleventh hour,
and in the most unexpected manner. A lady,
residing in this neighborhood, induced me to try
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. I must
confess I regarded her advice as a forlorn hope,
but it was a fortunate and happy day when I
commenced to take these pills, for from the first
box I commenced to steadily improve. After
seven boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, I was
able to get up and dress, and after the eighth box
could go to market with my horse and cart, and
afterwards serve my customers. When a man can
conscientiously state that this wonderful recovery
is due entirely to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, it
should silence sceptics and induce other sufferers
who endured the torments that I did, to give this
wonderful preparation a fair and honest trial, as
I can truly state it has succeeded in putting me on
my legs again.”
Twelve Years!
HOW THE REPORTER DROPPED IN.
There is a neat and natty grocery store at the
corner of Rathdown and Palmerston streets, Mel-
bourne, when one can always drop in for an
interesting chat with the proprietoress, Mrs.
Margaret Ross, who assists her husband in man-
aging the business. The theme upon which the
conversation frequently turns is the marvellous
recovery from neuralgia which Mrs. Ross has ex-
perienced through using Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People. A reporter dropped in at the
store recently, and to him Mrs. Ross very kindly
narrated her experience.
“ I have been a great sufferer from neuralgia for
ten or twelve years,” she said, “ but nothing re-
lieved me except Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, which alone have effected a cure.
All changes of season proved futile to arrest my
malady. During the daytime my sufferings were
painful, but on retiring to rest, the agony I
suffered was excruciating, reducing me to a state
of general collapse. I felt it during the day
slightly, but most severely at night. ”
1 ‘ Did you consult any doctors at all ? ”
“Many; but received no permanent benefit.
They gave me draughts and powders, but the
effect was really nothing.”
“ You, of course, have tried other remedies ? ”
“ Yes; I tried everything, but obtained no
relief.”
“ What induced you to try Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People ? ”
“An elderly lady, resident at Heathcote, 74
years of age, who had been a sufferer from rheu-
matism for .40 years, and who had been absolutely
cured by their use, recommended me to try the
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
“How long since might that be ? ”
“ Six weeks since—the beginning of July. Dur-
ing the time I was taking the first box I lost only
one night’s rest, but since then I have never had
the least return of the old complaint.”
“ Have you used much of the medicine ? ”
“About four boxes. Previous to my taking
them the pain in my head reduced me to such a
state that I was on the verge of madness. The
last three years I had been very bad, so you can
imagine the pleasure it was to me to find a remedy
that;has actually made a new woman of me.”
“ That js so,” remarked Mr. Ross, who was pre-
sent. “ Had you seen my wife a few months since
and now, you would scarcely believe her to be the
same woman.”
“Yes,” added Mrs. Ross, “my recovery has
been most pronounced and satisfactory to me. I
have great faith in Dr. Williams’Pink Pills for
Pale People, and recommend them wherever pos-
sible.” >

“Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. Dear Sirs,—l
have several times been going to drop you a line
to tell you of the debt of gratitude I owe your
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ During the past 12 months I have been so-
journing in West Australia, accompanied by a
severe attack of neuralgia. I need hardly inform
you that this was a source of great annoyance to
me. I tried various remedies, but without avail,
when incidentally, a lady friend mentioned your
Pink Pills to me. As a forlorn hope, I gave them
a trial, amd was simply astonished at the beneficial
results, instant relief following.
“Should you care to make use of this testi-
monial, you are at perfect liberty to do so.—Re-
spectfully yours. Millie Finkelstein.”
An Eaglehawk Delegate.
HAS A TALE TO TELL.
I have for years been subject to dreadfu
attacks of diarrhoea,” said Mr. David Evans, of
Californian Gully, Eaglehawk, Victoria, to a
reporter, “and,” continued he, “it’s ten years
ago now since they first came upon me. I was
in Sydney at the time, working at my trade as a
tirfsmith. The attacks were so severe that they
tesulted in iny losing my employment, as my
employer stated he must secure a regular atten-
dance in his employes. I subsequently worked
at Marrickville and Summer Hill, and had again
to leave through constant absence from illness. I
attended at the Sydney Infirmary for a while,
the medical officer recommended me to try a
change of climate; so I came to Victoria, and
the change really did me good for a time. But
in last November the old complaint returned, so
I came on here in search of rest and recovery.
Still the diarrhoea continued, and I obtained no
relief until early last July.”
“ And the reason ? ”
. “ A friend of mine advised me to try Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, and I did. After the third box
I commenced to lose all symptoms of the diarrhoea,
and by the time I was half-way through the fourth
I felt myself fairly on the way to recovery. At
the sixth, when half way through, I discontinued
taking them, and feel now as well as ever. I can-
not describe the agonies I suffered. I had a con-
tinued gripping and laxity of the bowels that
exhausted me. I had tried all other known reme-
dies without result, and I am thankful that I at
last struck Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
Mr. Evans is now a hale, hearty and robust man,
and no one seeing him now wouid anticipate that
he had recently gone through such a severe and
trying illness.
Auburn Anxiety.
WHAT A YOUNG LADY DESIRED.
Hearing that a young lady residing at Auburn,
who had previously been suffering from anaemia,
had tried a course of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, a reporter took his way to Mrs.
Dick’s well-known store in Parramatta - road,
Auburn. Miss Victoria Dick made her appear-
ance in a few moments and courteously received
the visitor. In response to an enquiry Miss Dick
said : “ I certainly do owe much to Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People ; I can speak with feel-
ing, for I’ve suffered for nearly seven years.
Anaemia was the principal complaint, but I often
suffered tortures from indigestion, accompanied by
sick headaches of the most violent description.
I began to look upon myself as a regular invalid,
and fancied there was no way of escape. Apart
from the pains of indigestion I seemed to have
absolutely no desire to eat, and everything
seemed tasteless to me. My mother would often
try and tempt me to eat by making dainty little
dishes, but I simply couldn’t bear the sight of
food. I used to feel great listlessness. My work
became a regular nightmare to me, as I could
hardly drag myself about. I would long for
night to come, and then long for day. lam now
twenty-three years of age; I was about sixteen
years old when my illness seemed to start, and it
went on for close on seven years. For many
nights I would hardly get any sleep, and then it
would be of the most fitful description. The pains
in my head would rack me dreadfully. I would
feel as limp as a kitten, and at times be quite
unable to stir, much less do any work. I had
plenty of medical advice, for I tried four doctors,
one after the other, but no real benefit seemed to
ensue. I swallowed quarts of medicines and tried
all sorts of remedies. My mother thinking that
perhaps a change would do me good, sent me to
Melbourne for a holiday, but it seemed of no use,
for I returned home as bad as ever. However,
about twelve months ago I gave Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People a trial, for, as I said,
they could do me no harm and perhaps might do
me some good. I procured four boxes and deter-
mined to hope for the best.”
“ And with what result ? ”
“ Well you may hardly believe it ; the very
first box gave slight relief, though at the time I
was so dispirited that I scarcely gave it the credit
it deserved. Presently I noticed my headaches
became less and less painful, the color gradually
came back to my face, and, as I persevered with
the pills, the painful symptoms slowly disappeared,
my skin was firmer, I suddenly seemed to have an
appetite—such as I had not possessed for years.
Previously I might have beeri eating sawdust for
all the taste I had, but this was soon altered ; the
dull, heavy feeling left, and I felt as if I could
run a mile, or go up and downstairs all day. As
you may imagine, I was formerly very much run-
down and most nervous and hysterical, but now
I seem to feel the life flowing through my veins ;
only those who have been ill can understand the
relief I felt at the gradual cessation of all unfavor-
able symptoms. I continued taking the pills till
I took eight boxes in all. lam confident that Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are alone responsible for my
cure. lam now in perfect health. I was recom-
mended to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People by Miss Kennerdale in this district, and
she told me they had completely cured her of a
very similar ailment. lam thankful I listened to
her kind advice.”
Aldermanic Experience.
The people of this country have good cause to
view with alarm an outbreak of influenza, as it
leaves behind it more shattered constitutions than
any other known disease. Mr. Edward Botting,
for ten years a Councillor of the united townships
of Bedford, Olden, and Palmerston, inErontenao
County, is one who nearly fell a victim to this
scourge. To a Whig correspondent Mr. Botting
said “ About two years ago I had a bad attack
of influv.'.za, and the after-effects of that malig-
nant trouble brought me so low that my friends
despaired of my recovery. I was troubled with
severe and constant pains in the back, sensations
of extreme dizziness and weakness, and was, in
fact, in a generally used-up condition. I had read
frequently of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and felt
they must have some special virtues, else they
could not obtain such strong endorsements in all
parts of the country. I determined to try them,
and I bless the day that I came to that conclusion.
Before the first box was finished I felt benefited,
and I continued their use until I was as strong as
ever. I consider Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills the
best medicine sold.
Hot Sea Baths.
PROOF OF THEIR USELESSNESS.
Mrs. C. Fairclough, wife of the proprietor of
the Carnarvon Hotel, Port Arthur, Tas., lias
passed through some of the severest sufferings
ever inflicted upon unfortunate human beings.
Rheumatism, or rheumatic gout, was her afflic-
tion, and medical science and patent medicines
were worse than nought. Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills alone succeeded in effecting once more a
remarkable and inexpensive cure.
For years, so she stated, she had been a sufferer
from rheumatic affections, which at first seemed
trivial, but in spite of ordinary precautions con-
tinued to increase so as to give rise to consider-
able alarm. The doctors pronounced the case to
be one of much severity, the complaint being
rheumatic gout. Everything was tried, but with-
out avail, and Mrs. Fairclough became at length
a pronounced invalid.
For months she was hopeless of ever recovering
the use of her limbs, and the pains which she en-
dured were absolutely beyond conception. Hot
sea baths were tried, but failed, and, in fact, she
was looked upon as a confirmed invalid.
Worrying over her illness brought on a variety
of other complaints. The least cold affected her to
an abnormal degree. She was seized with pains
in the feet so that she could hardly walk, even a
feeble effort giving her considerable pain. She
was compelled to sit in a warm place constantly,
and her appetite fell away, so that she ate but one
meal in three days. Every effort was made to
secure relief, the best medical advice procurable
being obtained. It was without avail, however,
for she, so far from getting better, grew steadily
worse.
Hearing of some of the marvellous cures effected
by the use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People she decided to try them. Her husband
laughed at her decision and condemned the medi-
cine as being useless and inoperative. But, acting
on the belief that if Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills did
her no good a few could not do any harm, she
gave them a trial.
After commencing to take the pills she noticed
a decided improvement in her condition. The
pains were not so aggravated, and she could
stand without assistance, a feat which previously
she was unable to accomplish. Soon the pains
in the feet ceased altogether, and she felt like a
new woman. Delighted with the discovery which
she had made, she continued to use Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, and after carefully taking them con-
tinuously according to directions for a period of
two months she felt that she had no longer any
occasion to use the medicine.
“I cannot explain to you,” Mrs. Fairclough
said, “ how grateful I am for having discovered
a cure for my complaint. I was in torture and
completely broken down with despair for my con-
dition. I felt that life was a burden, and I seemed
to be in everybody’s way by day and by night.
My husband is as grateful as myself for the cure
effected, and we both feel that I owe a new lease
of my life to the use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People. I have every faith that they
have completely driven out the demon of rheu-
matic gout from my system. lam now a new
woman, and, being well and strong and hearty,
am able to perform all my duties satisfactorily.
And all is due to the use for two months of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
Ploughing.
RECOLLECTIONS OF A HORTON RIVER
FARMER.
Mr. George Batchelor, a farmer on the Horton
River, N.S.W., at 59 years of age, just when his
strength would prove most valuable, became the
victim of the attacks of those enemies to life ar.d
limb —Insomnia, Nervous Debility, Despondency,
and Intense Suffering. He was rescued, however,
by Dr. Williams Pink Pills for Pale People. The
reporter, who found him ploughing, obtained the
following particulars :
“ I think,” said Mr. Batchelor, “ my case is one
that should have all the publicity possible. lam
65 years of age, and have been resident in this dis-
trict for about 12 years. Prior to my coming
here I had always enjoyed the best of health, and
I had been accustomed to work very hard. About
six years ago, however, all my energy seemed to
gradually desert me. I suffered acutely and con-
stantly from pains in my limbs, lost my appetite,
and for months at a stretch I did not know' what
it was to get a good night’s sleep. Work of the
very lightest nature at last became beyond the
question with me, and I could do nothing but sit
by the window and content myself by watching
the others work the farm. I gave up all hope of
ever being able to do any more work, and almost
believed Jhat every week which commenced would
be my-last. In my endeavors to stay the ravages
of the ailment which had overtaken me, and was
gradually drawing me into the grave, 1 tried
innumerable so-called cures, and swallowed an
immense quantity of medical compounds, but
beyond giving me temporary relief occasionally,
none of them did me any good; in fact, I
honestly believe that the majority of them only
aggravated my sufferings. A few months back
I was just on the point of giving up in despair
when I happened to read in a newspaper of some
wonderful cures effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People, and I determined to give
them a trial. After taking the first box, I felt
considerably relieved, and so marked was the
improvement in my strength that I decided to
continue their use. I did so, and up to the pre-
sent time I have taken five boxes. My inability
has all disappeared, and I am pleased to say that
to-day I am as strong and hearty as I ever was
in my life. It is no trouble to me to do a day’s
farm work now, and I feel quite equal to the
task of walking at least thirty miles a day.
Everyone who knows me can testify to the great
cure Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have effected in
me, and in recommending them for similar cases
to mine I can honestly say that they are worth
more than their weight in gold. If everybody
knew their true value, there would be no other
medicine sold.”
“ Mount Magnet, Murchison, W.A., May 9,
1898. Dear Sirs, —I received the Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People quite safely and in
good order. I have taken two boxes myself for
rheumatism, which I have had in both my knees.
I was unable to kneel down. I have had the
rheumatism on me for over fourteen years. I feel
the pain quite gone from me now, and am as
nimble on my legs as I was twenty years ago, and
I feel like a young fellow again. I have told my
friends what your Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have
done for me, and they are now taking them. You
are at liberty to advertise this for the sake of other
Poe. sufferers.— l am, kc., H. Baldwin ”
An Ashby Anecdote.
AS TOLD IN AUTUMN-STREET.
Influenza is now rampant all over Australia,
and the greatest precautions possible should be
taken to guard against it, as one has not only to
contend with the malady itself, but with its fear-
ful and devastating after-effects. That this is so is
shown most clearly by the case of Miss Jamieson,
of Autumn-street, Ashby, Geelong, Victoria.
“Some few years ago,” she told a reporter, “I
suffered from a very severe attack of influenza. I
could never get rid of its after-effects until 1 tried
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. Al-
though generally experiencing robust health pre-
vious to this attack, when at last I was able to
leave my room I was almost of ghostly proportions
—not at all like lam now. And I continued in
this state for some time. I did not mind the
weakness so much, feeling sure that would wear off
in time with good nourishment. But, in March
of last year, I commenced to experience great
inconvenience from pains in my right side. I
consulted a medical man, who said that it was
merely the after-effects of the influenza, and I
was not to worry myself. But, as the pains
continued, I did worry myself, and went to Mel-
bourne to consult a specialist. He informed me
that it was partial paralysis. He put me through
a course of treatment that left me as I was, and I
returned home fairly worn out. Whether the
worry or the heroic treatment I had undergone
was responsible or not I will not venture an
opinion upon, but a week afterwards erysipelas
set in on my face, and for weeks and months I
suffered the greatest agony. In January a friend
(a Miss Evans, of Eaglehawk, near Bendigo)
came down to see me, and one day she bought me
a box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. When visiting
Melbourne I took them, and four days after the
pain commenced to go away. I continued taking
them for some time, and in all I used eight boxes,
discontinuing them in August. From then until
now I have not had the least symptom of a return
of either complaints—complaints that I had not
been free from for four years. I am 22 years of
age. Scores know my sufferings, and the means of
my recovery I am only too pleased to supply.”
What Paleness Means.
A MATTER FOR THE GIRLS.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth.
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, yellow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched,
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at
the time their daughters are budding into
womanhood. If your daughter is pale, com-
plains of weakness, is “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side, if her temper is
fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
for the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
womanhood, and thus avoid the risks of disease
or early decay.
The Vitality of the Human
Race.
The public are offered Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
with a full confidence that there is no disease
arising from a Watery or Impoverished Condition
of the Blood, Shattered Nervous Forces, which
they will not cure if the directions are faithfully

not a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly scien-

the Blood) upon the Vital Organs of both Men
and Women. It is a well - recognised fact
that upon the healthy activity of the vital
apparatus depends the mental and physical
well - being _of every person come to adult
years. It is that which gives the rosy blush
to the cheek, and the soft light to the eye of the
maiden. The elastic step, the ringing laugh, and
the strong right arm of youth own this same main-
spring. How soon do irregularities rob the face
of color, the eye of brightness ! The blood be-
comes impoverished, the victim pale. The pallor
of the skin is often the only outward mark of the
trouble within. “Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People ” enrich the impoverished blood and

mantles the faded cheek.
