“Premier Reid,” says a Melb.
“is much harassed by his excessive obesity.”
Humph ! girth-galled.
Thus a Melb. chiropodist:—
Beer enlarges the feet of women. It is beer-drinking
that has spoiled the feet of English and German women.
The Americans who have adopted that drink are begin-
ning to lose the beauty of their feet. French women
(especially Parisiennes) are noted for their neat, small
feet. They are great wine drinkers. So with Spanish
and Italian women. If their feet are not so small, they
are always beautifully formed. The average Australian
woman has a foot not quite so large as that of English or
German type, but then it is not so finely moulded as that
of a woman of a wine-drinking country. I speak, of
course, mainly of the fashionable division, though the
rule applies to all classes. Take a hard-working woman
who drinks beer or porter and compare her feet with
those of a Wine or spirit drinker. You will see I am
right.
Subjects wanted for comparison. Send sample
foot, with testimonials, previous experience, and
guarantee to The Bulletin office.
Hard upon the engagement of H. H.
Champion comes news from England of
the marriage of George Bernard Shaw to Miss
Payne Townshend. After 20 years’ steady de-
nunciation of marriage, Shaw’s fall is a good score
for Cupid. But, oh ! how those anti-marriage
sentiments of Shaw’s crowd upon the memory !
For instance:
What is any respectable girl brought up to do but to
catch some rich man’s fancy and get the benefit of his
money by marrying him—as if a marriage-ceremony could
make any difference in the right or wrong of the thing !
Again: ;
The only way for a woman to provide for herself de-
cently is for her to be good to some man that can afford
to be good to her.—Mrs. Warren’s Profession.
Shaw calls that revered, respectable institution,
the Family, “ this horrible domestic institution,”
and declares that he has “no respect for popular
morality.” Ah, there was a Bernard Shaw !

Godoldok the Guardian.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hennon—he was a Yankee in the Soura-
baya arsenal—looked foolish as he answered me.
“ Well, I ought to’ve known, mister —that’s a
fact ! But I reckon I’m so much mixed up with
iron and sich, that I don’t take no stock in stone
fixin’s. Folks here calls it ‘ Godoldok,’ and out-
side that I know nothin’, ’cept that it’s ugly
enough to scare scales off an alligator ! ”
“ Godoldok ! ” The sound of the name—half-
portentous, half-laughable—suited well enough
this monstrous, cross-legged figure, with hideous
red and yellow face, and huge eyes staring, with a
cruel calm, away across the tree-tops to the
darkening Java Sea. The sinking sun touched
off with crimson edging the crude, ungraceful out-
lines, and the little grey moro-doves fluttered
gently about the insolent grotesqueness of the
head, but there was no mistaking the serene
brutality—the placid, passionless malevolence —
that marks the work of ancient Hindu hands.
“ Or Hindu-taught, at all events,” I said to
Hennon. “ Brought here, I should say, from
some of the Brahminical ruins in the interior.
But, why ? There’s a story hanging to this ; and,
if I don’t fossick it out, I’m a -—”
“ Don’t say ‘ Dutchman ’! ” Hennon chipped in.
“ It’s a phrase that don’t exactly suit the location.
And, as for the story—darn me if I can make out
how you Britishers came by a reputation for mind-
ing your own business 1 ”
“ But this is my business, friend Hennon, And,
to prove it, I’ll stick you into the story, some-
how ! ”
Which threat is here fulfilled.
• * * *
A fat, good-humored-looking old Chinaman,
with a wisp of scarlet at the end of his scanty
grey pigtail, and his blue silk baju thrown back
from his capacious yellow stomach. He took his
long bamboo-stemmed pipe from his mouth, and
twinkled at me through his silver-rimmed glasses,
but not till I had saluted him did he get up from
his stone bench to make me an elaborate bow.
“You know the Straits-Malay ? ” he asked, in
that tongue, as he waved a hand towards the seat.
“ And you know, also, the regulations ? ”
“ About salaaming to the white man ? Oh, yes \
But I don’t want any man to salute me against
his will; and, in any case, I’m not a Dutchman.”
“My son, I know it. To a Dutchman would
I have given the forced reverence his foolish law
exacts ; to you, as a stranger, I show willing
respect.”
“ But the Dutchmen say that you Chinese like
them better than the Javanese do ? ”
“Sampaada!” the old man called, and from
behind the gorgeously-flowered hanging of the
doorway came a slim, top-knotted Javani boy.
“ Cigars to the gentleman, and fire. Like them ! ”
he went on, as the lad came back with a cigar-box
and a lighted joss-stick. “We like them about
as much as Chassan here likes this,” and he coolly
thrust his glowing pipe-bowl against the. boy’s
saffron cheek. “And we haven’t, like him” —
as the unlucky urchin skipped and howled —“ the
relief of making a noise about it.”
Here was a heathen worth talking to. I lighted
a long Trajarta, and essayed a farther peep into
the dim, unsuspected recesses of the mysterious
Cathayan mind.
“ The Dutch,” I said, “ call themselves the
justest nation in the world.”
Quang-Lo sunk me at the first shot. So do
the English,” he answered, quickly. “ And, like
the Dutch, they stand alone in that opinion.’
Which is so far true that the average Briton—
getting outside his own four seas has nothing
short of moral earthquake to suffer at the dis-
covery that “ British fair-play ” is no more, to the
rest of the world, than a pleasing figment of the
British imagination.
“You don’t make us eat dirt, as these Dutch-
men do, you would say. No !—but you would if
it suited you. Every nation ” —Quang-Lo waved
his pipe round his shaven crown—“ does exactly
what agrees, not with its conscience, but with its
pocket.”
Here — ex oribus infidelium, and unpalatable,
even to me —was a truth so indisputable that I
could only feebly say, “Your Straits countrymen,
at all events, don’t appear dissatisfied with British
rule.” _ . ...
“ Why should they be, my son ? It is with acts
we have to do—not motives ; and as we Chinese
are perhaps the most constitutionally cruel race
upon earth ” —here this delightfully-candid pagan
lowered his voice—“we will show these Dutch-
men, when we some day get the better of them,
that we didn’t require any Godoldok as a re-
minder of what we owe them ! ”
“ Godoldok ! ” I repeated eagerly. “ I’ve seen
him ! What does he mean ? How did he come
there? Nobody seems to know —though I have
asked scores of people.” .
Quang-Lo arose, beckoned me within his por-
tiere, and lit a lamp that filled with rosy glow his
quaint little den—glittering with lacquer-work
and porcelain, and fragrant with sandalwood.
“Did you ask any Chinaman? ” he said, as he
put bottles on the table—with mangoes and tama-
rinds, and burly little jars of unspeakable Chinese
preserves. “ No ! Well, we remember—let. who
will forget! Choose your drink, and help your-
self.” „ , 7
He poured himself a liberal jorum of kornkum-
mel —filled his long pipe—and kept me till mid-
night listening to matters which left the Spanish
Inquisition little to boast of. And of these was
the story of Godoldok.
There is no dignity so stupendous as a short,
fat man’s ; and the Governor-General —five-feet-
two in height, and little less round the middle—
was, in his own opinion, tremendce rnajestatis ;
which self-estimate—in the days when Sourabaya
was the seat of central government —he had abun-
dant opportunities of translating into action.
“ Fellow!” he said, “do you understand? It
is the Government of His Netherlandic Majesty
which does you the honor to offer to buy your
land! And do you dare to ref use ? ”
“ Excellency ! ” the venerable Chinaman an-
swered from his knees, “ it is indeed, as your Ex-
cellency says, a great honor. But money is of no
consequence to me in comparison with the place
in which I have dwelt so many years, and which
—being now very old—l hope soon to leave to my
children.”
“ Excellency’s ” red face grew purple, and for
half-a-minute he stumped to and fro before the
profundity of his astonishment would let him
even swear.
“ Hear this funny fellow! ” he said ; then,
“ Hear this facetious dog, who pretends to put
his misbegotten yellow spawn in the scale against
the express wish of—of Me—gentlemen— Me !
—and he laughed witli such stertorous ferocity
that his medical attendant ventured to suggest:
“If your Excellency would be calmer, lne
“ Away, ass ! —away ! And you, Chinaman
once more I—you will not take the price we otter
you ? ” „
“No price, Excellency, would - . • '
“Get up, dog !—get up, and come hither.
The old man—shaky, but unshakable—came
over to the table, and the Governor, standing
opposite, took from his pocket a cobanp.
“ Bear witness, gentlemen all! r lhe Government
of the Netherlands—through me, its unworthy
servant—has offered this man 60,000 guilders tor
his land, whereon to build a new residency. He
refuses - and the government —again through me
pays over to him this ” —he slapped the two-cent.
piece upon the table—“ and takes the land !
The Chinaman—thrust out of doors—went away,
unruffled ; he thought the two-cent business was
some weird, unfathomable kind of joke. But,
that night, came a party of soldiers and explained
to him with their musket-butts that there was no
joke at all in the matter, and that he and his had
forthwith to depart from off “Government pro-
perty.”
* * * *
“ What insolent hound is that ?” The Governor
jumped up in his carriage and pointed with his
sheathed sword to a figure that stood up, solitary
and conspicuous, from the line of natives cringing
in the dust till the great man should have passed.
“ Bring the fellow hither ! ”
With bayonets in his rear, the recalcitrant was
marched to the carriage—a Chinaman, old and
feeble, with dull despair in his eye. And the
Governor —knowing him well—spoke as to a
stranger.
“ Who are you, fellow, and whence come you,
not to know who I am, and what your duty is
when I pass by ? ’* . •
And the old man, looking him steadily in the
eye, answered quietly, “Too well I know thee
tyrant ! scoundrel ! Robber of my children !
(3urse of Sourabaya, and accursed, thyself, of
every honest man in it.”
“Ha!” said the Governor. “Ho! hum! Is
that all ? ” and, to the paralysing astonishment of
his staff, he coolly told his coachman to drive on.
* * * •
But that night, upon the open space between
the Presidency and Tagassan village, the moon
looked down at a select but miscellaneous
party. The Governor, two of his officials, a ser-
geant’s guard of soldiers,' a kneeling Chinaman,
a brawny little Javanese standing over him with
a heavy parang.
The Governor raised his hand —the Javani
swung his weapon ; the Governor’s hand went
down—the Chinaman’s head left his shoulders at a
stroke.
“Government’s receipt,” said the great man,
with a chuckle. “ And, .1 say, Krantz—wouldn’t
it be a good idea to bury him here ? ”
The subordinate—dutifully convulsed at the
merry conceit aforesaid —thought it would hardly
do. ‘ ‘ The other yellow animals, Excellency,
would probably dig him up again, to send his
bones to China.”
“ Would they so ? Get spades, and let the
grave be dug! ” and the Governor, lighting a
cigar, strolled to and fro till the work was nearly
finished.
“I have it, Krantz !” he said then. I know
how to keep this carrion down tdl doomsday, and
spite these Javanese curs, as well. What do they
call that big stone image they -think -such a lot ot
—the thing that’s to bring such ill-luck to boura-
baya, if it’s ever moved - • .
“ Godoldok, your Excellency moans, ancl the
secretary lowered his voice as he glanced towards
the Javanese executioner. “ I fea,r,.it would niake
trouble; the people hold it very sacred. And,
besides, the figure is enormously heavy.”
“Sacred? Tuyfel, sir!—don’t the sneaking
dogs call themselves Mahometans? They’ll have
their own priests about their ears if they make
any fuss about a Hindu statue ! And, as ft>r the
weight, sir”—the Governor put his bristles up—
“the thing shall be done, sir, if it takes every
man and every bullock in bourabaya. to do it!
And you, sir-r-as you’re so good at raising objec-
tions—you’ll take charge of the matter. And
you’ll see tha,t the job is done within the week,
too—if you’re wise ! ” '
It was done ! Amidst the smothered curses ot
a people whose Hinduism projects everywhere
through a thin veneer of Islam, Godoldok was
laboriously transported to where, above the China-
man’s bones, be has sat till the Dutchmen them-
selves have forgotten most of the story.
But, for the Javani and the Chinaman, so many
cruel memories cluster round that grim, colossal
figure that, to the Dutch in Java, “Godoldok!
may one day be the watchword of destruction.

The Cripple.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sitting beside the city gate,
Calmar the Cripple cursed his fate.
“ Parsons,” he snarled, “ say ‘ kiss the> rod ’!
What do I owe to the grace of God ?
What has their good God done for me ?
Made me a scorn and a mockery.
Eh, but they talk so fair and fine !
Would they had twisted limbs like mine !
All that I see can ride or run—
I am a dead man under the sun.”
Striding in haste the Cripple by,
Went a patrician, proud of eye j
And to that human heap absurd
Threw forth a coin, with a kindly word.
“Damn him ! Damn him ! Early and late,”
Calmar the Cripple cursed in hate.
“ He was born high ; I was born low—
That is the reason I hate him so.
He has the best to drink and eat;
I am an outcast on the street.
He goes apparelled like a king j
Rags are my richest covering.
Fortune to him has given in dower
Beauty and Birth and Wealth and Power.
Me she has cramped in a crippled frame,
Born to a heritage of shame.
He, with his sunny and gallant air,
Idol of women is everywhere.
Me they regard with pitying scorn,
Wondering why I was ever born;
And, as they pass me by in pride,
Sweep their delicate skirts aside,
Knowing not that their passing feet
Thrill me and fill me with rapture sweet.
Knowing not that their subtle, fine
Perfume maddens my brain like wine.
He he can talk to them, make them smile' j
None of them guess he’s a fool the white 1
Little he knows as he laughs with glee-
How he is understood by me ;
Body and soul of him understood—
Scabbard of velvet, sword of wood.
Ah, if I only had his chance,
How would my life gleam with Romance
Just then a Presence passing by-
R-ard the Cripple’s sorrowful cry ;
Touched him and said : “ AJas, .for thee !
Be thou the man that thou wouldst bel ”
Swiftly the Cripple rose straight'and (tall,
Flinging his crutches against the wall,
Striding away in a gallant style :
Fate at his elbow laughed the while.
Two nights after a passing crew
Out of the river a body drew.
* This is a case,” said,the coroner,
‘’Common enough—he drowned his care !
“Give me your verdict quickly then.”
“ Tired of life,” said the jurymen.
CREEVE ROE.
The Window.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Men swore to themselves when they thought of
Mrs. Hay—that is to say, many men did. She
was frequently described as a devil; but the term
is so vague it might have been a compliment.
Mrs. Hay was pretty and audacious; when the
man was willing and fairly presentable, she would
go far, but had a horror of extremes. The women
said she went too far, the men thought not far
enough, and the men knew her best. She led
men on to nowhere, and dropped them there, and
one or two of them never returned.
But Bakewell had won a promise. Perhaps he
was more captivating or more determined than
other men, or perhaps it was her mood. Certainly
he danced wonderfully well. The last waltz was
still in her veins, and the music enveloped her
like a warm atmosphere. Then the night was so
fair, and the garden so full of langour. Given a
man, and a woman, and a garden, and the serpent
is quite superfluous nowadays. At any rate, Mrs.
Hay presently found herself clasped tight in
Fred’s arms, feeling almost helpless, but bargain-
ing excitedly. It was then Bakewell won the
solemn promise, and sealed it.
“I know the window,” he whispered, “ I can
see it from my own house. I have watched it
night after night. Do not fail me, or—or ”
He kissed her.
* * * *
The house was surrounded by a large garden ;
the window had a screen of shrubbery. Bake-
well stole to it in the darkness of the early hours,
and tapped twice upon the pane. Then he raised
the window ever so gently and clambered quietly
into the room. A head was dimly visible on the
white pillow over against the wall. Fred called
her name in a whisper. No answer. He called
again and smiled. “ Shy,” he murmured, and it
pleased him to think so. He crept to the door.
There was no key, so he secured it by jamming a
chair under the look. Then he stole back to the
bed-side. He wore a long overcoat reaching
almost to his ankles.
* * * *
In the passage Bedford was battering at the
door; his daughter, Mrs. Hay, stood near in the
darkness, just as she had sprung from her bed.
From within the room came terrifying shouts of
“fire” and “thieves.” Outside there were
hurried footsteps and the muffled curses of a man
in hasty retreat. At last there was a crash, and
the door gave way. Upon the floor of the bed-
room, rolled into a ball, with all the sheets and
blankets about him, kicked Mrs. Hay’s grand-
father, a very stout, strong, deaf old gentleman ;
and as he wallowed he screamed of fire and
thieves, and burglars, and declared that he was
murdered in his bed by twelve unscrupulous bush-
rangers.
Redford’s match disclosed the lamentable con-
dition of the venerable gentleman, but already
Mrs. Hay bad contrived to secrete the black over-
coat she had discovered on the carpet by the bed-
side.
The window was closed, and Redford could find
no traces of an intruder. Had it not been for the
broken chair he might have convinced his father
that he had really become subject to nightmares
in his old age.
* * • *
Next evening a carrier brought Fred Bakewell
a parcel containing a long overcoat and a short
note. The note was anonymous, and ran :
You fool! my window was bolted.”
B.D.
Man Proposes, but.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the story of a girl who went fishing and of
what she caught. The fish she wished to land was
a rather nice boy with a good position and
friends; and the girl in her wisdom thought he
would be the better of a wife. But the man was
not in the mood to consider seriously the beauties
of woman ; he had been on the hook before when
it was a pleasure to be thus tortured, and having
been duly played-with and fooled, was tossed
back again somewhat the worse for the operation.
Which gave him a desire to play a woman on his
hook, should the chance come.
_ But the girl knew not of all this; so she de-
livered herself into the hands of the enemy. And
the end of the game came thus : They had gone the
usual way of a man and a maid, that is, by dances
and moonlight walks and little presents and glove
wagers, but so far the man had said nothing
definite, and the girl, thinking to bring matters to
a head, went in for a furious flirtation with another
man. Just then the young man went off for his
annual holiday, and on his return learned certain
things. Then, being a youth of understanding,
he perceived that the time had arrived. So he
called at the house of the girl, and, after friendly
greetings, he talked pleasantly awhile. Then
rising, as if to take his leave, he remarked, “By
the way, Miss Cliffe, I didn’t tell you of the piece
of luck I had since I’ve been away. I drew a
horse in one of Tatt.’s sweep i, and he ran third.
Of course it isn t a fortune, but it means seven or
eight hundred clear.”
“ I’m so pleased to hear it. That was a slice of
luck.”
Yes, and in addition to that, the governor’s
raised my screw. I’m getting ,£3OO a year now.”
Then the girl, thinking the fish was safely
hooked and the line taut, began to haul in on
the line.
“Really, Mr. Waring, how nice ! And I sup-
pose you’re thinking of getting married now ? ”
“Well, I suppose I could marry now. The
sweep-money would nearly buy and furnish a
decent house, and the salary is enough to get
married on. Don’t you think so?”
“ Really, I have never given the matter a
thought.
“ Damme,”thought the man, “she might lie a
little more scientifically.”
“ But then-—there’s the lady ! I know so
many girls, just a little ; but few well enough for
that.”
The girl waited.
Then the man rose and took the girl’s hand in
his, and placed the other on his shoulder.
“ Miss Cliffe—Dorothy—you have heard what I
just said—about my prospects and all that—and
really, I’m not a bad sort of a fellow, you know ;
and I do think I’d make a good sort of' a husband’
and be kind to a girl ” Here he halted for a
moment, and the girl looked up into his face and
put up her mouth to be kissed ; and the man was
just about to do it when he thought of that other
one. His face hardened; he dropped the girl’s
hand, and looked round at the clock.
“By Jove,” he burst out, “nearly eleven. I
ought to be home by this.” Then he grabbed his
hat. “ Good night, Miss Cliffe ; by-the-way, to-
day’s the first of April; you won’t forget, will
you ? Good night.”
The girl did not forget!
F. CROSON.
H. : “ Speculators amuse themselves as to
the chance of Cardinal Gibbons, or some other Non -
‘ Italian, becoming Pope. But the Pope must be
an Italian, by a custom over 300 years old, adopted
to obviate national jealousies, which are surely
now stronger than ever, wherefore it is merely
tickling the ignorant to write about a foreigner
getting the Pontificate.
“ Constance Clyde ” to The Bulletin :
Maoriland, with its advanced socialistic legislation,
possesses the poorest attempts at radical literature. The
workman is so well represented in Parliament that he
doesn’t need representation on the press. Consequently,
the M.L. journalist of socialistic tendencies will begin to
earn the half of an honest living only on the return to
power of that party which didn’t represent the country
some nine or ten years ago. Meanwhile, he is adding
“May the kingdom of Captain Russell come” to the
general journalistic prayer, “ Give me this day my daily
idea,” without, so far, much sign of success. As for the
M.L. penwoman, fortunately for herself, she hardly existsas
yet. Where she does she usually earns her living by doing
something else. Maoriland would seem to sustain the
theory that in proportion as a country progresses in “ reli-
gion ’’and so-called morality, it retrogrades in literature.
Themost churchy and respectable coun try in Australasia, it
isthernost “lost” from a literary standpoint. In Dunedin,
which is the holy of holies, a poet is the man who goes
round the offices asking you to buy his rhymed version of
Job. You buy it to help on a “ cause,” which is never, by
any possibility, the cause of literature. What literary
encouragement there is takes the form of prize com-
petitions, and that poetry doesn’t rank high here is
shown by the fact that even this dubious form of encour-
agement has never been extended towards the virse-
maker except on the especial occasion of the provincial
jubilee, and then the poet had to forward with his effusion,
a certified statement that he had consumed so much tea
and sugar at a certain store ! Pegasus harnessed to the
grocer’s cart! Theaverage M.L. brain-power is, probably,
greater than in most parts of Australia, but it is early
shunted off on religious or commercial rails.
